THE RED PAGE.
RE-ECHOES
“WITH DEATH'S PROPHETIG" BEER.
Lay the bottle there beside rre : put the glass upon
the chair:
You’d better fill it up before you go:
For my hand’s a bit unsteady, and I wouldn’t
like to swear—
And if I spilt a drop I should, I know.
Ah ! many’s the good bottle I have buried to the
world
In obedience to an honest drinker’s code :
Now I’ve come to the last bottle that shall ever
wet my throttle,
And the dead marines are calling down the road.
Well, I’ve done my share of labour, and I’ve had
my share of fun,
And I’ve kissed my share of women in my time ;
And it warms me to. remember —now that I must
out and run—
The glorious sins committed in my prime.
For I followed all my instincts, and they never
led me wrong—
It’s refraining makes the thought of Death a
load :
O, the days of love and laughter—and the nights
that followed after!
And the dead girls are singing down the road.
J*m nearly day; but still...l’m happier when
wetter...
Remember that I honour you, my wife;
For a good girl is good, and a good mate is better,
But a good wife ’s the best thing in life...
I *m thinking of old times, my dear, before I mar-
ried you—
To you and them my last drink is owed :
“ Good-bye ! Here’s luck ! ” I pass ; and now
we’ll break the glass,
For the dead mates are beckoning down the road.
THE BANDY-COOT
[Our Nature Writer explains that this coot does
not come in his proper place in the advertised
series. This is due. partly to the fact that he is a
oasual and unexpected kind of coot, and partly
to our Nature Writer’s belief that he himself
was bom out of series, coming second where he
should have been first, and thus failing, according
to the law of primogeniture, to succeed to the
title and estates of his father, who was an English
noble of the highest degree, with the consequence
that he has to turn out early in the morning to
hunt coots for business instead of partridges for
pleasure, though this is nevertheless a satisfaction
to him, as, indeed, it should be to every person who
has come close to the Great Heart of Nature, which
is full of love and tenderness for the humblest
animal that crawls, and should be worshipped by
everyone who can afford it and sell bis impres-
sions to the newspapers like this.]
THIS BANDY-COOT
I WATCH him, artless and pure. He
browseth on the emerald grass and on
the kangaroo grass, knee-deep on the
sandstone tors. I hear him sing his
light soprano aria when he is cut out of a log
to feed my cattle-pup. I observe the moon
glint on his delicate coat of grey, shot
with silver, and he streaketh hastily
for safety when I sool the pup’s
mother after him. His eyes are brown
lakes, eloquent of his snout is
Eointed like a pig’s. Gaily he cometh to feed
l the grey evening on my swede-turnips,
and crieth tenderly to his mate as he
cometh, in a voice like a squeezed india-
rubber doll. His moans are.music ; his legs
are poetry in motion ; and his claws scratch
worse than a buckrabbit’s. Loving and
courageous is he; his flesh is fairly sweet
when not too old. His nest he buildeth
under low bushes, thatched snugly from the
storm, and every night he burroweth his
way into my corn-shed.
Lightly may shine the moon, gleaming
thro’ the forest; coldly the stars may burn,
set imperishable in the icy vault of blue;
but every evening the Bandy-coot will be
found awake and attending to business. The
weird noises of the Night surround us—the
throbbing of Great Nature’s heart pulsing
more deeply in the darkness ; listen closely,
and you may hear Bandy munching native
carrots, his favourite luxury. Birds of prey
flit to and fro ; noiselessly their wings and
beaks move, seeking their food. Low lieth
the Bandy-coot close to a log; he does n’t
like being snapped up for a mopoke’s
holiday. The Southern Cross, seen on
the horizon, riseth hour after hour,
till it dippef 7 toward the South, heralding
midnight: .-.all the Bandy-coot muncheth on.
Ever as trie hours pass questeth he for fresh
native carrots ; making his wife bring him
the juiciest she can find, whilst he huggeth
the sheltering log or bush, carefully letting
her take the chances.
Fair stands the cornshed by the river flat ;
full to o’er-brimining with the golden grain.
Listen close ! ye may hear the Bandy-coot
playing the devil among the corn-cobs ; or
squeaking with fright- as he heareth the
approaching carpet-snake. In the cocky’s
garden we shall find him where the spuds
grow, nibbling the tubers .-.rid leaving Ihe
stalk. So fards the Bandy—fairest of coots.
Loudly at eve the wind may roar, fiercely
tire rain may fall ; little careth the male
Bandy-coot; he rouseth his wife into the
rain to dig food, while he stayeth at home
and keepeth the nest warm for her return.
Such is true affection.
The age of the Bandy-coot man knoweth
not : some are tough enough to be a hun-
dred ; no amount of cooking will soften them.
His children he lovetli dearer than his wife :
usually eating them when about half-grown ;
in times of scarcity all his relations have to
go. In the day of Creation, Nature left a
wide gap between her masterpieces—the
wombat and porcupine. Into that gap, with
noble self-effacement, steppeth the Bandy-
coot.
[?]FALDER.
AFTERMATH
Tramp ,*
Spring ? Is it Spring at last,
Here in this Austral land ?
Can it be that the Winter has passed ?
For up on the hill where leaves lie dead
The smile of the fresh young grass is spread ;
The wattles shimmer like frozen gold ;
And down in the valley in veils of snow
Like waiting brides the fruit-trees show;.
And the song of the swallow that just now rang
Is the song that last Spring’s swallow sang.
And now that the Spring has come,
I who am growing old,
I will give voice and sing !
It is Spring, it is Spring... But my voice is dumb,
And my heart that once rang can no more ring
Than, the song of the swallow that died last Spring.
O, the Austral Spring is coming, I can hear the
wild bees humming
And *the bell-bird in the ranges singing high,
And the blue wrens in the bramble by the creek
where oft I ramble
In the fragrant air beneath a sunny sky.
And tile wattle trees are stirring with the feathered
songsters whirring
As they seek the sparkling nectar from the bloom
That bedecks, the near and far land with a golden
tinted garland,
Right away to where the lofty mountains loom.
And above the fields of clover with the warm sun
shining over,
Mounting upward, ever upward, on the wing,
Soar the skylarks in their glory as they tell their.
Springtide story
In the stirring, thrilling chorus that they sing...
0, the Austral. Spring has found us, and 'fair
Nature smiles around us
In a brightly-spangled garb of green and gold ;
She has shed her old grey jacket that stood all the
winter racket,
And was woven by the fingers of the cold.
D.B.
Go to Australia ! when the Spring is near,
And listen to the magpie’s morning song
Swelling amid the ranges shrill and clear—
Eohoing through the stillness loud and long.
Wander along the river flats, where grows
The golden*wattle—loveliest of blooms:
All in a perfect yellow blaze, that glows .
Like cloth of gold from ancient Tyre’s looms.
In noontide’s heat go seek the shady banks
Of fern-fringed creeks, which gurgle on their way:
Where merry little waves, in laughing ranks,
Murmur like bees throughout the peaceful day.
When evening’s hush has settled on the land,
Go meet the milkmaid by the stockyard gate;
There you may speak her fair and hold tier hand—
What more can any man desire of Fate ?
MUNDALA.
Dear girls, the. Spring is come again
With almond-bloom and sprout of crops,
With violets that deck the lane
And bloom profuse in-drapers’ shops.
Come out ! come out! the wattle spoils
To shame your hair, tho’ dyed to-day ;
Put on your silks, delaines,, and voiles—
Where are the furs of yesterday ?
Skip out, with glint of lingerie ;
Unheeded let 1 Spring poets bleat
Of Austral land arid spangled sea :
Our Spring trips in with satin’d feet.
Nor empty-handed comes the maid,
But brings a yearly gift for each :
For Thirty brings she. Artful Aid,
For Seventeen the Bloom of Peach.
ANDYS.
As when across the blue eyes of a child,
Ruffles and darkens in that azure deep
A sudden blur of’tears—and wakes from sleep,
But leaves the tender features undefiled
From any lasting mark : so, ealm and mild,
Turns Winter into Spring—the wattles weep
Soft tears awhile, then from their branches peep
The golden tassels—close together piled—
As though the tree were plaflted on a reef
Of living gold, which, mounting thro’ its veins,
Bursts forth to show what wealth its roots immure,
And every swelling bud and dancing leaf
Sways to the music of remembered rains—
Winter’s finale and Spring’s overture 1
M.E.K.
Languor of waking holds the rested land,
And through grey scrub and o’er the bare red
plain
One steals with greening tread and magic hand
To spread Spring’s fleeting garment once again.
Beneath the ground Life’s vibrant pulse grown
strong,
Thrilling the seed and rootlet with its might,
Issues in springing grass and leafy song
And bursts in calyxed beauty on the sight.
Blow winds to which glad golden wattles lend
A sweetness that o’er leagues of plain is borne,
Where grass and wild geranium bend and bend
Till star-flowers Sliqiv-Has poppies thro’ the corn.
Oh, Spring-kissed Bush with blossom-beckoning
hands, °
Whose smile once caught, no other smile e’er
tames !
Dearest I love her when as now she stands—
The radiant Bride that tragic summer claims.
GASK.
WOMWN AND EDUATION
[The writer of an article reprinted Jul >’ 2]
from The Popular Science Monthly) contended that the
higher education of woman is surely extinguishing' par
race, both directly by its effects on her organisation, ancl,
indirectly, by rendering early marriage impossible for the
average man.” Contributors’ comments follow : no space
for more :—]
ONE WOMAN'S VIEW.
ri’lHE writer’s ideal seems to be marriage at
I eighteen and a family of nine or ten.
M Excellent, doubtless, for the nation ; but
for the individual woman ? Every woman
knows that, leaving out the question of actual
violent pain, each baby means to its mother at
least six months of extreme discomfort. lhere-
fore a family of ten means for the mother five
years during which she cannot enjoy life. How
many men would be willing to give five years
ill-health for the good of their country, and no
other reward ? Why should women be expected
to, and blamed as pleasure-loving and cowardly if
they object?
I am no advocate of the higher education, and
dislike equally the over-educated female who con-
siders babies a bore, and the woman of fashion
who regards them as toys. My ideal career for a
woman is that of wife and mother ; but if she has
a large family it should be recorded to her credit
and not looked on as mere duty to the State. •
A middle-class girl marries—not, let us hope, at
eighteen, when she is unformed mentally and
physically; but at three or four or four-and-
twenty. For the first few months everything is
delightful; an adoring and adored husband, “a
house of her own,” new dresses, and the joy of
being Mrs. Jones. Then comes the mew life. If
she is a true woman, there is happiness of the
highest kind in the .thought of his child and
hers that is to be. Still, the weary round
of days must be gone through. It is a
“detail,” but much of life depends on details.
As the weeks drag on, she becomes more and more
weary ; a sound night’s sleep is impossible ; arid
she dreads, and yet longs, for the coming ordeal.
At last it is over, and worn out and yet happy,
she holds her child in her arms.
Life begins for her again in the old routine; but
the new little life takes much of her time and
energy. Servants and climate are two things
often forgotten in the demai. t for large
families from middle-class Queensland women.
Our long summers are exhausting in themselves;
and with the strain of little children and incom-
petent servants women soon become faded aiid
sallow. In well-to-do English households children
almost from their birth fair under the sway of
“nurse,” but in most; Queensland homes “mother”
has to take her place. “Mother” looks older
than her thirty years; and she would give any-
thing for a rest away from the children, but with
small means that is out of the question. A week’s
holiday at the seaside means harder Work for her.
So. the years'go on, and she sinks into a domestic
drudge. Money does not come with the increasing
family, and butchers’ and bakers’ bills are a per-
petual worry. Before her marriage she was thought
to have a pretty taste in music, but hours are too
precious now to be wasted in practising. Her
only interests now are the children and domestics
—her own and her neighbours’; for she is “ too
tired to read.”
At forty the ten children have arrived, and she
has lost all the brightness and prettiness she once
possessed. She is always tired, and the family
doctor speaks of the necessity of a serious opera-
tion. Still, she has “ done her duty to the State.”
If, on the other hand, she had had four or five
children, with reasonable intervals between them,
she would have kept strong, and have beep able
to look after them’ better. One of them would not
have died of scarlet fever and another of croup.
She would have been a companion to her husband
—which, with ten children and a small income,
was an impossibility.
No, if large families are to be desired for the
good of the country, let us encourage the mothers
with something besides the joy of patriotism.
Instead of subsidising useless politicians, give a
bonus to every mother on the arrival of a fourth
child, and continue and increase it with each new-
comer. Under present circumstances, every
moderately well-to-do woman is asked to sacrifice
herself for the good of the country—and how
much more do the poor ones suffer ?
C.M.
A MAN'S IDEA.
JUDGING from Australian experience, I
should say that the Writer mistakes cause
for effect. Niiae out of every ten girls of
this country who follow-up “the higher
education” are deficient in normal sex-instinct in
the first instance ; and even did they remain in a
state of primeval ignorance would probably be
inferior mothers.
Education possibly aggravates the trouble, for
women who aim at self-improvement refuse
to take the common-sense view of the sub-
ject that men students do. They exalt edu-
cation to a religion, and become as fanatical
about it as any dancing dervish who ever
gyrated his way to salvation. The male student,
if he is exceptionally diligent, spends perhaps
eight hours daily on his work. The rest of his
waking hours are spent in athletics, diversions,
and the companionship of damsels not addicted
to “the higher education.” The female student
has no thought outside her books, and memorises
by laborious study what the male grasps by under-
standing. The studious woman is, in nearly
every instance, a member of a third sex. She
lacks the natural delight in the company of
a man which is taken by even the most cir
cumspect of her sisters not devoted to the higher
education, while as for the little danee-and-
p’cnic flirtations— the conservatory embraces and
tfie kisses at the front gate—that tend to make
life worth living for the majority of the sex, she
regards them with absolute abhorrence.
I am not speaking about the woman of high
intellect, who is a totally different being. As far
as my experience of her goes, she possesses
Erimitive passions above the average; and, when
e sees her coming, the man of moral sentiments
should “get him to a monkery.”
A.B.
COPYRIGHT
m i-IE point is this. In the matter of copy-
I right, Canada and Australia are constitu-
M tionallv and practically in the same pair of
shoes. Constitutionally, because each is
a federation of States under the British Crown ;
and to each, in its Constitution Act, the British
Parliament has ceded express power to legis-
late regarding copyright. Practically, because
Canadian readers, and writers, and artists, and
musicians, and playwrights, and printers, etc.,
have necessarily motives and interests similar to
the motives and interests of Australian printers,
and playwrights, and musicians, and artists, and
writers, and readers, etc.
Therefore, the laws that have been made by
Canada can be made by Australia ; and it is likely
that the policy which has been found good by
Canada will be found good by Australia—since
Canada, working under similar conditions, has
had 33 years’ more federal experience.
J*
Copyright does not include trade-marks or
patents for inventions: these are a matter for
separate legislation. Copyright is the exclusive
right of multiplying copies of a literary or artistic
work (by printing, for example); or the exclusive
right of performing a play or a piece of music.
By law, copyright belongs to the author —to keep,
or sell, or otherwise dispose of during a certain
time. That is to say, when a man writes a book
or carves a statue, or so on, the law says that no
one shall reproduce it without his permission : he
has the sole right of making copies, the copyright.
British copyright laws have force in Australia ;
but some of the British laws are admitted to be
bad, and some were made a long time ago. And
they were made chiefly for Britain, not for Aus-
tralia. Bo the Constitution gives the Parliament
of the Commonwealth express power to make
copyright laws for Australia. That is why
Senator Keating has introduced the Copyright
Bill now being discussed by the Senate.
In many ways this Bill would be a good law ;
but in the main principle it would not.
When Senator Keating framed his Bill, he had
a choice of two ways, of doing it. One was to
follow the example of Canada, and make an Aus-
tralian law for the benefit of Australians, ju ; as
Canada has made Canadian laws for the benefit of
Canadians. The other way was to adopt all the
old or bad laws of Britain, made chiefly for the
benefit of Britain, and try and tinker them up to
date. Unfortunately it is the last way that
Senator Keating has chosen.
&
Canada says to Britain: By our .Constitution
you admit that copyright is a matter that con-
cerns us, and you empower us to legislate
regarding it. Well, these are the things we
want. We want to create art and literature and
music in Canada, and to encourage artists and
authors and musicians by protecting them in the
enjoyment of the profits of their work!Therefore
we give copyright. At the same time we want to
secure for Canadians the best things quickest—
the best things in literary and dramatic and
musical and artistic work, as soon as they
can be had elsewhere. Therefore, we give copyright
to all the world on the same conditions as Cana-
dians comply with —to all the world that gives
our authors equivalent copyright. We also want to
promote Canadian industry—therefore we insist
that, to secure Canadian copyright, works pro-
duced in other countries must be reproduced in
Canada within one month. If they are noi, we
reserve the right to get them the best way we can.
And these wants Canada has provided for.
Senator Keating’s Bill says: We too will pro-
tect local authors. But we will protect foreign
authors just as much or more—far more than
Canada protects them. They need not give any
return for this protection: they need not make
their books available to Australian readers, and
we will do without their books, yet prevent any-
body from reprinting or importing these. They
need not even reciprocate our generosity. And
we will not try to promote local industry: Aus-
tralian books may provide a living for British or
German workmen, but they need not provide a
living for Australian workmen ; while foreign
works will be protected wherever they are printed.
J*
So the otiier point is this. Canada does a deal
better for Canadians than Senator Keating pro-
poses to do for Australians. And Canada and Aus-
tralia have similar powers under the same Imperial
laws.

Sept. Scribner’s prints some curious letters of
Bancroft, the Amer. historian, describing student
life at Gottingen university about 1818. There is
a reference to “Professor Forster, the famous
voyager, who went once round the world with
Captain Cook, and was afterwards made professor
at Halle.”
He was a rude, ill-mannered fellow. A young Jewish
girl happened to he in company with him one evening.
The company was rather dull. She was guilty of yawn-
ing. Forster, perceiving her mouth distended, during
the operation cried out,, “Lord, Miss, I hope you don’t
mean to eat me.” “Never fear,” answered she, “I’m a
Jewess.”
Bancroft’s student habit was to rise before five,
and drink several cups of coffee before seven.
Then he ate a piece of brown bread and butter,
which lasted him till dinner. With the exception
of two, or perhaps three, hours, he was closely at
work till eleven at night.
Eichhorn told me yesterday that he labours at present
from five in the morning till nine at night, that he has
all his life gone on much in the same way ; that when he
was first made professor, he studied 15 hours daily, and
never experienced any inconvenience in respect of his
health.
Now we can explain the dullness of Bancroft’s
historical work—a quarry for less fatigued men to
build from. By the way, in Bancroft’s day, Wolf,
most erudite of professors, had in his ripe age
given up study, and was eating good dinners and
sporting with Amaryllis and others. Bancroft
records this fall (?) from grace with Puritan dis-
approval.
A.G.S.

Another Convincing; Proof.
THIS TIME AT MERRYLANDS.
The Case of Mr. H. A-M3ND.
(By a Local lloporter.)
Whilst much of the anguish and grief
which exist in some families as the re-
sult of ill-health is hid from the gaze
of the outside world, and is Unown
only to those who are immediately
concerned, sufficient transpires to ex-
cite the sympathy of every genuine
philanthropist, and to bring forth
strenuous efforts on behalf of suffering
humanity. Here is an instance in which
the reader will recognise a desire on
the part of Mr. Henry Almond, store-
keeper, Park-road, Merrylands, to ex-
tend a helping hand to any who may
be afflicted as he was some years back.
“It was when I was living in Tam-
worth," said Mr. Almond, “that I
I'rst knew what bad health was, and
that must be a good while back, for L
have been living here these fourteen
years past."
“in what way was your health af-
fected V' enquired a man from the
office.
“Weil, for two years previous to
coming here, and for a good while af-
ter starting this business, I was al-
most mad with neuralgia, and you
may guess that having a complaint
like that so long must have had a
direful effect upon my system general-
ly. Heally, sometimes I was fairly rag-
ing, owing to the agony 1 was in, and
many a time wished that I was dead
and out of the world altogether. Oh !
it was a life of fearful misery, and I
pity any poor soul in similar straits.
You may rest assured that I resorted
to pretty well every known treatment
in the hope of gaining some relief, but*
success evaded all my efforts. My
gums, my jaws, the cheek bones were
the parts where the torture principally
existed ; but, curiously enough, there
were days when one side of my face
and head was affected till nightfall,
and then the pains shifted over to the,
other side. And as for sleep—well, do
you think anybody could sleep under
such adverse circumstances V I know I
couldn’t, as a rule, though, at long
intervals, when nature could not hold
out any longer, I lapsed into a state
of unconsciousness that might be called
sleep, and whilst it lasted 1 had the
worst dreams you could think of."
“I don’t suppose you were usually
tit to do much work ?”
“Indeed I was not, and T. was also
so low-spirited that I did not seem to
care whether I was ever able to work
a<~/ain or not. As a, mat ter of fact f
had no interest in anything, excepting
that T wanted to get better if it woro
possible, which didn’t seem very like-
ly, for instead of getting better I got
worse as the months wore on. Shornv r
after the neuralgia attacked mo, 1 no-
ticed that my "digestion was getting
bad, and so it continued to fail until
eventually my internal organs were so
completely unset that even the lightest
of food could not be assimilated. , A.
heavy sensation in the chest, which
made it impossible to draw a deep
breath, was one of my daily. discom-
forts, and after eating a little my
stomach became inflated, and caused
considerable discomfort until the wind
was all belched away. My tongue had
a dirty coating, and the taste in mv
moutli of a morning was most dis-
agreeable-elearly indicating the bi-
lious state I was in ; and another
thing that troubled me was my 81
—very poor indeed sometimes, whiio
my eyes felt twice their proper size,
and as heavy as lead. Between mv
shoulders I had more pains to bear,
and I ached so much across the loins
that it was painful to walk even a
short distance. But you’ve no idea of
the fearful state of nervousness I got
into—used to give little jumps and
twitches, as though I had St. Vitus
dance sometimes, and the unsettled
frame of mind I was in gave rise to
much mental misery also. Simple mat-
ters irritated me, and Caused unduo
excitement, and 1 tell you candidly I
couldn’t find a moment’s peace or hap-
piness anyhow."
■'Alter so much ineffective treatment
how did you manage to get well
again
“This way : A new customer came to
the shop one day, and, seeing how ill
1 was, said ‘Did you ever try Clements
Tonic V I said : ‘Mo ; and i’m sick of
taking physics.’ Be came in again, and
asked if I had got any yet, to which
1 answered in the negative. Be said :
‘Well, try a bottle ; it won’t break
you/ and at last I did, for which I
am devoutly thankful. Oh ! it was like
something you read about in a novel,
the way Clements Tonic served me;
but it was a joyful reality neverthe-
less. To think that I had suffered for
years, and only then had got a re-
medy to do me good was something
to be thankful for, and 1 was, too.
Why, Clements Tonic began to sootho
my nerves when I had only taken two
or three bottles, and ypu couldn’t cre-
dit the amount of ease it had given me
already ; it roused the weakened ener-
gies of mv system and stimulated my
appetite to such a degree that beforo
long 1 was eating as well as ever —
when the neuralgia had entirely gone, of
course you cun guess how < rateful I was.
It is also in regard to indigestion that
Clements Tonic is deserving of recogni-
tion at my hands, for that was, after
all, one of the principal things from
which I suffered. Yes ; my last remedy
fairly opened tny eyes as to what me-
dicine really can do when it is of the
right kind/for in spite of everything
else failing, Clements Ton e set to
work, and put me right in no time. A
few weeks’ use of it made such an al-
tered man of me that 1 had nearly
forgotten wlmt ‘it was like to get up
feebng half-dead with weariness. My
breath was swret, and I had no un-
pleasant taste in my mouth of a
morning, uiy eyes were bright and the
sight good, 1 slept soundly each
night, every pain had gone, so what
more could any man wish for ?”
“And what has your health been
like since V”
“Splendid, excepting on an occasion
when 1 contracted influenza through
getting up all hours at night during
mv wife’s illness. She was suffering
from influenza, too, and soon there
was a pair of us. so we had to close
shop for a while. My word, we did
have a bad time! Wife got like a bag
of bones, but. Clements Tonic made her
eat four or five meals a day, and she
was soon well again, I was awfully
weak and miserable ; mv nerves broke
down, again, and once more I suffered
the tortures of neuralgia ; but not for
long though, as I knew what would
cure me. and so it did. Yes, Clements
Tonic showed its wonderful properties
again, and I’ve such belief in its cur-
ino- capacities that I recommend its
use to all. More than that,
publish mv story in manner that
your mind may suggest.
STATUTORY -DECLARATION.
T HENRY ALMOND, ol Park Road,
Merrylands, in tbe State of New South
Wales. Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have

tains my full .permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Merrylands this
twenty-third day of September, one thousand
nine hundred and four, before me,

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nostalgia.
0, dolesome days are Auckland’s now—
Dark, deluging, and chill;
Erom out the misty Manukau
The savage south winds shrill;
And, as across splenetic skies
The weeping clouds are borne,
I dree my weird, and realise
That man was made to mourn.
O, Waitemata’s sullen tide,
And hills bleak, bare, and brown !
O, greyly-shaded countryside
And wet and windy town !
Your winter is my discontent,
Your sombreness my gloom;
Your vapors circumambient
My shadowed soul entomb ;
Till brighter scenes my vision haunt,
As, hungry for the sun,
Far northward, on a rueful jaunt,
My recollections run,
Where tropic aureolas gloss
The stretch of shining sea.
That bounds the Isles of Helios,
And all they mean to me.
There, where eternal summer’s smiles
Make glad the glowing day,
Serenity unruffled whiles
World-weariness away.
There, in a fruitful garden-land,
Sweet Nature’s autograph
Is writ, for all who understand
That man was made to laugh.
Ah, “ Sorrow’s Crown of Sorrow ! ” Yet
The retrospect brings balm.
It is not all a vain regret,
For storm gives place to calm;
And, in the light of former days,
I, somehow, seem to see
A promise which my spirit stays—
What has been, yet may be.
M.L.
TALAFEKAU.
After All.
When the dust is in your eyes
And the grave damp in your hair,
It may matter very little
What you did or what you were.
You have wit to make men laugh,
You have woes that make men weep;
But that wit and woe will vanish
When at last you fall asleep.
You have gold enough for ten,
For yourself and nine beside;
At the death-hour God will ask you
Why those nine men starved and died.
There is profit sure in loss,
And in giving there is gain ;
And a man may kneel at altars
And be nine times worse than Cain.
He may walk with head held high,
But the murder-brands are red —
The nine scars his forehead on
Tell the world of nine men dead.
In the death-hour he may hear
Prayer of priest and child and friend,
But the pine men shall be waiting
In the lonely evening-end.
What will all earth’s gold be worth
All the prayers and psalms that rise
When he looks—forlorn and stricken —
In their strange accusing eyes ?
When the yellow mould is heaped
On your oaken coffin lid,
It may be that it will matter
What you were and what you did.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Inland.
At the turn of the track the path emerges
To the bare, bleak hill, from the dense, dark
trees,
You can hear the echoes of distant surges,
You can catch a glimpse of the open seas,
A patch of blue on the far horizon—
A touch of color beyond the gray.
It is good to pause and rest the eyes on
The clean, salt sea that is miles away.
It is good to recall the days of roaming—
The gleaming sands in the Eastern bights—
The sparkle of waves from the forefoot foaming,
And the welcome warnings of beacon lights.
The lofty spars and the taut shrouds wailing
Like harp-strings stirred by the steady Trades.
I have left the land and gone a-sailing
In a Ship of Dreams that is manned by Shades.
I can see the curve of the canvas swelling,
Like a captive cloud, ere they sheet it home.
I can feel the shock of the bows repelling
All the serried onslaughts of waves that comb.
I see the flashing of sprays that board her,
And hear the song of the tropic breeze,
The whispered word or the shouted order,
The crash and thunder of topping seas.
But the track winds round, and away from sea-
ward—
Away from the dreams that it brought by chance,
And our thoughts drift down with the wind to
leeward.
As we turn away from the old romance.
The last low notes of the breakers’ chorus
Die, like the days that are dead for me,
We set our eyes to the land before us —
We bid good-bye to the distant sea.
M.L.

THURSDAY. OCTOBER 5, 1905.
FROM “SPLENDID ISOLATION”-A
DESCENT.
The Anglo-Japanese treaty, which has just been
superseded by another and stronger bond, was the
first departure made by Great Britain from the
policy of “splendid isolation,” which had been
its vaunt for some decades. The British theory
of foreign politics up till then had been that the
Triple Alliance neutralised the power both of
Russia and France, and left Britain with an over-
whelming navy to be sought as a friend by all
parties. When Russia anti France formed the
Dual Alliance and there were indications—as at
the conclusion of the Jap-Chinese war—that
the Triple and Dual Alliances might, at
the prompting of danger or of profit, act
unitedly, the policy of “splendid isolation’’
didn’t look quite so attractive. “Splendid”
seemed in danger of being supplanted by
“ dangerous ” as the fitting adjective. It would
probably be found, if the veil were lifted from
foreign policy affairs, that at this time Britain
made overtures to the United States for a
defensive and offensive alliance, and found that
Uncle Sam was in a mood to play the splendid
isolation game himself, holding, as he tliought,
cards good enough for a lone hand. Without a
doubt, at that time reasonable overtures to any
European Power on the part of Britain would
have been welcomed, for Britain is still the greatest
country financially of the European group, and
possessed of supreme maritime strength. But the
undesirableness of ‘ ‘ entangling European alliances ”
had become, like Freetrade, one of the fetishes
of the British politician, and when an alliance was
decided upon it was with an Asiatic Power.
(It has not yet become the habit to apply the
adjective “entangling” to combinations with
niggers and heathens.) That agreement, though
only of a temporary and strictly limited
character, was felt to call for some apology.
“Great Britain grants, does not seek alliances,”
was the salve that one British statesman applied
to his country’s pride. Now the remorseless logic
of events forces Great Britain to seek a renewal of
that alliance, on terms no longer limited and tem-
porary, but of such character as the nation has
never consented to since Marlborough’s cam-
paigns against the great Louis.
• * * *
The new treaty will endure for 10 years and so
much longer as it is allowed to go without denun-
ciation after a year’s notice by either Power. In
all Asiatic matters Britain and Japan are to act as
one. If, by reason of an unprovoked attack or
any aggressive action, wherever arising, the
interests of either party are attacked, the
other party must join in the war. It may
be stated as an axiom of war calculations
that the enemy whom you wish to attack
is always the first aggressor. It was the
aggressive action of Denmark in presuming to
have a fleet that made Britain wage the war which
ended in the destruction of that fleet at the battle
of Copenhagen. It was the aggressive attitude
of Spain in refusing to fully recognise his brother
as king that made Napoleon attack that country.
It was the aggressive attitude of the lamb in
persisting in drinking at the same stream that
made the wolf eat the sheepling. For all practical
purposes the reservations about “ unprovoked
attacks” and “aggressive actions” may be
ignored. What remains is the fact that if Japan
goes to war over an Asiatic matter, Britain must
come to its aid. If Britain is attacked in Asia
Japan must join in the war. There is a practical
“ pool ” of armaments for
The conditions under which armed assistance shall be
afforded, in the circumstances mentioned, and the means
whereby that assistance shall be made available, shall be
arranged by the naval and military authorities of the High
Contracting Parties, who from time to time shall mutually
consult fully and freely upon all questions of mutual
interest.
That is to say, Japan has a right to be consulted
as to Britain’s naval and military arrangements,
and vice versa. It is the most complete and whole-
hearted compact possible, with no limitations
about a benevolent neutrality, or suggestions as to
merely keeping a ring and ensuring a fair fight.
In Japan’s next war Britain must join in, even
if the war be against the United States, which
“aggressively ” continues tc hold the Philippines,
or France, which is still “aggressively ” located in
Tonquin. As to the position of Australia, which,
in an “aggressive” sort of way, continues to
partly exclude Asiatic immigrants, its situation is
preuliar, and seems likely to call for a good deal of
negotiation.
• * * *
It must be with a very wry face that Great
Britain accepts this fact. From an attitude remi-
niscent of the sturdy days of the Roman Republic,
which “never made treaties except under terms
of conquest,” the British power comes at one step
down to the level of the days of Constantine,
when Sarmatians had to be subsidised to keep off
the Goths, and Goths to keep off the Sarmatians.
But the treaty with Japan had become necessary,
or so nearly necessary that the only alternative
was a courageous national policy on the part of
Great Britain, a reorganisation of her industries,
a restoration of her agricultural population, and
the devotion of her manhood to the task of
defence. Japan having been sheltered by Britain
until it reached the rank of a first-class Power,
the balance of might could no longer be secured
—as it might have been before —by a treaty,
say, with France. Such a combination might be
left helpless before an agreement between Japan,
Russia, and Germany, which could have attacked
the alliance on land at two points. A national
re-organisation in Britain would, of course, change
the situation ; it would make it possible for an
Anglo-French alliance to defend the boundaries of
France and India by land as well as to command
the sea. But Britain has at present practically
no army, and it is a matter of some doubt whether
it is now capable of making the efforts and the
sacrifices necessary for such re-organisation as will
make national land defence real and possible.
The country has been, for years, becoming
flaccid at the heart. You may still meet English-
men in high places in London, but rather rarely.
That city grows yearly more and more the
Stock Exchange and the amusement ground
of the international financier, and rural England
drifts month by month into the hands of foreign
millionaires. Assuming that Britain wishes to
remain the Britain of to-day, to allow present
tendencies to develop unchecked, the Japanese
treaty is almost essential to the national existence
—certainly essential to the security of India, for
Japan, if not allied with Britain, would almost
certainly obtain an alliance with some other Power.
And, as Japan is avowedly hostile to European
rule in Asia, and there is hardly a Power with
which it could make alliance that is not hostile to
British rule in India, there is only a choice between
an Anglo-Jap treaty for the (at least temporary)
defence of British India, or a Jap treaty with
someone else for the destruction of British India.
• * * *
Withal, the measure of security that Britain
wins is not full, pressed down and overflowing.
Japan, it is true, guarantees Britain in India.
But of what practical value would that guarantee
be in case of an invasion of India? Would
Britain be able to send Japanese troops
through Bengal to the frontier of Hindu-
stan, to thus advertise to its Asiatic subject
that he was under the protection, not
of the superior European, but of another
Asiatic? All that we know about the Asiatic
mind suggests that such policy would be rife
with possibilities of disaster. Britain holds
India, not so much by physical as by moral force.
The bulk of the Indian army is of the subject race,
so that the conqueror does not even hold down the
country with his own arms. Once thoroughly
free the Indian mind from the sense of the inevit-
able superiority of the European —once give him
the idea that an Asiatic from Japan is as good as
an Englishman—and the days of British
rule in India are numbered. On the other
hand, what Japan gains by the treaty is
very real. Its suddenly won position as a Power
is absolutely guaranteed and its overlordship of a
good part of Asia secured. The only danger the
Monkey has now to fear is that of a pan-European
combination against Great Britain and Japan, and
that is but remotely impossible. In diplomacy
Tokio has taken full advantage of the knowledge
that London was in a tight place.
* * » *
So far as Australia is concerned the treaty is, of
course, profoundly distasteful. In the various
conflicting currents of “foreign policy” Aus-
tralia has little interest; but is firm in the
one faith that white race supremacy is the
only hope of democratic civilisation. An alliance
which further buttresses a Yellow Race, and
raises up at our very door a powerful antagonist
of European ideas, cannot be looked upon in any
other light than that of a calamity. But there is
at least this reason for congratulation, that Japan
has not been able to secure—so far as the pub-
lished terms of the treaty indicate—any rights of
free immigration within the Empire which would
-conflict with a White Australian policy. There is
some ground for uneasiness in the cabled news
that Canada “has applied to be brought under
the terms of the treaty,” for there is nothing in
the treaty so far as published to affect Canada.
But, presumably, if there were any secret clauses
dealing with immigration, Australia would have
been info, med, and silence on that point means
relief from a dread which was very real.
BENT IS DOING HIS BEST.
Lately some deluded persons at St. Kilda (Mel-
bourne) gave a smoke-night to that nefarious Tory,
M'Cutcheon, M.L.A., and Premier Bent arose
amid the haze to make a few remarks. The bur-
den of these observations was that Victoria
required more population, and that he (Thomas)
was doing all he knew in the good cause. To
quote a daily paper report, headline and all:
Mr. Bent doino his Best.
Mr. Bent said the time had arrived when the .’ should
consider the question of population. They had ouudings
and institutions and means of government for four times
the number of people here, and they must do what they
could to attract to the State the right class of people. If
thej’ could secure a reduction in the rates of passage, and
have people coming here in thousands, they would be
reducing their taxation and increasing their prosperity.
They were about to submit a scheme of agricultural edu-
cation which would make for the advancement of indus-
try, and in other ways they were doing their best for the
people of Victoria.
If Thomas Bent is doing his best, all that can be
said is that it is a very poor best, and until a really
heavy graduated land-tax is put on —one heavy
enough to break up the great estates, to make
the holding of great estates ruinous, and to make
the accumulation of any more great estates im-
possible—neither Bent’s best nor his worst will
do much good to the population question in Vic-
toria. When the Premier of a State which, for
over 13 years, has sent away on an average 12,000
or 14,000 of its citizens annually, talks about
bringing in strange people instead of considering
how to stop the appalling outflow of his. own
people, it is a humorously wooden-headed business.
* # * *
It is 35 years p’xvce the curse of the great land
monopolies fell • lefinitely on Victoria as to
start driving the *_.mer’s sons out of the country
to look for land. For 35 years almost the only
serious chance Victoria has had of accumulating
population has been in the expansion of Melbourne
and a few other towns, and in the building up of
manufactures in Melbourne and one or two other
towns. The great country population, which
should have grown up concurrently with the city
population, has been suppressed or extirpated by
gross and unpatriotic landlords, who shooed the
people off their vacant principalities, and refused
to let them live on any fair or possible terms in
their own country. Most of the country towns
which should have grown up into prosperous
manufacturing centres are strangled by the
same curse; the land around them is mostly
kept a wilderness by the insane greed of the great
land monopolists. In such a case concrete ex-
ample is far better than loose declamation.
Victoria is divided into 37 counties. Nine of
these are mostly inaccessible mountain top, and
four are almost wholly desert. There are 24
fertile or reasonably fertile counties. Between
1871 and 1881 nine of these showed a decrease of
population, while in four counties which showed
an increase the gain of population was 4, 58, 81
and 123 individuals respectively. Between 1881
and 1891, eight counties showed a decrease of
population, and eight showed a loss of population
between 1891 and 1901. To get down to particu-
lars in some of the worst cases :
* * * *
County op Bourke : This is the county in which Mel-
bourne is situated. Population, apart from Melbourne,
in 1861, was 26,880 ; in 1901, only 21,k73. The County of
Bourke has an area of 1721 square miles, and contains, in
addition to Melbourne, many small towns. Population
outside the towns almost nil. The big estates of the
sheep-breeders start right at Melbourne’s backdoor ; a
few miles out and you strike grass and emptiness and the
curse of the land monopolist.
County of Grant: Next door to Melbourne. Well
watered and very fertile. Population 73,828 in 1871 ;
only 71,03 k in 1901. Contains Geelong, Ballarat East,
Buninyong, Queenscliff, &c.—towns growing ; country
districts being turned into a wilderness. Area, 1834
square miles ; population outside the towns about one
person to two square miles. Mostly owned by one or two
persons, who would rather see their State sink into Tophet
than give up the satisfaction of looking out on their miles
and miles of empty plains and their occasional sheep, and
saying “ We are monarehs of all we survey.” Same
sentiment which influenced William the Conqueror when
he desolated Hampshire to make the New Forest.
County of Daliiousie : Due north from Melbourne, and
only 43 miles distant. Population in 1871, 26,k71 ; in
1901 only 20,878. Fertile and well-watered. Traversed
by railway to Sydney and also railway to Bendigo. Area
1310 square miles. Outside the towns probably one person
to square mile.
County of Talbot : Also in the well-watered area ; 65
miles from Melbourne ; traversed by five railways and
contains ten towns of some importance. Population
in 1871, 8k,762; in 1901, only 57,398. Area 1621 square
miles. Outside the towns population almost invisible.
• * * *
County of Grenville : Towards the fertile west. Con-
tains such important towns as Ballarat West, Smythes-
dale, and Pitfield. Population in 1871, 60,917 ; in 1901
only k8,1k6. Outside a few towns a human being is
almost as rare as an alligator. Area, 1465 square miles.
County of Ripon : Further west; well-watered and
fertile. Population in 1871, lk,olo ; in 1901 only 12,689.
Area, 1759 square miles. Contains four important towns.
Outside them, about one human being to 2000 acres.
County of Follett : In the south-west, by the Glenelg
River. Fertile. Had 27k9 people in 1891 and only 2715
in 1901. Principal town has almost the whole population.
Area, 1103 square miles. About one person to eight
square miles outside the town.
County of Low an : In the west. Area, 4971 square
miles. Had 16,820 people in 1891, and only 1k,190 in
1901.
County of Kara Kara : Traversed by three railways
and contains a number of small towns. Area, 2301 square
miles. Population in 1881, 16,515 ; in 1901 only 16,k31.
Outside its towns, population almost nil.
County of Gladstone : Traversed by four railways and
pretty well watered. Area, 1802 square miles. Popula-
tion in 1881, 19,995 ; in 1901 down to 17,953.
County of G unbower : Has two railways, and three
rivers. Area, 1348 square miles. Contained 8706 people in
1891, and only 8167 in 1901. Average population off the
railway lines about nobody to the square mile.
County of Rodney (where the Kvabramapootra is):
Has two lines of railway ; well-watered ; splendidly irri-
gated at the State’s expense, the local swindlers having
refused to pay. Area, 1699 square miles. Contained
19,155 people in 1891, and 19,1k0 in 1901.
County of Wonnangatta : Splendidly watered by moun-
tain streams,. Abundance of water power. Area, 2224
square miles. Population in 1861, 3972; in 1901 only
2271.
* * * *
There is some material in this for Bent to do
his best upon. With the single exception of Won-
nangatta, which is partly a mountain region, the
counties above enumerated are in the most fertile
and best-watered part of Victoria, and their
decline is owing to the big landlord curse, and to
nothing else under the sun. They are simply
regions where the population is warned off. And
in addition to these counties, which show an abso-
lute loss of population since some bygone census
year when they were respectively at their poor
little zenith, there are others in the fertile Western
District which are practically stationary—Heytes-
bury (444 increase in 10 years); Villiers (168 in-
crease in 10 years); Normanby (250 increase in 10
years); and the like. The whole Western District,
comprising nine counties, or one-sixth of the State
the best-watered, most fertile, and most landlord-
cursed part of Victoria —has gained less than 5000
new population in 30 years, though the State in
general has gained 700,000. One barren, drought-
curse! county up in the Mallee, which has for its
sole advantage the fact that there are no big land-
lords to keep it back, shows nearly twice this prc«
gress. The barren and half-frozen mountain
counties are progressing because they are so poor
that the big land-thief didn’t annex them; yet
they have almost every possible drawback
except him. It is a sorry record. The artisan
and the manufacturer have done their share
to make Victoria a great State —they have built
up Melbourne. The farmer has done his share
and done it well in those places where the
great bloated insolent landlord did not in-
trude, and where the small independent cultivator
had a show. For instance. Grant, the first county
to the west of Melbourne, belongs mostly to one
or two proprietors, and its population has dimi-
nished 3000 in 30 years ; while Mornington county,
to the east of Melbourne, where the small pro-
prietor has had something of a show, showed over
19,000 increase in the same time. Premier Bent
is now “up against” the great Western District
landlords. He can’t make land. He can’t, by
the most desperate search, find good State lands
where there are nonethe most violent
inquiry which Melbourne Age could make only
revealed 14,000 acres of really good land still
in the hands of the State. He can’t get land on
voluntary principles from the great sharks at a
price which will render any big scheme of Closer
Settlement possible, for no great body of small
settlers could afford to buy at the price he would
have to charge after the principal shark was bought
out. So unless he can bring the great estates
down from the absurd values which they have
attained through exemption from taxation
unless, in other words, he can tax them down to a
fair value and force them into the market—the
spectacle of Thomas doing his best will be neither
amusing nor profitable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Old Age Pensions Joke.
Joseph Carruthers, Premier of New South
Wales, is a sidelong personage in his political
doings. He doesn’t meet his enemy face to face,
but sidles up to him—a trifle to the rear—and
snaps at his tail. He doesn’t propose to repeal
democratic enactments that are gall and worm-
wood to his six-and-eightpenny soul; he rather
notes points that require amendment, and in
his enthusiasm he repairs the Act till it is dead or
useless. He is doing this with the Arbitration
law, and he proposes to do much the same sort of
thing with the Old Age Pensions enactment. As
regards the latter, the measure which he has intro-
duced in the Assembly is a marvel of ingenuity
in the way of small, mean, sour restrictions
calculated to make any self-respecting ancient
cut his throat on the whited sepulchre of
Carruthers’ doorstep rather than accept
his grudging assistance. By way of a first
step towards reducing the number of pen-
sioners, it is intended that henceforth no one is to
be eligible for a pension unless he has lived
thirty-five years in N.S, Wales, and in addition
the years of residence must have been so far
consecutive that the applicant must have spent
35 out of the last 40 years in N.S. Wales. The
present Act requires 25 years residence, and it
must be so far consecutive as to be 25 years out of
the last 27. The suggested change disqualifies, in
one act, all the mass of immigrants who arrived
between 1870 and 1880, and in that period the
arrivals by sea alone numbered no less than
330,000. How many of these remained in the
State it is impossible to say, but as the gold-boom
was then past, and the country was settling down,
it is certain that a very large proportion of them
remained. By way of another innovation, where tie
present Act disqualifies the person who has served
a sentence of five years, the proposed new Act
will disqualify the person who has had a sentence
of five years passed on him, whether it was served
or not; apparently even if it was passed by a
judicial error, and never enforced, it may be held
as a disqualification. The foreigner who has the
requisite residential qualification now gets his
pension if he has been naturalised for 10 years ; it
is proposed to require that he must have been
naturalised for 20 years. The present Act dis-
qualifies for a pension the individual, even if
otherwise eligible, who has an income of £52 a
year or property worth £390; the proposed new
one will disqualify every person who has an income
of £39 or property worth £195. Also, when a pen-
sioner with a little property die§, the State is to
seize the property and sell it in order to recover
the pension, which thus degenerates into a loan.
But these are merely items of grinding economy—
the economy of a mean man who saves money
at the expense of the weakest class in the
community, and hits the person of least re-
sistance, so as to avoid taxing the wealthy
absentee who spends his money in England and
slanders with Stinking Fish yarns the country
that supports him. The peculiar genius of
Joseph Carruthers is shown in certain other
provisions. First he desires to make the examina-
tion of the applicant for a pension public— to
pillory the boasted “grand old pioneer” of so
much sentimental political oratory before a gaping
crowd as a pauper, a mendicant, and a show. This
will, doubtless, save a good deal of money, and if
it were proposed that the applicant must turn up
in court in a nude condition a much larger saving
might be effected. The idea of making the appli-
cant turn up in a nude state is hereby submitted
to Joseph Carruthers’ consideration. The idea
is said to be that this subjecting to publicity
of the old man or old woman will tend to shame
his affluent offspring, if there are any, into
providing for their parent, but that result would
be much better effected if the affluent offspring
were dragged to court and there ordered to pro-
vide for the aged progenitor. Putting the aged
progenitor up for public exhibition in order to
arouse a spirit of liberality in the affluent off-
spring is a circuitous way of doing things and
quite worthy of Joseph. Curiously enough, the
sidelong Joseph, with all his desire to make the
affluent offspring provide for the old man and the
old woman, doesn’t take the straight, simple and
effective method of just making them provide.
Instead of doing this, his Bill falls heavily on the
aged progenitor again, for it enacts that his or her
pension may be refused if the court is satisfied
that the offspring, or any other relatives, are
able and willing to provide. If it proves later
on that the court made a mistake in assuming
that they were willing, or if they lied when
they said they were willing, or if they were
really willing but their willingness promptly eva-
porated, then the aged progenitor may starve.
The proposed Act makes no provision for com-
pelling the affluent offspring or other relative to
live up to the promises on the strength of which
the pension was refused, so apparently any
malignant uncle, or second cousin, or step-son, or
other land-agent will be able to reduce the vener-
able one to hopeless want by promising and not
performing. There is, in all this, a touching
desire not to be too rough on the affluent off-
spring. Again, the whole pension can be
withheld if anybody is willing, or is supposed to
be willing, to partially maintain the applicant; if
part of his maintenance is supposed to be


provided for, the other part can be cut off.
Finally, even if the applicant is of the right
age, has the necessary residence qualifica-
tion, has an unblemished character, and has
no money, no property, and not a relative m the
world, the pension may be reduced as much as
the Commissioner pleases—and if the Commis-
sioner is the sort of man who wants to keep square
with Joseph by making things easy for Joseph s
surplus, he probably will please. If anything can
hasten on the Federal Old Age Pension Act, this
inhuman travesty ought to do it. Fhe whole
business can’t be taken out of the hands of State
politicians like Carruthers a day too soon.
Looking for Land.
Booth’s cable that he has 5000 families, packed
up ready for export to Australia, to be forwarded
f.o.b. as soon as this country is ready to receive
them, has caused a considerable flutter amongst
the State Premiers. They are all hustling around
looking for desirable allotments, with the excep-
tion of the Westralian Premier, who proudly
announces that his land is ready and waiting ;
and the greatest hustle of all is being accomplished
by Bent, of Victoria, who is covering great dis-
tances in his new motor-car, and saying “ Bloom-
ing,” and “Slap-bang,” and “ Huh !” with great
vigor. After five days’ breathless exertion, Bent
and his Minister of Lands, Murray, an-
nounced that they have found an ideal spot on
which to drop a fair-sized parcel of immigrants.
The spot bears the euphonious and significant title
of the Barren Plains, and is situated near Warr-
nambool. There are 25,000 acres of these plains,
all of which belong to the Government, though
they have been, and are still, open for selection.
Present writer knows the place well, and is
of opinion that, if Booth’s little army is placed
there, every individual will die within six
months. The plains consist of a curious, spewy,
barren, treeless area, placed, strangely enough, in
the heart of a forest. No grass grows on them,
the only vegetation being a useless sort of grass-
tree, which no animal will eat. An enterprising
farmer leased a portion of the area once, and put
some hardy young stock on it, with the result that
all but seven died. Even kangaroos and rabbits
avoid the dreadful spot. Two or three months
ago the Director of Agriculture, Dr. Cherry, had
a look at these plains, and he is now experiment-
ing with a plot. He believes that if it is drained
and manured it may be worth cultivating. The
chances are, however, that it may not. Most of the
area is boggy and water-logged for the best part of
the year; and, even if the land could be used, the
roads to the nearest railway are probably the worst
in the State, barring, perhaps, some in Gippsland.
Yet, to inspect this abode of desolation, Bent and
his minions had to pass through thousands upon
thousands of acres of the finest land in Victoria,
which is still practically unsettled. They had to
go—in the train —through huge stretches be-
longing to the Chirnsides and others, between
Melbourne and Geelong. Then, from Geelong to
Terang, on either side of the railway, further large
areas of magnificent land, running right up to the
railway fences, are passed through, with a few
sheep or cattle losing themselves in the rich
grasses. It is no exaggeration to say that, along
this one line alone, there could be settled 20,000
or 100,000 families. Labor-leader Watson put
his finger on the sore spot when he said, in
reference to this same scheme, that a land-tax,
with a stiff progressive incidence, would prove
the best method of finding land for farming immi-
grants.

The Federal Capital.
The Federal capital question is apparently almost
at a deadlock, though Prime Minister Deakin has
hopes of extricating it by a Bill relating to a peg.
The Australian Parliament selected a site in the
district which was declared, by an official appointed
by the N.S.W. Government to report on possible
sites, to be the most suitable in the State. It
selected a site not less than 100 miles from Sydney
as provided by the Constitution. It selected not
less than 100 square miles, as provided by the
Constitution. In asking for 900 square miles it
showed remarkable moderation ; considering that
there is required room for a city and its suburbs,
for a reservoir, for a catchment area to supply
water, and for an outlet for sewerage, it asked for
about the very smallest area that could possibly
suffice. And the Australian Parliament selected
one of the areas which N. S. Wales distinctly offered
by reserving the State lands thereabouts from fur-
ther alienation until the capital was decided upon.
Now Joseph Carruthers, of N.S.W., has per-
suaded the State Parliament to refuse this site,
and has repudiated the offer. Thereby Joseph is
true to his policy of trying to cover his wretched
inability to manage the things that are his busi-
ness by bellowing wildly about the things that are
not his business, by posing largely as the defender
of the State against imaginary incursions and
encroachments, and as the person who will be
brutally uncivil to the Australian Government
even if he dies for it, likewise as the perni-
cious small boy who will stick out his tongue
at the Australian Parliament to the last.
In pursuit of the bubble reputation as the indi-
vidual who defended somebody against some-
thing with an unspecified weapon, and thereby
averted certain circumstances and horrors not at
all defined, Joseph Carruthers wants the High
Court to decide the question, whatever it is, that
lies between him and Prime Minister Deakin ;
and Deakin, with his wearying affability, says he
is willing, if any means can be found to bring the
matter before the High Court. And now Carru-
thers doesn’t know for certain how to bring the
matter before the High Court, but Deakin is
preparing a Bill which he hopes will create
sufficient disturbance to hang the necessary law
suit on. The High Court isn’t supposed to
arbitrate on disputes, but to give judgment on
alleged breaches of law—therefore somebody
must break the law, and must break it in a large
enough way for the smash to attract the High
Court’s attention. So far all that Carruthers
can suggest is that the Australian Government
should drive in a peg to mark the first
beginnings of the new capital site, and
that he should proceed for trespass, and
thus open up the whole question, and it is
possible that the peg idea will be tried in
default of anything better. But it seems also
possible that the High Court will simply say that
trespass is a question to be dealt with by haul-
ing the man who personally drove the peg before
the nearest magistrate. And a magistrate, at the
worst, can fine him about 2s. 6d. ; while he may
decide that, unless the peg is driven in on a
highway where old women are liable tafall over
it, there is no offence. There is no law against
people walking upon unfenced State lands and
knocking about bits of wood thereon. Even if
the peg were painted red, so as to frighten cows,
it would hardly amount to a definite offence, or at
most would be an offence that ass. fine would
cover. Something might be done with Carruthers’
scheme, perhaps, if Prime Minister Deakin’s
minion came over the border and stole a
cow on the proposed capital site, or
slaughtered a heifer there without a licence,
or started hawking without a licence, or
assaulted a policeman, or set fire to the grass, or
set up an illicit still; there are any number of
ways in which the agent of the Australian
Government can attract the attention of the
N.S.W. law, but they all seem to be ways which
may simply lead to the agent being personally
dragged before a J.P. In fact, unless the Aus-
tralian Government sends the Federal army on to
Monaro, and it deliberately takes possession of
900 square miles of country, and warns the local
policeman that he will advance at the risk of get-
ting his uniform torn, and then starts to collect
the dog-tax as a Federal impost, it doesn’t appear
as if there is any certainty that Joseph Car-
RUTHERs’ scheme will amount to anything. The
peg alone looks rather insufficient, unless some-
thing is hung on it by way of emphasis. Perhaps
if it were a tall peg, and had a cross-piece at the
top, and Carruthers were hung on it, the matter
might be brought at once within the High Court’s
jurisdiction.

Educating an Upper House.
On Sept. 20th the Victorian House of Review,
after very brief consideration, passed the Factories
and Shops Acts Consolidation P>ill sent up to it by
the Assembly, and handed it on to Governor
Talbottle for his beautiful autograph. The sig-
nificance lies in the fact that the Bill, which not
only consolidates all measures of factory legisla-
tion, but also regulates the appointment of
Wages Boards, is a permanent measure, and was
actually passed “on the voices.” Certainly that
regrettable Tory landlord Harwood made an ex-
piring suggestion about fixing the Act’s duration
for three years only, but he was ignored. Yet
when, in ’96, Turner and Peacock first intro-
duced the Wages Board principle the Tory pro-
phecies about “restricting trade,” “harassing
manufacturers,” “Socialistic legislation,” “de-
struction,” “ruin,” etc., were loud and pitiful
enough to make the walls weep. That first Act
was passed for a three-years term only, because
the terrified Upper House insisted that it should
be so; since then it has been extended and
renewed; once it lapsed entirely for a few
months while Iceberg Irvine was getting measured
for his new Premiership suit; now it is passed by
the once-irreconcilable Landlords “on the voices.”
If the House of Landlords had passed the Bill of
1896 as a permanent measure an immense amount
of the Parliament’s time would have been saved,
and things would have been just where they are
now. All the wasted energy arose through
the hopeless ignorance of a crowd of legis-
lative dullards, who couldn’t see so far ahead
as the people they help to rule. It took nine
years to educate these mouldy and conceited
M.’sL.C. over this one matter up to the intellec-
tual level of the ordinary bootmaker or carpenter,
and to show them that they were wrong and the
bootmaker or carpenter was right. If Victoria
had only one House of State Parliament it would
be, as regards this law, just where it is now, save
that it would have been spared the loss of much
time, worry, legislative salary, and display of
Upper House ignorance. The reason why legisla-
tion is so pathetically slow is that the Assembly
has always to educate the Upper Tomb as it goes
along, and that place is dreadfully wooden-headed
over its lessons.

Standing Orders.
The Standing Orders Committee of the House of
Representatives has published its suggested rules
for debate, as revised to Sept. 14, 1905. The
House at present has no Standing Orders of its
own, and is working under the S. Australian rules
of debate ; this is due to the desire of the Reid
Indians to carry on a guerilla warfare of obstruc-
tion against any National Government in office,
and the lack of desire to do anything but
get into recess when they win office them-
selves. True, Reid was at one time going
to preserve the purity of the home and to
destroy Socialism by devoting a 1905 session
to Standing Orders, but he got “rattled ” over the
High Court trouble and received marching orders
instead. So the House still lacks Standing
Orders, and if an odd sitting could be devoted to
the consideration of these draft rules before the
session closes it would be a useful thing. There is
not much of a contentious nature in the rules sug-
gested. No attempt has been made in them to
cope with a system of organised obstruction which
might arise (and which, in the tariff and other
debates, actually did arise to a certain extent).
As they are, however, the draft Standing Orders
do away with many of the occasions for obstruc-
tion. For instance, No. 84 :
The following motions are not open to debate, shall be
moved without argument or opinion offered, and shall be
forthwith put from the Chair and the vote taken
(a) A motion for the first reading of a Bill.
(b) A motion that this debate be now adjourned.
(c) A motion in Committee that the Chairman report
progress, and ask leave to sit again.
(and) A motion in Committee that the Chairman leave the
Chair.
(e) A motion to reinstate on the Notice Paper any busi-
ness which has lapsed because of a count-out.
Should any such motion be negatived no similar motion
shall be received within a quarter of an hour of the
declaration of the preceding decision.
“A quarter of an hour” might be changed to
“an hour,” and the claws of the obstructionist
would then be considerably clipped. A proposed
new Standing Order to follow 38—
If the Speaker considers at any time that his attention
is being called unnecessarily to the absence of a quorum
and he is satisfied that there is a quorum within the
precincts of the House he may decline to count the
House.
has caused a great deal of silly comment. It
does not mean, as the REiD-ites insist, that the
Speaker will keep a House going with five or six
members present; but that he may, if he thinks
that a fair course, suppress the obstructive prac-
tice of calling for frequent “ counts ” (all of which
waste time), so long as he is satisfied that there is
a quorum of members in the precincts of the House
doing, or ready to do, business. If, for example,
during debate the jaundiced Johnson or the
wearisome Wilks drives members out of the
Chamber for a drink, or a breath of fresh air, or
the mellow joy of silence, the Speaker may, after
once satisfying himself that there is a quorum avail-
able, refuse to allow those gentlemen—or any equally
deadly bores on the other side—to insist on fellow-
members submitting to the torture of their talk.
If the Standing Orders could be made to read that
a talker failing to keep a quorum should thereby
lose his right to continue his speech, and that the
House should count out only when, the person
who emptied the House being silenced, the ringing
of the bell fails to attract a quorum, it would meet
the case better. But probably that would be too
original a notion altogether, not to be found in
May and therefore anathema. The only really
serious point of criticism in the whole draft is
raised by Standing Orders 254, 255, 256, 257
elaborating the procedure to be adopted for the dis-
cussion and possible acceptance of amendments pro-
posed inßills by theGovernor-General. Now we all
know that the G.-G. has nothing more serious to
do in the way of proposing than healths and votes
of thanks. If he amended a Bill of the Australian
Legislature he would soon find himself out of
office. But the venerable humbug that the G.-G.,
like the sovereign in Britain, has the power of
amendment and rejection of Parliamentary Bills
is solemnly enshrined in the Standing Orders !
There is no reason for it at all, unless it is to mock
the G.-G. with the shadow of powers that he does
not possess. And the G.-G. is an amiable and
capable figure-head, with an amiable and capable
wife, and does not deserve any such gratuitous
“cheek.”

The Effrontery of Certain Absentees
Ip the Westralian Chamber of Mines could use
segments of its cheek in the rock-drills, it would
start anew era in mining. The Secretary of that
body told the Tariff Commission, now sitting at
Kalgoorlie, that mining machinery should be
duty-free because
Mining was taxed by the State to an enormous extent.
It had to pay dividend duties and heavy railway charges.
The price of water was considerable.
And the mostly-foreign co.’s which are looting
Westralian gold pay very little in direct taxes,
and get their water far below cost, and at a rate
quite unprofitable to the State, while, as for
the railway charges, these are not taxes but very
moderate payments for services rendered. The total
of dividend duties for 1904 was £125,000, and the
total mining receipts £121,000. This second sum
represented the fees paid in return for survey,
assay, and inspection services, and the use of
public batteries, and didn’t recoup the State for
the cost of those services, so the shortage was a
sum taken as taxation from the people in general
as a bonus to the mining industry. The first sum
a little more than covers the loss on the Goldfields
Water Supply account, certified to by the Audi-
tor-General as £101,910 for 1904. Since the divi-
dend-tax falls not only on mining co.’s, but on
banking and all other corporations, the proportion
of the total of £125,000 dividend-tax paid
by the mining co.’s is probably less than
£101,910, the deficit on the water scheme
carried out for the benefit of the mine-
owners. Net result, thus, is that mining is
not “taxed to an enormous extent by the State ” ;
on the contrary, it is not taxed at all and gets the
benefit of police protection, cheap posts and tele-
graphs, and all other advantages of civilisation
without paying a cent in return. And the mostly
absentee vampire co.’s represented on the Chamber
of Mines have the ineffable impudence to ask
that the Australian machinery industry should be
prejudiced to allow them free imports !
