Secession—from N.S.W.
“X. 8.” writes :
I am about to start a secession movement, and wish to
ask the help of The Bulletin. It is to be a real secession
movement—not a mean and miserable little thing, con-
ceived in stupidity, and dying out in bluster, like John
Haynes’s secession. My object is to secede from New
South Wales, and form anew Australian State. It has
been clear, for a long time—though I say it with shame—
that N.S.W. is no place for a man who cares more about
Australia, the nation, than he does about N.S. Wales, the
State, or Sydney, the town. Putting aside the work
of a few men like Barton and O’Connor and Wise —
who, of course, have been practically repudiated by
N.S.W., and have been uniformly accused of having been
bought by the other States —putting aside the work of
these men, the record of the relations between the State
and the Federation is a record of meanness, and provin-
cialism, and dishonesty. Those are strong words, but
they’re true. The record began with a rank swindle,
perpetrated by N.S.W. on the other States, by which the
majority necessary to secure the passage of the Common-
wealth Bill was raised from 50,000 to 80,000. At least, if
N.S.W. didn’t do this, she allowed George Reid to do it,
and tolerated him after he had done it. The Bill was
carried, against a hurricane of lying and abuse
from the provincialists. But it was only carried
because the arch-provineialist, Reid, haggled
until he managed to insert in it a clause,
which nobody can understand, whereby N.S.W. was to
get the Federal capital. Because nobody can understand
the clause, the establishment of the capital has been
delayed, it has also been delayed because, whenever the
Federal Government made a movement towards settling
it, the State Government and Parliament blocked it.
Meanwhile, the air has been rent with cries about the
wickedness of the Commonwealth in depriving N.S.W. of
her right to the capital. If N.S. W. hasn’t done this, she
has tolerated Governments and Parliaments and news-
papers that have. The Federal tariff was imposed, and
N.S.W. squealed at the prospect of more taxation and a large
surplus which she didn’t want. To-day, she is squealing—or
encouraging Carrcthers to squeal—because the surplus
which she didn’t want shows signs of diminution. The
Federal surplus gave her a chance to be honest, and pay
current expenditure out of revenue, instead of out of
loans. She indignantly refused—or she tolerates and still
tolerates Governments which refused —to do anything of
the sort, and roundly cursed the Commonwealth for
making honesty possible. Not only did she refuse to be
honest herself, but when the Commonwealth declined to
be a party to the swindle of paying for defence works, post
office buildings, repairs, &c., out of loans, she complained of
the gross injustice that Federation was inflicting upon her.
Federation has given her over £4,000,000 of revenue which
she wouldn’t otherwise have had. Still she snivels —or
allows Carruthers to snivel—at the “ cost of Federa-
tion,” and at the difficulty it has introduced into State
finance. The real difficulty it has introduced into State
finance is the difficulty of being quite as dishonest as the
State used to be. Still, See and Carruthers seem to
have surmounted this difficulty without much trouble.
N.S.W. maintains a set of land laws which make it next
to impossible for her own citizens to get a decent piece of
land, unless, of course, they can afford to employ W. N.
Willis. And all the time, she snarls —or allows Car-
ruthers to snarl —at the Commonwealth for preventing 1
people from coming from the other end of the world
to settle on land that they couldn’t get near, even if the
Federal Government paid their passages and met them on
the wharf with a four-in-hand. It’s true that Carruthers
has begun to realise this, in his own mean way, and has
begun to make some land available for settlement. But
when Deakix tried to help him to get some immigrants to
settle on it, and asked in a most courteous way to be
allowed to communicate direct with the Agent-General,
he was met with a piece of rude stupidity which is un-
equalled even in the history of Carruthers. Yet N.S.W.
tolerates Carruthers.
And now, at the end of this record of meanness and
stupidity and dishonesty, this precious Carruthers, who
has muddled the finances, muddled the Lands Commis-
sion, muddled nearly everything he has touched, coolly
tells the Legislative Assembly, in answer to a foolish
question by a person named Anderson, that he “ views
with apprehension ” the prospects of a debate on the sub-
ject of Federal legislation, knowing, as he does, “ the
strong feeling of members of this House, and of the
people of N.S.W., with regard to the effects of Federal
legislation.” Then, by way of being humorous, he tells
one Kelly that he is “ very much inclined to put a notice
on the business paper for Home Rule in N.S.W.” I don’t
mind telling Carruthers that I “ view with apprehen-
sion” the prospect of Home Rule in N.S.W. under
J. Carruthers. Fancy re-transferring the Defence, the
Customs, the Post Office from a Government and Parlia-
ment which, with all their faults, have been honest and
intelligent, to a Government led by J. Carruthers, and a
Parliament which tolerates him. The prospect makes
one shiver.
The fact is, the only branches of public life in N.S.W.
that can be viewed without something very like con-
tempt, are the branches that belong to the Common-
wealth. The Commonwealth, indeed, has its weaknesses,
and very serious weaknesses they are. They arise mainly
from the presence and influence in the Federal Parlia-
ment of people like Reid, Cook, Johnson, Wilks, and
Kelly— all of them persons of the Carruthers type, and
all of them thinking their own cabbage-patch vastly more
important than Australia. There are, unfortunately,
members of the same type from other States. But no
State has produced such a large and baneful crop of them
as N.S.W.
These are some of the reasons why I want to secede
from N.S.W., and found anew, and truly Australian,
State. It wouldn’t be difficult to frame the Constitution.
The first thing- to put in, after preliminaries, would be a
clause making J. Carrutiiers, and all persons like him,
prohibited immigrants, for ever and ever. The next, a
clause making the Daily Telegraph, or at any rate its
scandalous and wicked accounts of Federal matters, a
prohibited import. For the rest, the State may be thrown
open to everybody who is Australian first, New South
Welshman second, and Sydney man third. There are
still a good many of these in N.S.W., as J. Carruthkrs
may find out some day to his cost. I’ll be glad to hear of
intending seceders.

Making His Aquaintance.
From time to time the loyal Britisher who regards
the Anglo-Monkey alliance in much the same way
as the Sunday-school teacher regards the Bock of
Ages, arises and deplores the fact that we do not
know our gallant brown ally as well as we ought
to. Well, we are beginning to learn things about
the ally aforesaid, and it looks very much as if he
will form a violent contrast to Sir Lancelot of
the Lake by the time he protrudes some more from
the mists of the dark ages and the horrid mess of
disembowelled suicide, which latter makes his past
look like a widespread post mortem. A while back
every loyal Jingo deemed it his duty to point out
to his small son the necessity of taking
a lesson from our gallant brown ally, and
spent hours dropping down dead in public places
in his amazement that the Monkey should, in a
mere five minutes, have learned all there was to
learn about Christian and human ethics. In fact,
the tall buildings U3ed to shake with the vibration
caused by Fat falling on the pavement in his grief
that we were on the same level as the anthropoid
ape when compared with our gallant ally ; while
it wasn’t safe to be out in the streets owing
to the explosion of prominent citizens at the
bare thought of the Immigration Restriction Act.
The first large disturbing fact that reached us was
the recent news that our gallant ally, on hearing of
the establishment of peace, had burnt several
churches and a mission station in Tolcio. Next day,
of course, the cable fairly boiled with a perspiring
message tumbling over itself to explain that the
arson was due to a low-down Salvationist spitting
on the pavement, or stating that peace was good, or
doing something equally disloyal. Although all
good Britishers agreed that the arson was justifi-
able, the incident was rather unfortunate ; but
Fat still stated in a thick voice that our gallant
ally’s reputation for patriotism was as notable a
circumstance as a long beer in Hades. In this
age of disillusion, said Fat and the Jingo
press, the law of gravitation is liable to be
proven bogus, and the globe a hallucination, and
W. N. Willis a rumor, and the public debt a
myth, but the one certain fact is the pure fire of
patriotism in the bosom of our gallant ally. We
were asked to believe that when the Universe
went bung the said patriotism would be the only
thing left, and would go on revolving in space
and shedding a bright light on the cavity. And
now even this belief is beginning to get some
severe jolts. The cable man, in an absent-minded
mood, sent a message the other day, stating that
the naval officers of our gallant ally had,
in a year’s operations, embezzled £30,000,
just as if they were Europeans. Seeing that
there were millions of pounds floating about, and
that the embezzlement went on for a year, and
that the Jap is credited with knowing the white
man’s system right through, the chances are that
the cable man is trying to break the full damage
to us gently, much in the same way that Jones tells
Mrs. Smith that Smith has had his hat ruined, by
way of progressing to the real fact, which is that
Smith has been run over by a freight train.
This, however, is a detail; the news is a further
contribution to our knowledge of our gallant ally,
and is accordingly something to rejoice about.
A Hew Arbitration Principle.
Judge Hetdon, the new president of N.S.W.
Arbitration Court, has explained the principle on
which “the question of preference to unionists
will always be dealt with.” He won’t grant pre-
ference on the ground that unionism is a good
thing and should be promoted ; for, “if the Legis-
lature had held that view, it would have made
preference compulsory.” The only principle the
new president can discover is this -
As far as possible the same award must be given as
would have been arrived at by the parties themselves,
So, the casual wharf-laborers’ union having al-
ready won preference, the award incorporates
preference. But constant wharf laborers not
having won preference, they are not granted pre-
ference in the award. Judge Heydon’s guiding-
principle, thus stated, is so fallacious that it should
be promptly changed. Though the Legislature
didn’t say in so many words that unionism was
a good thing and should be promoted, it said that
arbitration was a good thing and should be pro-
moted ; and the only way in which arbitration
can be promoted is by promoting unionism.
Workmen are divided into two classes—those who
agree to arbitrate and who bind themselves by
penalties to obey awards in arbitration, and those
who won’t agree to do either of these things. And
because it believed that arbitration is a good
thing, the Legislature certainly intended that,
unless there were special objections, the class that
agrees to arbitrate should be preferred. Judge
Hkydon says that the Legislature would have made
preference compulsory if it had thought unionism
(that is to say, arbitration) should be promoted.
But in coming to that conclusion the Judge over-
looks the fact that there may be special objections
so strong as to make the general rule unworkable,
or at any rate irksome. Therefore, while clearly
enough indicating that preference was desirable,
it gave the Court discretion to withhold prefer-
ence where special circumstances were disclosed.
If, further, a union can only get preference by
winning it apart from the Court, only those unions
can, generally speaking, get it which have been
successful in past strikes ; and the whole Act is
based on the assumption that the results of strikes
were uncertain and often unjust. It is hot those
who have deserved preference and other
victories that have won them, but those
who have had might on their side,
and not necessarily right. And the Court
is supposed to consider right and not might.
Even now other bodies might win preference, too,
if they could strike ; but strikes are illegal. If
two men had a dispute over a piece of land, and
came before Judge Heydon, the Court wouldn’t
argue like this “ I think if they had fought for
it, Brown would have knocked Jones out; there-
fore, I give the land to Brown.” Yet that is ifm
alleged principle he lays down to guide him
through the difficulties of the Arbitration Court.
It is a principle that ought to be discarded as
being utterly unworthy of a Court that is supposed
to be governed by equity.
Health Minister Cameron has introduced a pro-
gressive and commendable Pure Food Bill to the
Victorian Assembly. Briefly, it provides for
appointing a Foods Standard Committee, consist-
ing of the chairman of the Board of Health, the
University professor of chemistry, the ditto of
physiology, the medical officer of Melbourne city,
the president of the Chamber of Manufactures

and the ditto ditto of Commerce. What know*
ledge these last two have of pure food—except
such as they consume personally—Heaven and
Bent only know. This committee is to prescribe
standards for the composition, strength and
quality of any food, and to prohibit the manufac-
ture, storage, or packing of food that is contrary
to standard. Every package must bear a label
showing its net weight. Any article containing or
mixed with any substance in any quantity which
diminishes its nutritive value shall be deemed
to be adulterated. The person who sells wines
containing prohibited ingredients is made re-
sponsible. Injurious articles are prohibited
in the manufacture of children’s toys — the
first impulse of the average infant being to stuff
its toy into its mouth. Beer is to be deemed
below the standard if it contains more than one-
hundredth of a grain of arsenic per gallon, or any
lead, copper, strychnine or other abnormal consti-
tuent, Also all alleged disinfectants are to have
their constituents specified on the label. Goods
are to be labelled as to quantity and quality, and
graded as per the Board’s regulations ; all that are
below the Board’s lowest grade and all deleterious
substances to be confiscated and destroyed.
♦ —
Successive Westralian Governments, in their
anxiety to splash up the proceeds of various loans,
have attempted to separate large and enterprising
oolonies of rabbits by running a wire fence for
about 1000 miles across their Great Sand Patch.
The alleged rabbit-proof fence falls down at
frequent intervals, and search parties have to
burrow in the sand to find the posts and straining
joints. The cost of maintenance is, by now,
almost as great as the original expenditure.
Rabbits are swarming on both sides of the fence,
but the fenoe is maintained all the same. Some
extraordinary regulations have been issued by the
bosses of the Rabbit .Department—they have a
department for everything in Westralia. One is
that anybody seen in possession of a rabbit, dead
or alive, may be fined £50, and to even have the
ears, nose or tail of a bunny stowed away in your
olothes is an offence. Rabbits may be shot,
poisoned, or knocked on the head with a tele-
graph pole, but must not be removed from where
they fell. The wayback miners, living for months
on tinned dog and damper, scrupulously observe
the regulations for rabbit suppresion, and no dead
bunnies are left about their camps. The officer in
charge of the Rabbit Department (the Chief
Rabbiter) is Crawford, until recently acting-
Direotor of Agriculture, but superseded by
Chaplin, of Queensland. Now he draws a screw
nearly as big as Chaplin’s for looking after the
fence which divides one rabbit from another.
The Westralian estimates are very instructive, so
far as concerns the art of rushing money down
a steep place into the sea.

Clause six of Senator Keating’s Copyright Bill
reads!—=■
No copyright, performing right, or lecturing right shall
subsist under this Act in any profane, indecent,
seditious, or lihellous work or matter.
This was the cause of a rather interesting debate.
Senator Symon led off by pouncing on the word
“profane,” which he rightly denounced as vague
and meaningless. So “profane” was shot out,
and “ blasphemous ” —which isn’t a very great
improvement—substituted. Blasphemy of course
is largely a matter of education and temperament.
Then Symon tried, rather foolishly, to have
‘‘immoral” put in the plaoe of “indecent.” and
instanced the famous “ Fruits of Philosophy ” as
a hook that weathered the storm of a trial for
“ indecency.” The definition “ immoral ” would
have bagged that book, said Symon. To which
Senator Givens made the apt and obvious reply
that it would bag a good many more—to wit
Byron, Shakspeare (here Symon turned pale),
and the Bible, not to mention Pope, Shelley and
Burns. It is rather amazing that Symon should
be responsible tor such an inept suggestion,
for “immoral” is one of the least definite
words in the English language, Senator
De Largie remarked that he had heard the
word applied to. the writings of Henry George,
And didn’t Walter Murdoch get into trouble
with those eminently respectable publishers—
Ward, Lock, & Co. —the other day for calling
Guy Boothby’s books immoral ? One senator
wanted to use “ obscene,” which is only indecent
writ large; and another advocated “prurient.”
One can imagine the High Court deciding whether
a given work was prurient or not, which again is
largely a matter of temperament, “ Don Juan,”
“Tom Jones,” “Tristram Shandy,” and the
“Canterbury Tales” are all prurient in parts;
but who would deny the benefit of copyright to
books of their calibre, although, as Macaulay
said of Leigh Hunt’s edition of the Comic
Pramatists of the Restoration, they are hardly
hooks to be given as prizes at a girls’ school?
finally, the Senate threw out “immoral,” andi
left the original word in. So any Australian
aufhor, if he wants protection for his books or
plays, must avoid blasphemy, indecency, sedition,
or libel.

A good deal of the talk in N.S.W. about “ the
obstacles put in the way of men who want to
select Crown lands,” and about the employment
of “cobweb regulations” to harass them after
they have got land, comes from people who don’t
want to see a progressive land-tax. Their idea is
to suggest that there is plenty of suitable public
lands still available if only it could be got. As a
matter of fact, a great deal of the time of local
land boards is taken up in discovering or inventing
excuses for settlers who haven’t complied with the
law. In many of the most hopeless districts a
man may break practically every other condition
of his lease so long as he shows himself a genuine
settler and keeps his rent not greatly in arrears. In
the West, before the Western Lands Commissioners
took that country over, by far the commonest land
board decision was—“ We find that forfeiture has
been incurred, but recommend that it be waived,”
and in 99 oases out of 100 the Minister waived it
accordingly. Granted that some of the depart-
ment’s methods are cumbersome, and that the
administration has been bad in patches, it is just
as well to keep in view the other aspect of the
matter —that much of the yelping about “ vile
land laws,” nialadministration, &c., is the red-
herring cunningly drawn across the trail of a pro-
gressive land-tax by people who want to hang on
to their big estates a while longer. Then, if only
this immigration of agriculturists can be whipped
up, they will be in clover ; the State will have to
get land somewhere, and just about that time the
owners will be heard from, and the prices they will
ask will make the angels weep. Also they will
make the settlers bleed, which will be even more
disastrous.

Mr. A. A. Blackman, who is not one of the great
Stinking Fish party, writes : —
I have just returned from a trip to England after a 3J
years interval. I calculate that I spent—out of a total
of seven months—quite two months in explaining to
all classes of Englishmen—including even some “ great
merchants” (names and addresses herewith)—that we did
not exclude those hatters; also that we did not burn
them alive or throw, them into a dungeon. The extent to
which that myth has got around is simply amazing. In
trains, here, there and everywhere I lost no chance—even
if I only had one of an audience (and I often had more)—
of explaining the situation. I simply explained why Aus-
tralia had—for the protection of the intending immigrant
chiefly—merely followed the United States and Canada in
adopting an anti-contract labor law with important
modifications not allowed by eitherof those countries. I did
not fail once to convince hearers that, properly regarded,
the contract labor clause, under which those hatters were
temporarily detained a little longer on the steamer than
would have been the case had their employer not. ne-
glected to comply with a very simple and trifling official
obligation, was a most wise and proper enactment. I did
not find even one person who was not under the impres-
sion that we had done something' awful to those hatters.
I feel inclined to recommend Australians to let the fatuous
British person slide, and lav themselves out to get—after
providing- the means of employing- them—a good number
of the best class of Dutch, German and Swedish settlers.
These people become part and parcel of the land of their
adoption, and would, moreover, be an acceptable leaven
tending to create that most proper and becoming form of
loyalty—loyalty to one’s own land.
—-- 1
“Better than nothing” is the kindest verdict
that any Victorian jury of disinterested experts
will give to Bent’s Closer Settlement Act. Until
a graduated land-tax shall compel the big holders
to sell their land at a fair market price, and a
Compulsory Purchase Bill shall give the Govern-
ment power to take what it wants, the inducement
to would-be settlers will not be very keen in Vic-
toria. The best of the land purchased thusfar is
only just good enough to tempt settlers under the
stringent terms of the Act. Still there is the
beginning of healthful legislation in the Act,
which serves Bent as an excuse for bellowing his
own praises around the country. It is “ better
than nothing.” And because it has this negative
virtue, the glaring error made by the Closer Set-
tlement Board in the matter of the Overnewton
estate appears pretty much of a public calamity
to hopeful citizens. The Act might have lived to
be respected as a well-meaning thing, but the
Board has brought it into disgrace by selecting six
or seven thousand acres of stony ground for cutting
up into farms. This transaction amounts to a
crime, and the Board now stands to be pelted
with chunks of the rock whereon its lamentable
misdeed was founded.

The Westralian Full Court broke out into an
extraordinary piece of insolence last week. Four
Chinese were charged with breaches of the Fac-
tories Act, and their lawyer had the cheek
to plead in defence that the law was bad
because it was repugnant to some alleged
treaty or other said to exist between
England and China. This plea that a law which
had been passed by an Australian Legislature,
and had the King’s sanction, could be over-
ruled and abolished and obliterated without
notice by a mere treaty was an astounding
sample of cheek, but the Court didn’t even rule
that the plea was bad ; in fact, it refused to
express an opinion on the matter. But it dis-
missed the case on the ground that it hadn’t
been proved that the Chinamen were Chinese, and
then Justice Parker broke into an extraordinary
diatribe against the Act itself and raged at large.
The habit some bumptious Judges have of mis-
taking themselves for legislators is remarkable,
and the necessity for holding an explanation
with these Judges is urgent. No doubt the Fac-
tories Act may seem remarkable to Justice Parker ;
but Westralia didn’t make him a Judge under the
impression that he had theintellect which was likely
to understand what kind of Factories Act the State
required, or the soul that was likely to appreciate or
sympathise with the necessity for such an Act. It
appointed him for the cold, wooden job of
administering the law, and he should stick to his
own wooden job, If Westralia had thought him
the sort of man who could make laws or decide
what kind of laws should be made, it would have
found him a different kind of job.

A Melbourne pressman :—
Melbourne medico H. YV. Bryant, in a recent lecture
under Health Society auspices, spoke a word on the
deterioration of Australian physique. Bryant has for 15
years been examining military’and naval recruits, and so
knows enough about his subject to make him worth
listening to. He says that, in the last two years, the
average of well-made men paraded before him has been
very low. Last Eight-hours Day called into the streets
what was, generally speaking, a procession of weeds.
Bryant spoke mainly of city dwellers, but the same
thing is happening all over Victoria. Some part of ti e
deterioration in the military can be ascribed to reduced
pay—once, a Victorian private got £15 a year for his
drills ; now he gets £6 12s, This narrows the field of
selection. But the trouble is here all right. Australian
games—particularly football—are good enough to build
up an athletic race, but the fact is that, as a
nation, we no longer play the games ; we watch them.
For every man playing foothall on a Saturday there
are 100 looking on, half of them smoking bad cigarettes
all the afternoon. It is a question whether the big pre-
miership competitions, to control athletic games in every
district, do not ultimately kill more muscle than they
develop. Government should look after this matter in
the schools. Education departments here are generally
ceasing, or attempting to cease, their old practices of
stunting body and mind by bad air and insufficient light ;
but they should go further, and make games or gym-
nasium practice compulsory. And for the populace, after
it leaves school, the Government should help to provide
football grounds, cricket grounds, swimming baths, or
gymnasia within easy reach of everybody in every town
and suburb. Finally, knock-off time’ in shops and
factories should give everybody half-an-hour or an hour
to exercise his body. And every young healthy man
found during that hour hanging over a bar-counter, or
cramping his stomach on a billiard-table, or slinging fool-
talk at a tea-room girl, should be publicly dropped into a
deep well.

Prince’s Court The Helter-Skelter Co.
At first you’re inclined to feel a bit annoyed
about it, and wonder why J. C. Williamson will
do such things. But you look around the house,
packed in every part, and find it merry as well as
full, and then you leave off wondering. Even J.
C. Williamson, you reflect, is entitled to make a
bit of money sometimes. And here, at Her
Majesty’s, in Sydney, he certainly doesn’t make it
under false pretences. The advt. says frankly
that you needn’t bring your brains when you come
to see “The J.P.” If you’re smart enough to
follow the usher to your seat you’re smart enough
to follow Caesar Montague through the three
acts. For one thing, you have followed
him often before. It was a Mahatma
or something that arranged things so that
the first production occurred at the Jewish New
Year; for the years of the Christian era don’t
give any idea of the extreme venerableness of this
old sinner—the gay giddy old smooth-faced
chap who exudes virtue and godly advice at home,
then flies across the Channel (or somewhere), and
gets into such a glorious state that he wanders
by mistake into Suzette’s room. Sometimes he
has a wife, sometimes a nephew. This time it’s a
nephew—it is usually a nephew nowadays, so as
to allow the nephew to get married to a giddy
thing unbeknown to uncle, for which, of course,
uncle will, if he finds it out, cut him off without
a shilling; and uncle always has £96,543
7s. lid. to leave to either a nephew or
the Society for the Domestication of the Polar
Bear, or to invest in Australia if it wasn’t for
Socialistic legislation. The principal difference
between this uncle and most of the others is that
most of the others have been hustling, bustling
comedians, while J. J. Dallas, the new man with
the English reputation, gets his effects in a quieter
way ; and the principal difference between this
nephew and the others is that the others haven’t
married a girl who can wear boy’s clothes half as
well as Miss I lorence Lloyd can. Somebody says
that Miss Lloyd, like all the rest of them that
get into something from the other side of
the wardrobe, gives herself away by smooth-
ing her back-hair. But Florence is' too good
a boy to brush her back-hair up; she
srtiooths it down — which marks all the
difference. Still, in general you can’t lpok
for fine effects in “ The J.P.” It is all
broad farce, and treated accordingly; you
don’t come to applaud finished acting, but' to
laugh; and there's plenty to laugh at. Conse-
quently you do laugh, though afterwards, for the
sake of your own good opinion of yourself, it’s
best to forget all about it. Miss Maud Chetwynd
and Gregan McMahon help to destroy Australia’s
chances of getting the £96,543 7s. lid. which
uncle might invest here if his nephew didn’t get
it; also Miss Chetwynd sings a little and dances a
little, and so does Miss Lloyd ; and the house
warmly approves both dive.-sions. In fact, the
house gets into such a frame of mind that it
would approve almost anything.

“A Woman of Pleasure,” revived at
Sydney Royal on Saturday, is of the saner order
of melodrama, and the sensationalism, though
laid on generously, respects the probabilities
somewhat. The story opens in an English
county, where the haughty Earl (George Cross)
suffers from cold feet and family pride to an
aggravating extent, and consequently his wife
(Miss Eugenie Duggan) proposes to elope with the
villain (Laurence Dunbar). But at the critical
moment she thinks of the progeny (Laura Sin-
clair) and its attractive piping treble, and decides
to endure the family pride, and to freeze patiently
in the cold draughts of the family castle. The
villain, not to be denied, abducts her, and
the curtain goes down on Act 1 with the haughty
earl resolving to cure the wounds in his family
pride by going and fighting for the Empire in
S’Africa, and burning some Boer’s quiet little farm,
and laying waste some Dutchman’s cow. That is
quite a natural transposition for melodrama.
Very often the whole cast is transported, while the
audience is having an interval drink, to Ashanti
or Thibet, for no earthly reason whatever, except
that the property master had some stuff that
would look well in an Ashanti forest or a Thibetan
Llamasery. The exciting scenes in S’Africa, and
the wreck of a transport on its way back from
Boer-slaying, are very effectively set forth, and the
company does justice to its opportunities. In ad-
dition to the principals mentioned, Edmund
Duggan, as a manly cleric, and O. R. Stanford, as
Jim Dench, are persons of some note.

As the Brough-Flemming Co. leaves by
the Waikare for Maoriland on Saturday of next
week, and as the Sydney public has shown no
sign that it is weary of Beauty’s flirtings and the
Bargee s haff ability, ” no change of piece is
announced. Yards and yards of Eight-Hour pro-
cession stretched around the auditorium at the
matinee on Monday, and all the federated seamen
m the yards agreed that the old Bargee (as given
by Mr. Brough) would make a humorous fellow in
the union. At next trip the Brough-Flemming
Co. will stage “ Quality.street,” a quaint, old
fashioned comedy* which Sydney has had the
misfortune to miss this time.
* * *
On October 14. after the Broughs have
departed, Alfred Dampier, a good old favorite of
whom Sydney lias seen nothing for a long while,
will take possession of the Criterion, and com-
mence his career with “ The Bush King.” Some-
thing might be said about the advisability of
making him a President instead of a King, to suit
the sensibilities of a country which has a con-
siderable republican instinct hidden away under its
clothes, but this point may stand over for further
consideration.
** * *
The march past the saluting-point of the
victorious Jap troops at the review held by Gene-
ral Nogi, after the fall of Port Arthur, beats all
ordinary processions out of sight. One wonders,
as the line stretches out past the crack of doom,
were any of the Japs killed, or were any absent in
hospital, or was it no one’s day for doing
hari-kari ? The Bulletin, it may be men-
tioned, is retrospecting on Mr. J. C. Williamson’s
Bio-Tableau, now starring at the Palace Theatre,
Sydney. All the pomp and circumstance, and
mighty little of the horrid details and sordid
effects of warfare are shown in the Bio-Flick.
But the Wandering Jew, Scenes in the Life of
Napoleon, the Land of the Midnight Sun, and
other non-bluggy pictures give a brilliant diversity.
The Bio-Flick will razzle until the order “Lights
out ” is given by the management.
** * *
If the Professional Musicians had adver-
tised their inauguration concert as “ A Saturday
Pop ” it would surely have drawn a good house.
Programme was capitally selected and ex-
cellently performed, but the advertised words,
“Symphony Orchestra,” scared the populace,
and the few Sydney-siders who would be
attracted by the blessed word Symphony said
they’d go next time-after Brown had told
them how he and Smith liked the first one. Either
that or they went as far as the Lyceum d.c. steps,
when perhaps their courage failed them. Those
steps look as if they led to a prison for steerage
passengers. If management won’t wash them,
at least it ought to provide rushes and
strew them with those old-fashioned vegetables.
Then it would be able to let the sanctified barn
some more. The Misses Collins, two Queensland
girls, with excellent soprano voices that showed
considerable training, made their first appearance
at the “Symphony.” Two solos and a duet
proved them to be an agreeably gifted pair of
youngsters and deserving of all the approving
noise an audience could make.
** * *
It was a pity that Miss Nance O’Neil
smudged her Sydney season with such a produc-
tion as “ The Fires of St. John.” That “master
work ” of Sudermann deals simply and straightly
with a squalid sex incident of the most repulsive
kind and that, too, “with the gloves off.” In the
farm-house of Brauer lives his wife, his daughter
Gertrude, and a foundling adopted daughter
called Marie. Brauer’s nephew, George, amuses
his adolescence by seeking to seduce Marie
in a rough and ready fashion, and doesn’t
succeed. Some years have passed over that
incident when the play opens with George
affianced to Gertrude, and Marie just awakened
to the fact that she “ loves ” George. Theeveofthe
wedding, George and Marie are left alone together
and she seduces him. Kneeling to him, she tells
him that she was sorry that she resisted him
before, and then, in a finale which it is impossible
to describe, it is made clear that no scruples
remain on either side. The light is shut off from
the stage at the critical point lest the curtain
shouldn’t come down quickly enough ; it is a wise
precaution. Next day George marries Gertrude.
* • • •
There can be no pretence that there is any
“ art ” reason for all this nastiness. There is no
shadow of a “problem” such as was urged in
justification of the nauseous “Resurrection.”
The play is just a sordid immorality incident be-
tween a man who is a low cad and a woman who
must be physiologically a maniac, for it is not
natural for an inexperienced woman to take a man
by storm. It is worse than the “Resurrection”
seduction incident, worse than “The Sign of the
Cross ” criminal assault scene, in its revolting and
unreal “realism.” The only literary production
of any note in the same camp is Zola’s “La
Terre.” It is not fair to the general public to stage
such stuff in a theatre to which both sexes and
all ages have access. Only a woman who has
attained the lateral mental development of the ex-
perienced matron could view it unharmed, and it
would not be a pleasant experience for her. Like
the sermons of the improper evangelists it should
be advertised “ For men only ” —if produced at all.
** * *
There isn’t a house in Sydney that answers
more promptly to the call of a good bill-of-fare
than the Tivoli. Manager Rickai'ds’ patrons are
connoisseurs in their way; they can’t be “ kidded ”
with big type and large ink-splashes— not to any
great extent, anyway. For a while lately the
place had large empty spaces ; now, with Rickards’
out scouting for new turns, the empty spaces are
disappearing, and some nights have vanished
altogether. And the bill (the same much-improved
bill that was noted last week) deserves the
business.
*& * * *
Paul Kruger—sexagenarian Kruger on
whom the sun was shining 25 years ago—crops up
again in Bland Holt’s current drama, at Mel-
bourne Royal, where the old man victorious gets a
“reception,” which amounts to a severe comment
on the subsequent war that knocked him out.
“ For England ” is a wide episodical fiction of the
Majuba period, with one act placed in the Trans-
vaal. The dear old Union Jack finally waves
triumphant under audaciously imaginative condi-
tions. The Boers seem to have all the best of it,
in accordance with history, until Lieutenant
Walter Baker is taken prisoner, whereupon the
British rally to the rescue, and the result is an
amazing battle tableau constructed on Waterloo
lines, and showing Baker in the military capa-
city of Bill Adams. He gets the Victoria Cross
for his valor, and returns to England in time
to explain that his late father’s murderer was not
himself, as his blind mother supposed, but Albert
Norman, a villain who has been chasing his
“revenge ” to S’Africa and back until his mono-
mania reduced him to a human wreck. The
earlier acts of “For England” are devoted to
love, and gee-gee, and Manasses Marks, a money-
lender, played by Bland Holt. A hunting scene,
the most picturesque and stirring thing of its kind
in Holt’s horsey records, leaves that active and
intelligent low comedian dangling from an oppor-
tune tree bough, whilst his docile steed walks
away from between his agitated legs. Bland’s
legs appear later in black tights, with trimmings
round the ankles, what time he masquerades as a
preposterous Zulu, but he returns to trousers at
the finish and delivers Norman up to justice. It


The name of the Senator who allows his
aged parents to live on an old age pension hasn’t
transpired yet. Dr. Lloyd, the remarkable Mel-
bourne J.P., who spoke very severely in public
about this alleged abandoned character, now
admits that he has no idea which Senator he was
talking about or where the Senator comes from.
He had been informed of the fact on a postcard
by a person unknown. Some of Dr. Lloyd’s
judgments look as though they were also supplied
on a postcard by parties unknown.

* * * *
If the story were more reasonable, Walter
Baker’s manly bearing and fine, hearty voice
would make his heroism absolutely convincing.
The British Government should recognise the ser-
vices of Walter as a “ loyalty ” promoter. He plays
his part for ten times its real dramatic worth, and
stems the tide of sympathy that flows towards the
Boer characters. Sty an,-Brawn and others lend a
little comic relief to the bit of Imperial bunting,
whilst Max Maxwell shines somewhat as a
chivalrous Boer, and Norman is the English traitor
who shoots him. The principal women parts,
played by Misses Boss, Ireland, Pollack and Mrs.
Holt, are named Alma, Vera, Lesbia and Keziah,
and, with the exception of Lesbia, who retires
early, they fall into the hands of the Boers, and
narrowly escape an awful doom. But their mis-
fortunes are of minor importance.

The well-decorated but otherwise deplor-
able drama—“ His Majesty’s Servant”—was to
flaunt its foolishness before a disappointed public
at the Princess’s until Tuesday of this week.
“Monsieur Beaucaire” will serve three nights,
and on Saturday a revival of “The Darling of the
Gods ’’comes due. “ The Darling ”is to elevate
Miss Jeffries to the outer heaven for one week,
but it is seemingly an open question whether
Julius Knight will reach the rolling clouds on
this occasion. A painful advt. credits Julius with
the intention to appear as the Lord High Execu-
tioner, a notoriously evil Jap who has hitherto
been and and beyond doubt. One is loth to be-
lieve, however, that the Darling of the Girls can
ever go elsewhere than upwards when he dies.
Probably the angels will manage to smuggle him
away on a rainbow of repentance.
** * *
The second month of “The Cingalee,” at
Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), is passing away like
the first one, and Bantock is understood to have
got married in the meantime. Miss Thomas sings
charmingly, the Tea girls cluster round their young
man as before, the missing birthmark retains its
vague significance, the rickshaw is there when re-
quired, and the new extras by Lauri and Miss
Rose Musgrove continue to catch on. The Golly -
wog marries the Dollywog nowadays, and the re-
sult of their dumb union is the Babywog, which
the happy father views with astonishment on his
return from the wars.
** * *
Reginald Ford, M.R.G.S., has lately been
talking of the South Polar regions in front of a
well-illustrated sheet at Melbourne Town Hall
and the Athenaeum Hall. Reginald (M.R GS )
refrains from blowing about the dangers and
inconveniences he put up with aboard the good
ship Discovery, and on the ice that blocked her
voyage. He was more modest than half-a-dozen
war lecturers of the Villiers type, and he is
chirpy and fluent, but there is no obvious reason
why Waxeellency Madden or John Forrest should
act as chairman in his honor. Moreover, the
Waxeellency is an error in tactics, seeing that the
public are wisely wary of any show which begins
with somebody in the chair. Reginald Ford, not-
withstanding the letters after his name,’ is a
showman. Hereafter he will travel with a lantern
and 130 slides, under the direction of Edwin
Geach, who regards an Antarctic adventurer or a
conjuror with the same cold commercial eye.
** * *
In the presence of a large weather-beaten
audience Prince’s Court declared itself open for a
second season on Saturday night. The Court
with its Chute, Toboggan-slide, Cafe Chantant]
&c., was a very prosperous undertaking last
summer, and now its joys are more varied and the
appointments in general are smarter. A Helter-
Skelter is anew development (or devilment) in
rapid down-hill flight warranted to cure the worst
form of liver complaint with one application of the
treatment. A series of war pictures worked with
moveable figures and assisted by cannonades, are
shown in anew wooden hall, 30yd. x 20yd., and a
new lady diver splashes in the tank where Annette
Kellermann played the mermaid of yore. The
one thing wanting at Prince’s Court just now is
better weather.
** * *
The reminiscences of old Melbournians
have received a pleasant shaking-up from the
announcement that Horace Lingard is about to
revive “Our Boys” a.t the Bijou for one week
Lingard claims to have played the aitchless
butterman in Byron’s comedy on 2000 occasions,
many of which were Australian events in the dim
long ago. Some of the surviving members of his
previous audiences will be glad to see him do it
again, if their failing limbs can climb the Bijou
steps, and for the more recent generations there is
the opportunity to sample the brightest work of
an author who was the boss humorist of his time.
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House £here are
some improvements in the first part without pre-
judice to the second part. The second part is main-
taining its vast success, and the Jackson Family
appears to grow an inch taller in popular esti-
mation every week. The Jacksons changed their
musical programme last Saturday. The conjuring
triumvirate—Le Roy, Talma and Bosco—will
change their programme next Saturday, and pro-
duce a fresh crop of strange deceptions and un-
accountable poultry. Meanwhile, a wire-fencing
is drawn across the front boundary of the stage to
prevent the geese and things from seeking refuge
in the orchestra.

“ Old Heidelberg ” at Adelaide Royal was
very well received, particularly by the feminine
part of the audiences. Harcourt Beatty did fine
work in a part not too well suited to him, and the
same may be said of Miss Nellie Stewart. In the
writer’s opinion, her growing mannerisms—always
most noticeable just when she aims at appearing
specially aytless and unaffected—don’t go well
with the simple “ Bitte, madeken, zwei glas bier ”
type of character. As for the play, it must rank
with ‘ Trilby as an exotic plant. The gallery—
a German gallery—flatters itself, by the way that
it could name the original of the Prince who was
kept on a chain by his court officials. The co. has
since fallen back on to the old familiar ground of
“ Sweet Nell,” the lady who got rid of all her
rivals in the monarch’s affections—and then said
“good night” and went home. At the Tivoli
I rank Clark and Harry Shine are still rioting
through a picnic known as Muldoon’s, but the
wizard Thurston is signalled in the offing.
AT POVERTY POINT.
In publishing- a photo, of Miss Florrie Green as “ the
most accomplished dancer in Australia,” The Bulletin, it
seems, raised a storm of dissent from the champions of
the other Melbourne dancing- school, run by the Misses
Hyams. Whereas graceful Miss Florrie embodies all the
virtues of the Green Academy, the airy Miss Maud
Chetwynd trips to the glory of the Hyams school, where
she was formerly a pupil teacher. But there can be no
question as to which is “ the more versatile artist.” Mrs.
Green’s daughter has been an accomplished performer in
public since she was a kiddie.
Cora Urquhart Brown-Potter appeared at the London
Bankruptcy Court last month ; this being the last act of
a sort of tragedy. Mrs. Potter bore up well till she was
asked why she had removed from Bray Lodge two little
Shetland ponies which ought to have been sold under the
bill of sale. Here she broke down, and sobbed bitterly,
I—l was very fond of those two little ponies ! ” Later
in the case she stated, in reply to a question, that the
most successful season she ever had was in ’97 in Aus-
tralia, when she made £9OOO. Her income since had been
about £5OOO per annum.
“S.S.”: “His Majesty’s Servant” at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s is the sort of play that turns the democratic stomach
and justifies the growing contempt for the stage and the
stage-player. In it everything that served for England
and the people against the Stuarts in the great Civil War
is made gross and venal. The piety of the Roundheads is
an abomination ; they are represented as hypocrites,
thieves, curs, and abusers of women. John Lambert,
hero of many a tough battle, is a despicable craven, and
Monk alone is allowed a rag of decency, because he it was
who made the re appearance of that prinked ape, Charles
11., possible in England. Meanwhile, there is comfort in
hearing women complaining that men are deserting the
theatre as they have deserted the ballroom. Such plays
as “ His Majesty’s Worm ” do not attract real men.
“Synic”: The management’s faith in Julius Knight as
a popular “draw” explains the production of “His
Majesty’s Servant,” the weakest and silliest of melodramas
that pretend to be high-class. The consistent unreality
of an Andersonian “ shocker ” is almost preferable to the
foolishness of the aforesaid costume play, written by two
women. A Melbourne girl, who has a romantic affection
for Walter Scott’s novels, suggests that if a. story
of Charles the Second’s early days was wanted the
authoresses of “ His Majesty’s Servant ” might have con-
structed a better play from “ Woodstock.” But
Scott’s yarn has presumably been already dramatised
more than once.

“0.K.” : Watching the Melbourne “Players” at work
upon “ Dandy Dick,” t’other night, one was disposed to
complain not so much of the want of ease in the ama-
teurs’mumming as of its general triviality. They give
so little weight to their characterisations. ’ The best per-
formance—that of the Dean, by a Mr. Hardinge—was
“ thin ” and youthful. Hardinge is an intelligent actor,
and versatile as amateurs go. He played the cockney
sailor, in “ The French Maid,” with abandon, and he got
some clever touches of clerical color into his picture of
the Dean. But he didn’t make himself nearly as old and
pompous as he should have been. The dignitary of 55, or
so, looked not more than 30, in spite of his grey hair,
whilst his widowed sister, the horsey Georgina (played by
Miss Lydgate), had the manners of a girl of about 22. A
similar lack of deliberation in everybody’s speech and
action accounted for the performance falling fully twenty
minutes short of ordinary professional time. Measured
by the clock an amateur performance never goes slow, if
it has been carefully rehearsed. It lacks the weight that
would steady it.
Lempriere Pringle has lately been doing Mephistopheles,
in scenes from the “ Faust ” opera, at London Coliseum,
and the work of that imposing basso gets particular praise!
Tasmania produced two high-class singers in Amy Sherwin
(who went to London years before Melba) and Lempriere
Pringle, but the little island seems to have exhausted
its, vocal strength.
The resemblance in face, figure and style between the
returned Maggie Stirling and Lady Hopetoun is mostly
remarked by people who never met the songstress on the
platform before she went to Europe. The resemblance
now is not as striking as it was of yore. At one time,
Melbourne had two duplicates of Lady Hopetoun appear-
ing in public—Miss Maggie Stirling and Miss Jennings
Carmichael, v. ,o wrote pretty little verses for the Aus-
tralasian. It was hard to say which of them looked the
more like a twin-sister of the vice-regal lady. Jennings
Carmichael took the platform on two or three occasions
as a lecturess on Australian Poetry or “ The Spirit of the
Bush,” or something, and spoke her yarn gracefully in a
sweet voice—the sympathetic sick-room voice of a pro-
fessional nurse. Then she went to England and died, as
nice ex-nurses are too apt to do.

former.
A lot of Scawttish cautiousness is practised by the audi-
ences at Jessie Maclaehlan’s concerts. Sandy, in the first
instance, samples the auld songs from a shilling seat, and
if favorably impressed he pays another bawbee and moves
forward. Or two of Sandy take a twa shilling seat
between them, and No. 1 retires at half-time in favor of
No, 2, who comes along on his brither’s pass-out check.
They have a long and earnest dispute as to how the two
halves of the entertainment shall be allotted, and if both
favor the first part they toss up for best innings—best 6
out of 10.
Jones, a nigger corner-man with Frank Clark’s show,
sing-s a song called “ I’ve got a white man working for
me.” It tells of a nigger who pays a white man two
dollars to shovel a load of coal into a cellar, and as the
white man toils the nigger sits on a fence and sings, jeer-
ingly, “I’ve got a white man working for me.” °ln the
second verse the white man gets annoyed, and throws
the nigger and the black audience that has collected
round him down into the coal-cellar, and the bruised coon
has a white man working for him no longer.
On a recent Monday night (Sept. 26th) “ The Cingalee ”
was nearly broken in two at Her Majesty’s Theatre, Mel-
bourne, by a tack that stood on the stage with the busi-
ness end upwards. At that point where Margaret Thomas
plants a soft, convincing kiss on the mouth of Haigh Jack-
son, and the long baritone should assume a look of idiotic
happiness, his features suddenly twisted in agony. The
audience wondered what Margaret had been eatino-, till
Jackson stooped and with a mouth working like a valve
pulled out of the sole of his boot and threw to the ground
something that fell with the tap of steel. Then Jackson
walked out on one heel and one toe, without waiting to
kiss or sing any more. It was fortunate that the tack
was not discovered by one of the four Cingalee maidens
who sit down with such a heart-stirring thump on the
floor.
“Edyson”: Am just recovering from that stunned
feeling after a visit to “The Cingalee.” Nothing in the
merit or demerit of the performance produced the shock,
but when the play was nearly over I suddenly recollected
that somebody had been awarded £4OOO damages or
something, because somebody else had borrowed or
conveyed the amazing original features of his comic
opera and built “The Cingalee” out of them. The fact
got me down. Why, I wonder in a dazed way, am I
writing comparatively difficult stuff, and selling it for
shillings, while it is possible to have second and third
hand matter stolen from one at such enormous profits. I
write to say I’ll provide thousands of similar ideas, and
go halves in the damages with the collaborator who will
provide the thief. Consider “The Cingalee” opening in a
tea garden, as “ Florodora ” did in a flower garden. Lauri
is chasing a little lost princess in “The Cingalee ” ;he was
chasing a little lost heiress in “ Florodora.” As a Chow
in “San To3',” Lauri steals things, and makes precisely
the same comic excuses as he does when he steals things
as the cocoa-colored humorist in “The Cingalee.” In
“ San Toy ” the heroine is borne away to a nabob’s palace ;
the same thing happens in “The Cingalee.” The
‘ Prapses” happen in “Florodora”; the “ Golly wogs”
happen in “ The Cingalee.” Where is the £4OOO worth of
material, that the builders of “The Cingalee” cribbed
from the niilitafy gentleman? The value of the thin«-
must lie in the 'fact that the brown girls dress in tight
white bed-gowns, and frequently assume a bowling posi-
tion with their backs to the audience. This is considered
a successful feature and meets with genera] approval, and
yet when Nance O’Neil merely turned her broad back on
the public how the experts yelled 1 I give it up.

“His Majesty’s Servant” lets loose a Swinburnian re-
miniscence—" I’ll fight for the lilies of virtue, or die for
the roses of fame.” The ladylike authors evidently
thought Swinburne’s “ lilies and languors of virtue ” good
enough to annex, but shuddered at the thought of an
audience of the admirers of Julius Knight’s legs being
asked to contemplate “ the raptures and roses of vice.”
“Melb.”: Stepping into a Victoria Parade tram, on
Sunday night, I met Fifine. Not wishing to glance rudely
at a strang-er, as I thought, I yet was conscious of the
presence of a most beautiful young lady, something
Parisienne, dressed in a salmon-lined fabric or confection
of perfect taste. Fifine ! Ten years younger than she was
10 years ago. All this after a stage career of the arduous
kind. As a girl, you were plain, Fifine, and knew it.
You determined to win by talent. So you warbled
your path into our hearts, and acted with the height of
chic. But by what transformation have you entered
the ranks of Beauty ? Here you are undeniably beautiful,
after conquering the London stage as well as the Austra-
lian, and even coruscating as the Belle of Drury under as
much calcium focus as ever George Rex plunged into.
Zangwill’s “Merely Mal-y Ann,” the new play of the
Tittell Brune co., is another claimant for the tear-pro-
ducing- record, long in dispute between “Camille’’and
“ East Lynne.” It was the howling success of the season
in the Big Smoke.
Eleanora Duse, who lately gave her farewell perfor-
mance in London, is one of the few actresses who have
played Juliet at 14, at which age Shakspeare places his
heroine, though it is usually turned to 16 in the per-
formance. Duse, child and grandchild of players, was
quite a stage veteran at 14, as she had acted with a
strolling company from a very early age. Her youthful
Juliet was acted in Verona, the city of the play, with
striking success. Duse is now 44.
Howard Vernon first appeared as a professional at
Ballarat (Vic.), at the age of 18, as Eglantine Roseleaf, in
the old farce “ Turn Him Out.” His first operatic essay
was made with Madame Simonsen’s co. at Sydney in Feb.,
1872 (he played Baron Puck in “ The Grand Duchess ”).
His Melbourne debut (King Bobeche in “Bluebeard”)
was in 1873, at the Opera House, under W. S. Lyster’s
management.

“T.D.”: Have had opportunities lately of seeing
exactly what sort of chimera is being chased by the
average Australian musician who goes to England. An
orchestra with which I sang last week at a fashionable
seaside resort included among its members one of the
world’s very best known ’cellists. The accompanist, who
was also a member of the orchestra, is ordinarily con-
ductor of one of the greatest Continental opera houses.
The double bass player was principal scraper of that
unweildy instrument at Drury Lane ; and the first violin
is known throughout Germany as leader of a big orchestra
there. Salaries averaged £3 a week. Of course they
were only filling in time during the international off-
season, but what would Hattenbach, Allpress and Co. say
to playing three programmes a day—six hours work,
seven days a week—for three obscure sovereigns.
Some more dramas suited to Anderson have been pro-
duced in London lately. One of them rejoices in a festive
heroine known as the Tiger Lily, whose mother (a circus
lady) was bitten by a tiger shortly before the birth of her
offspring. Therefore, the Lily is addicted to tigerish
instincts when the moon is at its full, and she goes around
clawing and scratching her acquaintances with all the
ferocity of the animal that bit mother. In another
current bellowdrama the principal man is a vampire, with
a countenance like unto the “ Face in the Coal-Scuttle.”
Every time the Vampire wants a new lease of life he kills
a pure young maiden and sucks her blood. Another male
character is about to commit suicide when he meets an
evening-dressed toff who wears a red mask, and proposes
a game of cards to decide which of them shall jump over
a cliff. The toff, losing the game, carries out iiis contract
faithfully, but before tumbling off the cliff he gives the
red mask to the other man and bids him wear it until
death, and the other fellow masks himself accordingly.
The grotesque horrors that delight Cockney audiences
appear to be going from awful to worse. Australia, by
the grace of Anderson, is bound to get the pick of these
shocks, so there’s a time coming when the “ Countenance
at the Ash-barrel ” and the “ Galvanised Corpse ” will be
remembered as insipid suggestions of future enormities.
“ S.S.”: To my mind, and speaking as a mere man, the
best-dressed woman on the Australian stage just now is
Harrie Ireland, of Blandolt’s co. The tall, auburn native
carries a frock to perfection, and her dresses are devised
with wonderful taste and artistic discernment. I dare
say there are several actresses in Australia who spend
twice as much on their wardrobes, but there is not one
who gives as much satisfaction. H.l.’s clothes are artfully
composed, they belong to her alone, and in color, forni,
and fit deserve the prize every time.
“ T.D.”: Went round to London Oxford Hall to hear
the Fitzstubbs twins, from Sydney, do their first turn on
the English boards, and sustained a great shock in an
incidental way. Programme gave out that somebody
named Kopski was going to do something, and presently
an apparition manifested itself in verification of the
announcement. It was suave, clean-shaven, dress-suited,
and carried a head of hair modelled on the Liszt pattern,
and it made some remarks to the audience about classical
music, after which it sat down at a grand piano and was
joined by the skimpy orchestra in a Mendelssohn
concerto. I was puzzled as to the identity of the
apparition. It seemed familiar, yet I knew not Kopski.
At last the truth flashed upon me. It was David Cope,
formerly a Sydney pianist, who has apparently found
someone’s hair restorer extremely efficacious. David
seems to have struck petroleum.
Travelling Australians, who are used to the complete-
ness with which grand opera is staged in Sydney and
Melbourne, when it is staged at all, ' are knocked over
when they first hear the older operas at Covent Garden
and some other great European houses. During the
recent Covent Garden season the recitative portions of
such old favorites as “ Don. Giovanni,” “Don Pasquale,”
and “ II Barbiere ” were accompanied, not by the orches-
tra, but by a tinkling piano somewhere in the win«s.
Even when Caruso sang, his voice was supported merely
by a chord here and there, and after an act or two the
persistent “tum-tum,” always on the same couple of
chords, was extremely irritating.

communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

LOST OPPORTUNITIES.
[For The Bbueti n.]
I might have kissed you, my sweet, m.v sweet,
On that bright, starlit night in the olden time.
In “Lovers’ Retreat ” in that golden time,
I might have kissed you—’tvvas meet, ’twas meet,
For roguishly smiling, with eyes a-dance,
You dared mo to do it; I missed my chance !
I might have lost you if I had been
Content with the answer you gave me first—
The positive “ No ! ” in decisive burst ;
Yes, then had I lost you, my queen, my queen !
Again I besought you. With downcast glance,
You answered me “ Yes ” ; I had missed my chance
I might have died and been laid to rest,
When I was so ill in the Spring last year.
But still I recovered, my dear, my dear,
For I thought that your heart would be grief opprest.
Ah, folly of mine to quit that trance !
I might have been buried ! I missed my chance !
MAORILAND. SINCLAIR S. CLARK.
* * * *
The holy calm of the Methodist Church at
West Devonport (Tas.) was disturbed during a
recent week-night service by the apparition of a
male person who had nodings on, save a handker-
chief tied around his head. For the rest of him,
he was as bare as Adam before the Drop. The
fleshly visitant entered at the main doorway of
the tabernacle, bowed to the officiating shepherd,
and petered into the outer- darkness. The choir
was singing a hymn at the time, and the lady
warblers, it seems, had been gazing at the appari-
tion with doubting eyes until the shepherd
remarked to the leading basso, “That man is
naked,’ whereupon the basso went in vain pursuit
of the retreating form. Worshippers facing the
pulpit saw nothing of the awful thing, but they
knew by the blushes of the girl choristers that an
object of shocking interest must have put in an
appearance. In the event of the nude person re-
peating his offence, he will find the eyes of the
whole congregation on the look out for him.
IN THE STREET.
EXCITED ORATOR: “If Carruthers ’as got any
backbone ” (with emphasis) “ why don’t 'e
bring it to the front?"
The dredge Pioneer, which Premier Bent
imported from England into Victoria, turns out a
sorry joke. It was imported on the ground that
it couldn’t be made in Australia, and when it was
proved that similar dredges had already been
made in Australia, Bent was a shocked man. Also
it was imported to serve as a model for local
builders, and when he was confronted with the
fact that the plans had been drawn up here and
sent to England for the instruction of the English
builders, Bent was a confused and angry man.
Anyhow the shoddy English concern was im-
ported at great expense, and ever since it arrived
it has been under repair to make it workable, till
at last an impression begins to gain ground that
the best thing to do is to pull the bad bargain to
pieces and rebuild.
After many struggles and delays, and after
throwing up the inquiry and then coming back to
it, and after numerous stipulations about protec-
tion, Parson Woolls Rutledge gave what he called
evidence before the Select Committee in support
of his tale about a discharged N.S.W. civil servant
who couldn’t get back to the service till he turned
R.C., and who, on turning R.C., was imme-
diately re-appointed at about £lOOO a year. The
evidence was, after all, hardly worth the trouble
involved in extracting it. All that bore even
indirectly on the case was embodied in seven
umouncements:—
(1) “ Many years ago statements came to me,” &c.
(2) “ On one occasion I was informed,” &c.
(3) “Several persons spoke to me,” &c.
(4) “ One gentleman particularly was very indignant,”
&c.
(5) “ On several other occasions persons spoke to me,”
&c.
(6) “ One gentleman spoke to me seven years ago,” &c.
(7) “ A few years ago a gentleman told me,” &c.
There is a great deal of the tattling old woman
about the parsonical character. Except in one
case, Woolls Rutledge neither said who told him
nor what he really was told ; he seems to have
lived in a mist of suspicion and hysterics, like
Mrs. Snagsby, in “ Bleak House,” who had fits of
violent jealousy among the cockroaches re the
“goings on ”of parties unknown. A cold business
man would have asked for the statements in
writing ; then he would have looked up the Public
Service List to see whether the alleged convert was
really engaged at the magnificent salary alleged ;
and he would have sent an intelligent individual
to find out, at a cost of perhaps 7s. 6d., about
what date Hall began to frequent the R.C. church,
and he would have compared that date with the
date of the re-appointment. But the unbusiness-
like parson, living in an atmosphere of old women
and tea and buns, seldom does things in that
fashion. He holds up his ear like a vast paper
bag for the collection of rumors, and when his ear
is full he slops over. He hears the vague suspicions
of the old lady with her face tied up in a red cloth,
and the shadowy apprehensions of the partially-
deaf man who thinks he read somewhere that the
Pope is a quadruped, till he becomes like a large,
foolish coal-scuttle, filled with vague reports about
nothing in particular. The habit of “ visiting the
flock ” and sympathising with their corns gets him
down. Probably the best idea would be to hand
the whole business over co women, and to pass an
Act indemnifying the clerical profession in case it
says anything libellous when it has hysterics on
top of the Book of Deuteronomy.
* * * *
Sub-inspector Dungey, of Melbourne, has
made the authorities there very angry by a brief
report in which he alleges that the city police are
either raw recruits or drunks, and the force has
almost torn its own uniform in its haste to
repudiate the insinuation.
* * * *
“The wickedest people in the world are
in Sydney,” according to bankrupt Moses Cohen.
Moses Cohen is himself in Sydney, by the way,
having been brought back from Auckland on a
charge of fleeing from his creditors ; so is Becky
Cohen, who was also brought back from Auckland,
and who had a large amount of money sewn up in
her singlet; and the city is also blessed with the pre-
sence of Solomon Cohen, who didn’t leave with
father Moses and sister Becky, but stayed behind
and gave them away to the creditors. Minnie
Cohen, however, is not on the premises at present.
* * * *
In olden days, when troops were raised by
feudal lords from their family retainers, it was
only natural that the men should be clothed in the
livery and carry the crest of the baron,
whose servants they practically were. Now
regiments are not raised by county magnates,
and things are different. But Governor Tal-
bottle, of Victoria, went bustling down to
Hamilton, the other day, to congratulate the
members of the 11th A.L.H. Regiment on
the fact that they wear the crest of his
Excellency’s livery buttons! Fancy the
Hamilton trooper of the A.L.H. bursting with
pride at this honorable distinction, shared with
Talbottle’s flunkeys.
* * * *
Bairnsdale (Vic.) is just now pondering
the case of a degenerate of an unusual
class. The damsel is 15, but “ very strong
and well-developed for her age.” Qn a recent
Saturday afternoon she took it into her head
to seize a small girl of six, bundle her into
the Roman Catholic Church in Main-street, lock
the doors, and then wallop her severely for no
apparent reason—the two being entire strangers.
She seems to have been a calm and
methodical lunatic, for she first removed the
child’s clothing, then lambasted her strenuously
with a strap, standing on her neck the mean-
while, so that the frequent passers-by should
not be disturbed by unseemly outcries.
Having thoroughly chastised the infant, the
damsel knocked loose one of her teeth, pulled it
out, and put it in the collection-box. She next
tried to soak the victim in the vessel containing
the holy water, but, finding this too small, she
dragged the unfortunate baby to an adjacent
horse-trough and soused her emphatically. Then
she cautioned the child to hold her peace, and
departed. The damsel seems a fit mate for that
crazy Cheltenham (Vic.) youth, of similar age,
who enticed a small boy from Melbourne into the
bush and practised an exactly similar piece of
liendishness on him, a couple of years ago. He
was adjudged temporarily insane, was speedily
released, and was soon after convicted of an
attempt at an even worse horror.
“T.D.”: Boothism is such a decadent
affair in its birthplace now that, after twelve
months of London, I can count on my fingers
the number of times I have heard its voci-
ferous “captings” raving and its well-drilled
bands a work. The Salvarmy is too beastly
respectable especially when one harks back to
the days when as a timid kiddie, he was
afraid of being taken through the streets of
Fog town by night because of the frequent skir-
mishes between the Boothites and the long-
vanished “Skeleton Army” which warred
upon them. The “ Army” has big,
respectable head-quarters in Queen
Victoria-street, opposite the shop
where John Taverner exhibits Vic-
torian peaches and jam, and plaster-
of-Paris nuggets. Otherwise it has
mostly deserted its back lane for a
snug little chapel that
once belonged to the
Wesleyans, or the
Baptists, or the Dir-
tites, or the Never-
Washers, or other
creeds too numer-
ous to particu-

as he used to do.
A North Queensland court found itself
tied up in hard knots the other day. It had a
solicitor before it, charged with murder. Late in
the proceedings one of the parties wanted some
depositions put in as evidence, and the police
magistrate, who had been hearing the case, got off
the bench, on summons, to put them in. The
C.P.S. took the P. M.’s place on the bench, and
then ruled that the P.M. couldn’t put in the
depositions because he (the 0.P.5.), and not the
P.M., was the custodian of the papers. The
P.M. couldn’t argue with the C.P.S., the
C.P.S. being for the moment the boss, so
the P.M. got out of the witness-box and
went on to the bench again. Then the
C.P.S. got off the bench and went into
the witness-box, and tendered the depositions.
The P.M. said that he couldn’t over-rule the pre-
vious bench’s decision, but as the C.P.S. hadn’t
seen the depositions signed he couldn’t swear to
anything, so he (the P.M.) couldn’t let the depo-
sitions go in. Just about this time one of the
parties drew attention to the fact that the P.M.
couldn’t be both a witness and a judge in the
same case, and long arguments were threatened
on that subject, when the P.M. said he reckoned
it didn’t matter anyhow—he couldn’t think of
committing the man on the evidence. Then the
crowd cheered, the constable called for silence,
and the P.M. and the C.P.S. went into their
offices to sort themselves out.

“ 5.A.0.” : I have just been inspecting a
large printery where women compositors have
taken the place of men. The tendency of the
times seems to be that the man can’t marry
because the woman has taken his job, and the
woman can’t marry because she is too busy on the
job she has taken. Moreover, she can’t marry
because she doesn’t earn enough to keep the man,
and, in any case, the man has degenerated through
lack of employment and isn't worth marrying.
THE EXCUSE.
TEACHER : “ This doesn't look like your father's
writing, Tommy.”
TOMMY : “ Well, it’s as near as I could get to it.”
George William Carveth, an ex-naval man
who has struck hard luck at Owaki (M.L.), is
such a curious case that it is hard to say whether
he is a martyr in asm all way or otherwise. Any-
how he looks like one. In 1848 he arrived in a
gun-brig at the then perfectly new and raw settle-
ment of Maoriland, and met a girl of 16, whom he
didn’t marry, though he lived with her for a year.
Then he went away to sea, but in 1859 he met the
same girl in Sydney (she was then aged 27), and
the old love revived, and again they lived toge-
ther in peace and happiness. Later he was called
away to America, but he returned and met his
girl again at Nelson, in 1863 (she was a girl of 31
by that time), and the flower of affection bloomed
some more in the garden of life, and they lived
together for a while—and parted. When he re-
turned from China he found her living with
Mr. Brown, so he married Mrs. Nesbit.
The years rolled on. His wife died at
last, and Brown died at last, and almost
the last words of Brown were a request that
he would look after his old love (now a girl of
about 70). So the blossom of true love once more
asserted itself. By this time defendant was past
his best hustling days and- very poor, but he
bought a cart and the skeleton of a horse for 305.,
and took the old lady out for drives on fine days.
Then she fell sick, and they couldn’t well eat the
horse, so he applied for an old age pension on her
behalf, and to help her to get it swore that she
was his wife —which she wasn’t. And now the
old battler is doing 12 months.
* * * *
An unemployed man reported an inter-
view with Queensland’s Treasurer recently to
some mates by paraphrasing Laurence Hope :
The Rev. Percy Nall, son of a well-known
Sydney journalist, arid a missionary in India, has
returned with a very poor account of Bengal
under British rule. The people of Eastern
Bengal, he says, are generally indifferent to
Christianity, because Western science and Western
worship of Mammon have got hold of them. Also
the Hindu—probably inspired by the example of
the Japs—is talking now of a natiorial religion,
and wants no foreign gods or goods; in some
places there is a boycott, not only of the British
missionary, but of British manufactures. In-
temperance and the use of opium are on the in-
crease, and the license of the native press is mis-
chievous. Altogether it is not a very cheering
picture, either from the missionary or the “ Im-
perial ” standpoint.

MICRO FIPP.
I wait; I question ; Kidston heeds me not;
He does but urge in answer to my prayer.
“ Arise and do ! ” Alas, he adds not what;
“ Arise and go ! ” Alas, he says not where.

N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade finds it
hard to forget his halfback and three-quarterback
exploits in football, and thus breaks out:
It was the experience of other nations that the boys
who were bookworms did not make the practical men
who carried on the true work of a country in the battle
of life.
But the best footballer may become a lawyer, and
what then ? The lawyer class does not carry on
true work ; it is the mere hireling of the disputa-
tious, ready to argue oh either side of the case.
Max Muller and Herbert Spencer were book-
worms.
* * * *
When a man wantonly addresses another
in opprobrious terms and that other feels impelled
to knock him down, but reflecting that the law
says
Whosoever in any public street, thoroughfare, or place
uses any threatening, abusive, or insulting words, or
behaviour with intent to provoke a breach of the peace,
or whereby a breach of the peace may be occasioned,
shall, on conviction, etc.
he determines to set a good example and appeals
to the nearest police court, how does he fare ?
Badly, almost ignominiously. N.S.W. Banco
Court (Darley, Cohen and Bring) backs up the
Full Courts of Victoria and Queensland in deciding
that the offensive person must actually cause a
breach of the peace, or must be proved to have had
the intention of causing it, before a conviction can
follow. The inference appears to be that the
abused person can only get the law on his side by
first knocking down the offender to show that his
language caused a breach of the peace.
* * * »
The High Court came to a great beer
decision last week. Some “dandelion beer”—
a temperance drink —was found on analysis by the
excise officers to contain more than 2 per cent, of
alcohol, the maximum allowed by the law to cheer
the heart of the teetotaller. Defendant pleaded
that, as made, his dandelion beer had nothing like
that amount of bite in it, but after it was bottled
a secondary fermentation had produced extra
alcohol, and the High Court held him blameless.
It is a, glad verdict for the prohibitionist, who will
now, probably, be able to get fairly “ forward” on
his beverage since secondary fermentation is a
thing that may happen to anybody. And if
it doesn’t happen, it may be arranged. In
the case under review there was no arrangement,
and the presence of the extra alcohol was purely
accidental. But if the accident doesn’t happen often
enough it will probably be hurried up by bad
teetotallers.
One William Cribb astonished the Brisbane
Y.M.C.A., the other day, by conditionally hand-
ing to that body a Gfeorge-street block worth
£25,000. Cribb, now pretty old, has only of late
years become religiously-inclined. A condition of
the gift is that the Y.M.C.A. pays the donor or his
wife an annuity of £4OO a year, which, at present
rental, will leave a balance of £4OO to the good
Young Men. This donation is the richest that
has yet fallen to any holy organisation in Queens-
land. The Y.M.C.A.is nowthrowingchests all over
the place, and is going to incorporate itself by Act
of Parliament, and already it talks airily of
closing the hotel on the corner when the lease
f ills ip, and of many things.
