A Melbourne bench got some information
lately as to the hours of oyster-saloon waiters,
when a couple of ex-cOuntrymen of the late
lamented Xenophon were shown to have been
worked, one 76 and the other 89 hours per week.
Eight hours’ sleep, 15 hours’ work, and one hour
for meals, toilet, recreation, amusement, etc., etc. !
Even the honoraries were shocked enough to
speak severely to the Greek sweater, and Demetrius
Phriedphysh was fined £5 altogether.
The scene at N.S.W. Lands Commission
when Peter Close confessed his master (whether
Peter spoke the truth or not has yet to be shown)
was dingily impressive. There was none of the
usual adornment of the legal tragedy—no coat of
arms, no j udicial throne, no wig of horse-hair, no
uniformed tip-staffs, criers nor police; just a
dusty room with a statuette of some forgotten
celebrity in an advanced state of grime, a
table or two, a judge looking stern and
sorry in his ordinary every-day habiliments, and a
big handsome witness seated uneasily in a chair
making statements which, if true, blighted for
ever his own character and that of W.P. Crick,
ex-Minister for Lands. A Court of Justice
showed that it could be awful without the frippery
that is defended on the ground of the necessity to
impress the vulgar, for when the reluctant witness
first mumbled “Mr. Crick,” a cold stillness, elo-
quent of much, settled on the dusty little room,
with its small group of listeners. Mr. Close was
asked as to the fate of a certain fee he had re-
ceived. Half, he said, he had devoted to his own
use. “ And the other half ? ” asked Mr. Pilcher,
suavely. “You put me in a most painful posi-
tion,” objected the witness. “And the other
half?” asked Mr. Pilcher. “That went to the
Minister.” “To whom?” “To the Minister,
Mr. Crick.” Thus the Lands Inquiry entered on
its final stage.
* * * *
The art-union prize-money which “ fly-
flat ” Naughton refunded to St. Vincent’s Hospital
management will be distributed among all the
general hospitals of Victoria. This decision shuts
the mouths of numerous vociferous sectarians who
protested that their little bits in the art-union
gamble were intended to benefit St. Vincent’s
Hospital only; and they wouldn't “agree” to
any general distribution of the cash that Naughton
anted up. It didn’t occur to the owlish dissentients
that St. Vincent’s had already got the whole of
the legitimate profit of its unfortunate scheme.
The money paid away in prizes was gone for ever,
as far as the hospital was concerned, so the fact
that Naughton had handed over to the police a
portion of the fraudulently-obtained £5OO didn’t
give St. Vincent’s a just claim to it. From first
to last the detective in charge of the case treated
the hospital as an injured party, whereas the
punting public who got no chance of a prize for
their money were the injured ones. Naughton
also was taken in. Instead of the light sentence
he expected when the’tec bluffed him into relin-
quishing the boodle he received five years with
tough labor.
* * * *
There are limits to the resisting power of
even a whale. The Ortona ran against one the
other day and cut it in two, greatly to the
disgust of old members of the local fish population
no doubt. There was a time when a whale could
safely bump the biggest ship that floated, but he
can’t do it now. The ocean is going to the devil,
in a Tory way of speaking. Whales are no longer
respected.
* * * *
A document of great historic interest in
Australia—one which the original owners pre-
sented to Sydney Art Gallery some years ago—has
disappeared. It is the requisition written by
John Macarthur, and signed by 134 other settlers,
asking Colonel Johnston to depose Governor
Bligh and assume the reins of government. It
was in a glass case with other Johnstonian relics.
Relics have strange wandering habits. A very
early volume of the Sydney Gazette disappeared
from the Free Public Library some time ago, and
the offer of £5 reward has failed to bring it back.
Sydney Morning Herald prints without
a gasp a suggestion from a Mr. Chas. W. D.
Goodchap that Australia, “to mark its gratitude
to the Royal House of Brunswick” (presumably
for having looked after the blessed succession so
well that our eyes were recently delighted by the
si gilt of the Yorklet), should change its name to
“Brunswick,” and should call its Federal capital
“ Guelph.” It is added that whilst he was Prime
Minister, G. H. Reid promised to consider this bit
of crawl.

Our peaceful colored brother has been
making a good-sized Sunday row at Lismore
(N.S.W.). The story of the fight and the bad
language was related in Court by Fikiria,
Bouckallil, Milki Ram, Muddy Ram, Ram
Kishan, and Lubbo Tally, and was a fine example
of hard swearing.

This year the Adelaide prison authorities
have been investigating the effects of drinking
cheap wine upon all persons locked up for
drunkenness. During three months covered by
the inquiry 50 per cent, of the mm and 70 per
cent, of the women “drunks” ascribed their fall
to “ pinky.”
In preparing the next edition of his
pamphlet favoring the dumping of Chows in Aus-
tralia, bookseller Cole ought to consider a Mel-
bourne love episode. A yellow exemplifier of all
the virtues ( vide pamphlet), after helping to
develop this country’s great natural resources by
fossicking for gold at Bendigo, came to Melbourne
and paid court to a white damsel. When the lady
gave him the cold shoulder he tried persuasion, on
two occasions, by throwing corrosive acid over her.
The tractable and industrious immigrant showed
great surprise when a policeman arrested him
while he was figuring out how much more acid it
would take to soften the heart of his adored one.
Many of our lovable visitors from the land of the
stinkpot fail to see much harm in flinging corrosive
chemicals about tne landscape, and the peaceable
aliens who rioted in Little Bourke-street a while
back varied the device by throwing powdered
glass and red pepper in each other’s eyes.

The earnest Victorian agriculturist who
goes to Melbourne once a year at show-time makes
some humorous mistakes. One
of h i.m, with a rather larger
out break of whiskers than usual,
got into the cabmen’s shelter
in Carpentaria-place with the
idea that it was a tram. The
Jehu-edifice remained stolidly
where it was, but the bushy
waited on with the calm ex-
pression of a mountain waiting
for Eternity. A cabman drove
up in about three-quarters of
an hour, and persuaded Bill to
climb into his cab, as the other
vehicle was hung up for an ex-
tended course of repairs. Bill
climbed, and his whiskers
streamed proudly down Collins-
street a few seconds after, the
cab not being large enough to con-
tain them all at once. It’s an ill
mistake that doesn’t blow somebody
a fare.
Sydney Wharf Laborers’ Union used to
insist on preference to unionists on the wharves,
and framed its rules to drastically limit the
number of its members. The daily papers were
shocked at this “union tyranny,’’and the high
Protection that the wharf laborers had built
around their industry was gradually broken down.
Now investigation by the Arbitration Court shows
that the average wharf laborer cannot earn a
living wage, not so much because he is paid at too
low a rate, but because he cannot get enough
work to do. There are 1250 union men to do
work which would only suffice to keep 500 in full
employment. This, in a way, justifies the old
policy of the union which the Tory press so
bitterly denounced ; and now that same press
has the impudence to scold the Wharf Laborers’
Union for not “ better arranging the work.’’

After all, the Dill-Macky campaign does
not bite deeply into the lives of the people. The
other day, in a Vic. country town, a young woman
nearly sacrificed her life trying to rescue a boy
from drowning. And she was a strong R.C. and
the boy a son of a strong “ Orange ” family ; and
between the two families there had been a good
deal of sectarian bitterness.

An obscure item on Tasmania’s estimates
serves to show up the-British navy and its ways.
The annual cost of the occasional descent of the
warships, when the summer girl congregates at
Hobart, used to be set down as £445. Included
in this was a yearly charge of £7O for breakages.
Whether this extraordinary mortality among the
crockery was due to the light frivolity of the
summer girls is not stated, but somebody thought
that to debit one-sixth of the whole charge to
broken glass and china was too strong, and dif-
ferent arrangements were made. The Admiral
now pays for his own breakages, and the break-
ages are said to have got down to about £2 a year.
Which suggests that the summer girl doesn’t dance
on the best tea-set now.

SAID WORSE.

Mournful to consider the ideas that grow
up with the Freetrade sentiment. A Mr. H. Bay
writes to Sydney D.T. as to “what shall we do
with our boys ? ”
We consequently have placed upon us the positive duty
of so directing the industrial life of the State that oppor-
tunities for the youth of the community to earn some
contribution to the maintenance of their home shall not
be unduly restricted, and in no way can this be more
legitimately done than in the distributing houses—
drapery emporiums, ironmongery establishments, and the
concerns which adopt the title of universal providers, and
others whose business entails the handling of large quanti-
ties of merchandise covering innumerable small items and
great variety of goods, and necessitating the division of
the business into numerous departments.
A noble ideal. Make ribbon-sellers of our boys !

THE SURVIVAL OF THE FITTEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I see the wild things, at their ease, their stores and
cellars raid,
Whilst men escape not want nor toil till in the grave
they’re laid.
So full of good, wild Nature is, and Art so full of evil,
The one best fitted to survive, methinks, must be the
Devil.
VIC.
JEEAICH.
A SONG OF YOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Give me the blood-red wine,
For company ;
A song for my true love to sing
Alone to me.
Give me the night to revel;
By day I’ll rest.
Ive tried the garish day. It’s sin
Seems but a jest.
I want white-heated keen Desire,
Not vain Regret;
I want each perfumed breeze to say
“ He’s far from Sheol yet.”
I want ten purple hours
Ere the digger turns the sod ;
The eleventh is for Remorse,
And Prayer—and God.
GEORGE BELL.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Treasurer Carruthers has decided on a new way
of presenting N.S. W. revenue returns ; he keeps the
revenue from the business undertakings separate from
other receipts. “ Revenue proper ”he calls the latter,
which is by way of a joke, seeing that the total includes
proceeds of land sales, repayments made to the Advances
to Settlers Board, and rents from “Rocks” resumptions.
Proceeds of land sales are not revenue ; and why
Sydney Harbor Trust should be a “ business under-
taking ” while the Rocks resumptions are not, isn’t
explained. The division is arbitrary and absurd.
The Advances to Settlers business is as much a commer-
cial undertaking as the Sydney Harbor Trust ; and the
interest on country towns water supply and sewerage is as
much a receipt from a business undertaking as the re-
venue from the Metropolitan and Newcastle water and
sewerage systems. Oarruthers’ object is apparently to
crowd into his “ revenue proper ” list as many as possible
of the things that don’t call for any expenditure, and to
put into the “ business undertakings ” just those things
that call for heaviest expenditure ; so that presently
Joseph will be able to discard the “business undertak-
ings” altogether when comparing his own vast
qualities as an economical financier with the
“ extravagance ” of the Australian Government. The
Darling Harbor rents are “revenue proper”;
they will swell Joseph’s figures, and there won’t
be any expenditure to speak of on the other side. The
same with “country towns water supply—interest ” : this
will swell revenue, and there will be no expenditure
against it; it is merely a matter of collecting interest
from local councils. The same with the advances to
settlers repayments and interest. On “ revenue proper ”
J. Carruthers will thus be able to show a surplus of vast
proportions, available for interest and the assistance of
“ business undertakings.”
For all practical purposes this shuffling of figures
is worth nothing—probably less than nothing, because
Treasurer Carruthers will no doubt arrange matters so as
to reflect great glory upon himself, and that may help to
maintain this unsatisfactory person in office longer than
he might otherwise stay there. Further, it is possible
that, since Carruthers only includes in the “business
undertakings” those few works which give the best
return, he will use that return as a bait with which to
borrow more money for other “ business undertakings” ;
the vast amount spent in other things which are not in-
cluded in Carruthers’ “ business undertakings ” will be
pushed under the counter, or otherwise kept hidden.
But this one thing is certain : Carruthers and Carruthers’
officers can’t make one division when dealing with
revenue and another when dealing with capital
and loans. If they say that the railways and tram-
ways, Harbor Trust, and Sydney and Newcastle
water and sewerage services are the State’s only
business undertakings, then they will have to also reckon
those as the only permanent and reproductive works in
which Cohen’s money has gone ; and in that case the
frayed gag that practically the whole of Cohen’s money
is in “ permanent and reproductive works ” —that is, in
“business undertakings”—will have to be dropped. In
short, either Carruthers’ division of the public debt, or
his new division of the public revenue and expenditure,
is a hollow fraud. To a great extent they are probably
both frauds.
* * # *
John Jacob Schuh, manager for a firm with the
beautiful names of Schneider and Abrahams, lately gave
some interesting information to the Senate’s Select
Committee on the Tobacco Monopoly. He reckoned that
now the Tobacco Combine, or Trust, or Shark, or Octopus,
or whatever it should be called, manufactures about three-
fourths of the Australian output of cigarettes, and that
.Schneider and Abrahams produce the other fourth or
thereabouts. The rest of the manufacturers, he holds,
are hardly worth counting. Therefore if Schneider and
Abrahams are swallowed up the Combine will own the
whole business. The Combine supplies most of the
demand for machine-made cigarettes, while the
other firm is in the hand-made business, but as
the Combine has started to turn out hand-made
cigarettes also it evidently intends to annex everything - .
It considers that the cigarette business is its pie, and like
a great American pie-biter, it means to have that pie un-
less the Government intervenes. And it means to have
the tobacco and cigar pie also, likewise any 7 other pie it
sees lying around. According to John Jacob Schuh—
who, being in the business himself, has no love for a
Federal tobacco monopoly, and, therefore, isn’t likely to
encourage it by promising large profits—the Australian
Government could make about £48,500 a year more, by
running the cigar and cigarette manufacture as a Govern-
ment monopoly, than it makes out of the Customs and
excise on these articles. Probably if the Government
monopoly included tobacco, a profit of £lOO,OOO would
easily be made—perhaps more than that, for allowance
must be made for the fact that John Jacob Schuh wasn’t
trying to encourage a Government monopoly. That in-
come would pay off the cost of the transcontinental rail-
way to Port Darwin inside 20 years—perhaps in much less
time —and the transcontinental line would add a new
State to Australia. The idea has much to recommend it.
Australia doesn’t owe anything to the tobacco octopus
that it should be tender about treading on the eight legs
of the creature.

A table with a bearing on the Australian
financial situation:—
British National Debt. Average
Reduction Increase Price of
(millions). (millions). Consols.

1894 .. 5 .. .. 101

1903 .. .. 31 .. 90
1904 .. .. .. 88
The quotations down to 1901 are for 2f per cents. ; after
that for 2j per cents. ; but that does not affect
the movements. While the debt was being reduced
the price of the national stock went up rapidly,
and with hardly a break ; as soon as the reduction was
stopped and the debt began to mount again, the price
dropped just as rapidly and regularly as it had risen
before. , Some other causes were probably at work ; but
giving all that in, the figures are a striking proof of the
effect of a sinking fund on public credit, in 1897, while
Britain was steadily wiping off seven millions a year, it
could have borrowed for renewal purposes at 112 ; in 1904,
when the sinking fund had been dropped, and the debt
had for years been increased instead of diminished, the
same transaction could only have been carried through at
88 —on 24 per cent, worse terms, that is, than previously 7.
Australia, during the next 10 years, has loans amounting
to £66,077,000 falling dqe, which will have to be renewed ;
and if it doesn’t take the hint thrown at it by the British
figures (and Canadian figures throw out the same hint),
it will miss a. remarkably fine business chance. If a reso-
lute effort to establish a sinking fund out of revenue
(every other sort of sinking fund is a fraud) won’t appeal.
to some reformers as a matter of common honesty, it
might appeal to them as a good stroke of business.
* *. * *
There is again vague mention in Sydney of a
desirable bank amalgamation—the A.J.S. and the City,
with probably a separate Assets Realisation concern to
take over any inoperative accounts. A large saving in
expenses could be made, and possibly a large increase in
efficiency, if the suggestion were carried into effect. In
their present condition neither of them is sufficiently pro-
gressive to be much of. a credit to Australian banking ;
and, though the new deposits in the A.J.S. are amply
covered and more than covered by the whole of the securi-
ties, while the City’s deposi to are safeguarded by the bank’s
large capital, the whole banking position would look better
if the two concerns were put on a new basis. As for the
City, whose shares have suffered severely of late, the last
balance-sheet showed that it could write a further 33 per
cent, off all assets, other than coin, &c., out of share-
holders’ funds (capital and reserves). But it is just as
certain as anything can be that is based on published
figures, that no such writing-off is called for. If only the
small reserves and half the capital were written off, the
disclosed earnings would be equal to £6 Is. 2d. per cent,
on the other half, which is about the same as the Com-
mercial of Sydney’s earnings, and rather more than those
of most Australian banks. These shares are £8 paid ;
present market value is about 575. 6d. On the published
accounts, and assuming that the bank is writing-off
enough for current bad debts, the market has thus taken
quite a gloomy enough view of the shareholders’ position.
If something cannot be done in the way of amalgamation,
then the City’s position would be strengthened by facing
the situation and writing down values. The return
would then show up decently, and a deal of unnecessary
anxiety would not be felt —in fact, it wouldn’t be
suggested.
* * * *
The figures which follow, relating to the year’s
business of the principal Australian gas co.’s, are not
worth as much as such figures ought to be worth. The pro-
fits are shown net ; but it is not possible to say in most
cases whether a fair amount, and in some cases whether
any amount at all, has been allowed for depreciation.
Further, whereas some co.’s put their depreciation allow-
ance aside and call it a reserve fund, in which cases the
reserve is not a real reserve at all, others write the
amount off absolutely, and it disappears from the accounts
altogether. With a few exceptions there are no published
accounts worth less than those of gas co.’s, and no class
of security, as a whole, in which the investor is more in
the dark, assuming he can’t go further than the published
accounts for information. The result is some-
times the disaster that has happened to the
Rockhampton (Q.) concern, which gaily went ahead pay-
ing big dividends until part of the works was run to an
absolute standstill ; then, without any reserve to replace
the worn-out plant, a large part of the business neces-
sarily collapsed, and dividends collapsed with it. Such as
they are, here are the figures :

No other class of business generally in the hands of public
co.’s pays such large divs., on the whole, as this of gas
supply. Launceston gets down to 4 p.c., but that is
because of the cheap municipally-owned electric supply
with which gas has to compete. Gas co.’s generally scorn
the suggestion that they hold a monopoly. They quite
approve the competitive system, they say. And this 4
p.c. dividend is the result in almost the only case in which
there is any real competition. Otherwise gas is generally
a most profitable business. The Brisbane co. shows, for
the past half-year, a profit at the rate of £lO 11s. 6d. p.c.
p.a. on its entire assets. South Brisbane shows £9 15s. ;
Bendigo just upon £9 ; Goulburn, £8 16s. ; Brighton
(Vic.), £7 10s; Newcastle and North Shore, £7 ss. ; the
Melbourne co., after paying - heavy interest, £6 2s. Id. ;
the Sydney co. (Australian Gaslight) £6 Bs., after paying
interest on loans (including debentures) amounting to
£436,453 at June 30 last. Taking capital and reserves as
at the date of the last available balance-sheets, the profits
on shareholders’ funds made by the co.’s of the State
capitals were
Perth ..£lB 12 5 p.c. i Adelaide ..£9 3 7 p.c.
Brisbane .. 11 18 9 „ Sydney .. 8 310 „
Hobart .. 912 6 „ | Melbourne .. 619 10 „
Lighting is a public necessity ; and the public pays from
£7 up to nearly £l9 per cent, to companies for the supply
of it. Such figures are not calculated to make the public
more content with private ownership.

During - the year the co. has raised £30,000 on bonds,
though only £20,000 is shown in the present accounts.
The premiums on this issue., £B4, instead of being put to
reserve, are shown in profit and loss, and included in un-
divided profit in the balance-sheet. The amount is small,
but it remains an unsatisfactory way of dealing with the
matter, all the same. The co. 12 months ago had £29,930
of floating debt, including overdraft. The bond-issue has
wiped off £12,478 ; the balance of the money has gone
into plant. Even now, therefore, there remains £17,452
of “ sundry creditors,” so that the co. will not have a
clean sheet even after it takes the other £lO,OOO of bonds
into account. For a co. which has been making such
large profits this is not satisfactory. Still, the co.’s slide
backwards has been stopped. Some figures

In 1904 things had drifted so far that the div. was only
shown as fully earned by cutting down the allowance for
wear and tear ; but things have improved a little. Even
now, however, the allowance is under 2 per cent, of the
total sunk in plant, works, &c. ; and it is doubtful
whether that is adequate, remembering that the 2 per
cent, (or less) has to cover repairs and renewals, which are
not separately charged, as in some concerns.

The foreclosed properties, which were written down
in 1903, are now valued at £13,808, being £1497 less than
12 months ago ; properties realised during the year show
£407 profit on the written down values. On the average
value held during the year the net rent (after paying
; rates and repairs) shows £4 7s. 6d. per cent. These pro-
perties, £3838 of loans (£3401 last year) and £1450 on
deposit, are all the co.’s resources. On the other side, it
owes only £l3 to the public ; in 1898 the liability was
£15,466, so that the clearing-up has been very satisfac-
tory. Having now practically a clean sheet (except for
the lost capital) this society goes out of business, the
Second Queen Building Society taking over all its assets
and liabilities.

Castlemainb Woollen Co. (Castlemaine,
Vic.): —
Written To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. off. serves, serves.

June, 1903 .. 1265 4 „ „ 1078 £4OO 2770

£lB9O of Reserves transferred to Capital 1750
June, 1904 .. 1285 4 p.c. = 1156 750 1129
Dec., 1904 ..301 301 1430
June, 1905 .. 1019 3 „ „ 900 119 1549
These bloated manufacturers are getting 3 per cent. now.
The year has shown a profit of £1320 ; but then nothing
has been written off for wear and tear of plant, &c.,
which stands in the accounts at over £37,000. If only 2
p.c. is charged (and for machinery that is not enough) the
net profits would be reduced to £5BO for the year,
or at the gorgeous rate of £1 18s. Bd. per cent,
on capital. It is quite clear that things cannot
go on at this rate. In 1902-3 the year’s gross profit was
£2214; next year, £2155; in the past year, £1320.
Already the net return on capital has got down to less
than half the interest payable on Government stock.
And yet the directors report that “ the machinery has
been fully employed during the year.” It is not that
Australian mills can’t get the work to do ; the trouble is
that they are compelled by law to pay reasonable wages,
whereas the foreign factories with which they must com-
pete are under no such obligation. To the Labor party
it should be obvious, from this and other co.s’ disappear-
ing profits (they have completely disappeared in many
cases), that it must abandon either its Wages Boards and
Arbitration Courts and high-wage ideals or else
its fiscal - sinking and low-tariff sympathies. The
present tariff is clearly not high enough to
permit this co. (and others are in a worse plight)
to go on under existing wage-conditions; and
the party will have to make up its mind which it will
have—(l) lower wages, (2) closed factories and no wages,
or (3) high tariff. Not only has the co. earned the
miserable rate of interest previously mentioned, but its
overdrafts and other public liabilities have increased
from £7450 to £8247; and the steady movement the
wrong way must, of course, only hasten the acute
trouble with the banker which is already afflicting at
least two co.-owned Australian woollen mills. And when
the banker steps in in that spirit, the workman generally
steps out —it becomes mostly a matter of realisation
then.

The profits shown a few years ago were, to some extent,
fictitious, the assets of some subsidiary co.’s having been
absurdly over-valued ; hence the writing down 12 months
ago. But the business is still remarkably profitable ; and
through the prudent policy of cutting down the dividend
the reserves are increasing rapidly. Shareholders’ funds
(capital and reserves, taken together) show for last half-
year earnings at the rate of £l4 Bs. per cent, per annum.
In the past 12 months business appears to have expanded
rapidly. In July, 1904, the co. had £23,790 in cash or on
deposit; £20,000 of this has since been put into stock or
is represented by outstanding accounts, and the co. also
owes £39,444 as against £27,639, also represented by
stock, &c. It is an erratic and to some extent risky busi-
ness ; and these movements probably mean big profits or
big losses—the season will settle which. The co. owns
the whole of the stock in the M'Culloch Carrying Co.,
Limited (£12,500). That co. lately declared a 6s. dividend ;
but this does not affect Wright, Heaton, and Co., which
takes credit for the profits half-yearly as they are made.
It is a curious arrangement.

The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. of
Maoriland (Dunedin): —
To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. serve. serve s.
1901 £4710
1902 £1370 9 p.c. = £9CO £l7O 5180
1903 1365 10 „ „ 1000 365 5545
1904 2234 12 ~ „ 1200 1034 6579
1905 4138 15 „ „ 1500 2638 9217
“ Agency and commission,” which brought in gross
£3915 in 1904, in the past year realised £6437 ; the jump,
the directors frankly say, js “ due to circumstances which
cannot be expected readily to recur.” But the co. is
in a comfortable position, anyway. It has deposits,
mortgages, and other investments of £17,914 ; and,
on the average held during the year, the interest
earnings show just 5 per cent.—sufficient of itself
to pay the very tidy dividend of 8 per cent, on capital.
What is the gross amount of trust estates in hand is not
disclosed ; apparently, however, the co. goes considerably
outside ordinary trustee business, and derives much profit
from other sources. But for that, the extreme irregu-
larity of the profits would suggest that the co. takes
credit for the full corpus commission on trust estates as
they come in, and leaves all future expenses to be borne
by the running commission, which is often inadequate for
the purpose. The co. gives very little information, and
what its practices are can’t be stated definitely ; but the
matter is worth inquiring into. This disposal of the
corpus commission is the one thing that throws suspicion
on many trustee co.s’ published accounts.

The Mutual Store has been undergoing re-organisation ;
competition had been giving the co. a wilted look. It is
stated that there has since been much improvement; but
“accumulations of old stocks have been disposed of at
greatly reduced prices, materially affecting the years
profits.” The accounts, however, don’t show this pro-
ceeding, which would be tantamount to a writing-off of
reserves. The incident shows, nevertheless, that the cold
eye of suspicion must be turned upon reserves that con-
sist largely of stocks of retailers’ merchandise. Some
other figures to show how things are shaping :
Mortgages, Interest Gross Working
Overdrafts, paid. Profit. Expenses.

W.th debt and working expenses moving down and gross
trading: profit moving up a trifle, the outlook is certainly
a shade less gloomy. The least satisfactory feature is that
the co. grabs still another dividend that hasn’t been fully
earned ; and that calls for particularly severe remark in
view of the miserable size of the reserves compared with
the liabilities to the public. Those liabilities won’t shrink
along with the assets which stand against them ; there-
fore a co. that owes £1 for every £1 of its capital, which
is about this co.’s position, ought, as a general thing, to
put twice as much to reserve as a co. that owes nothing.
Yet this co. goes on term after term setting aside nothing
at all.

Barrier Co-operative Society, Ltd., (Broken
Hill, N.S.W.): —
Divs. and Written To Re- Re-
Profits. Bonus. Off. serve. serve.
Dec., ’O4 .. £596 £371 £lO2 £123 £169
June, ’O5 .. 1,129 591 232 306 475
An excellent feature of this new society (it is now three
years old, and began from very small things) is that,
unlike most co-operative societies, it isn’t afraid to build
up a reserve. An annual and fixed 5 per cent, of the
gross profits goes to the fund. Apparently also all
bonuses uncalled for go the same way. The accounts are
not as clear as they might be on this point; £35 has dropped
into the reserves in the past half-year without explana-
tion, and, though included in the foregoing as profit,
may be the amount of these uncalled-for bonuses. An
annual 5 per cent, is written off stock, buildings, &c., and
10 per cent, off plant; considering where it is that the
society hangs out its sign, this is certainly not
any more than enough. The accounts lump share capital
and loans ; likewise the interest thereon. This might be
improved upon. The society has liquid assets (exclusive
of stock) amounting to £1903 (including £1416 outstand-
ing accounts, which, in a co-operative retail business, is
excessive); liabilities total £3458, £1250 of which is
balance due on premises. The business has increased
very rapidly ; the turn-over already amounts to £25,000 a
year.

Another of those little co.’s which are teaching the
farmers that the papers and politicians who have been
telling them about the blessings of individualism and the
beauties of self-reliance have all the while been bailing
up the wrong cow. This little co. (which, by the way,
sends out a very clear balance-sheet) is, like most of them,
working on a very small capital; but there is one thing in
its favor —its “ sundry debtors ” item is very small, so its
risk of being struck heavily by bad debts is not great.
Present turnover amounts to over £50,000 a year.
* * * *
Australia is making the usual fool of itself over
its zinc ores, and is feeding the foreigner instead of itself.
Average price of American zinc ore (60 per cent, zinc) for
past six months has been £8 17s. 6d. ; high price was
partly due to the Joplin mines being flooded. Spelter in
America sells at about the same price as in London, so,
assuming £25 per ton as the average value, 60 per cent,
zinc ore would be intrinsically worth £l5. Deducting
£8 17s. 6d. paid to the mine co.’s, £6 2s. 6d. is left to
cover smelting and other expenses, losses of metal, and
to provide profit. Broken Hill 40 per cent, zinc concen-
trates, with spelter at £25, are intrinsically worth £10;
and as the co.’s only get about £2 per ton, the returning
charges must come to £B, with the value of any silver
and lead recovered thrown in. As a ton of zinc concen-
trates contains between £2 and £3 worth of silver and
lead, the foreign buyers have thus a good thing on. The
American mining co. gets about half the intrinsic value
of the ore, while the Australian co.’s get only one-sixth,
or at the most one-fifth. The difference is accounted for
mainly because Australian ore is carried half round the
globe to foreign smelters. There is competition for
American zinc ore ; there is probably none at all to
influence Australian prices, because the buyers represent
a foreign spelter trust. The carrying of over three tons
of zinc ore to be smelted down into one ton of spelter is
preposterous in view of the local coal resources. Australia
has all the natural elements in favor of local spelter
works, but that may count for nothing if the trade has,
unaided, to face foreign bounties and subsidies.
* 4 * w
Baillieu’s people, who are putting £50,000 in the
Broken Hill South Blocks in return for half the mine, turn
out to be the Lake View Consols Co. This is only the third
time this S’African sort of finance has been introduced to
the Barrier ; previous occasions were when the Camden
Syndicate got a block of Junction shares, and the recent
transfer of a big block of Block 14 shares, but the latter
went into private hands. Bewick, Moreing were respon-
sible for the South Blocks deal. Both Hertzig and Hoover
spent much time on the mine during August. South
Blocks formed part of the B.H. South ; latter co. floated
it off in 200,000 shares —100,000 promoters’ and 100,000
contribs. Five years have been spent exploring to
440 ft., where there is a 21ft. lode. Most development has
been at 300 ft. ; lode there runs about 24ft. Everywhere
it is rich in lead but very poor in silver ; and under two
years ago, manager Wainwright, of the South—one of the
most successful managers on the line—did not see his
way to recommend erection of expensive plant; he
couldn’t see enough profit to compensate for the risk.
Profits will be small; but price paid is low. L.V. Consols
not only gets half of the mine for £50,000, but the £50,000
goes into the property, £35,000 of it in plant. Arrange-
ment is to form a new co. in 200,000 shares ; old share-
holders take 100,000 of these, of which contributors only
get 25,000. It was expected that they would kick
against that proposed division, but they have taken it
like lambs.

Veteran manager John Howell—a very much
aged veteran —returned to Australia in time to hear that
the London syndicate had exercised its options over the
Conrad mines, Howell (N.S.W.). The syndicate took
over the King Conrad a couple of months back ; it gets a
controlling interest now in the Consolidated, and the two
will be worked together. The syndicate began its explo-
rations last October, putting up £lO,OOO, to be spent on
the Consolidated. The ore developed at 400 ft., 140 ft.
below the co.’s deepest workings, settled the matter.
* * * *
lie N.S.W. Mines Department’s report in 1890
that a few tons of ore were sent from Coppabella as long
ago as that, a correspondent writes :—“ This ore came from
Mt. Gifford. A hunter named Parsons found galena on
Mt. Gifford, and taking some with him he called at
Musgrove’s house and showed him the ore and where he
got it. Musgrove, in turn, showed it to a man named
Bowman. Bowman took a five-ton trial out, and that
was the last that was ever heard of it.” Mt. Gifford (now
known as Irvine’s) is about five miles from the Coppabella
Proprietary.
* * * *
G. H. Blakemore, the Cobar Syndicate’s
manager, who had a good deal to do with the decision
to put the old O.S.A. (Cobar, N.S.W.) to another trial,
gives his opinion of the unexpected lead development.
The old shaft, 332 ft. deep, was continued to 457 ft. ;
leaving sft. for a well, they opened out and rushed in
a crosscut “ 24ft. long, practically all in high-grade
carbonate of lead. First 4ft. is worth about 15 per cent.,
next 9ft. about 30 per cent., and a bulk sample from the
last lift, assayed 52J per cent, lead, ldwt. gold, and
lldwt. silver.” This last stuff should be worth £7 12s.
per ton, gross. Blakemore thinks they should get a big
body of stuff ; but nothing definite can be said as to that
yet. In any case, however, the find could not be worked
from the present shaft. The £5O syndicate shares have
touched £3OO ; there are only 44 of them.

* * • *
Experiments were recently conducted at the
Ivanhoe Mine, W.A., by the metallurgist Mr. Brett, with
the Wilfley Slime Table on the fine sands. The results
achieved were astonishing. The amount of concentrates
saved (vide, p. 298, “Monthly Journal of the Chamber
of Mines,” W.A., July 31, 1905) has, by means of the extra
Slime Table, been increased by 45 per cent., whilst the
total gold values have increased 60 per cent. Of this
Concentrate saved by the Slime Table, 92 per cent, is
pure Concentrate Slime. The results of the experiments
have been so satisfactory that the management have
ordered 11 more with the intentions of installing them
forthwith. It is then proposed to increase the number to
25. The Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne,
are the sole manufacturers for Australasia.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining C 0.,,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes -40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluining, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

“Nothing Succeeds Like Success.” The
Hornsby “Upright” Water Tube Boiler continues its
successful career. Mail advice reports that the Cargo Fleet
Iron Coy. of Middlesbro, after having experience of this
boiler for some time, have now placed a contract with
Messrs. R. Hornsby and Sons, Ltd., for 10 more—alto-
gether 5500h.p. The Strand and Charing Cross Electric
Light Corporation of London, after similar experience,
have installed and on order over 20,000h.p., while the
Municipal Council of Finchley, London, have similarly
placed repeat orders for large installations. In Austra-
lasia, besides the 2500h.p. now being installed for the

* * * •
The big S. Australian Farmers’ Co-operative
Union, Ltd., which has an annual turnover of nearly half-
a-million, has just owned up to another bad year, again
through disastrous deals in wheat. The society made a
profit of £1174 in other directions, but lost £6640 on
wheat, thus going another £5466 to the bad. There is
serious talk of winding the business up ; but the farmers
who are afraid of a wheat ring will ni-nbahUr r. a
ore and let dead work slide. Anyway, as long as it lasts
at the O.K. there isn’t another manager in Queensland on
quite so good a wicket; the arrangement yields Gibb close
on to £3OOO a year. In other respects the management of
the co. is extremely economical. It has been proposed
lately to shift the office from Cairns to Brisbane. Result
would inevitably be to increase management expenses;
and there would be little or no advantage except to the
share-jobber. Mine’s output continues remarkable for
such a youngster; last three weeks’ profit was £12,500.
» » » *
Adelaide has been having a huge gamble in
Smith’s Creek (N.Q.) tin ; 40,000 shares were handled in a
week. Capt. Warren, who pricked the bladder once, has
been up again, reporting more hopefully (as he did, by
the way, of the B.H. Junction recently); but the mine
needs £lO,OOO spent on it, he says. Co. can’t now
have 10,000 shillings available ; and the people who put
in more money will want the big end of the mine—if there
is a mine or a big end. The people behind Smith’s Creek
ought to be left to have their own gamble ; pretty safe to
say that the outsider will fall in nine times out of ten.
As to Capt. Warren’s hopes, note that they are largely
based on better values in the end of the 300 ft. drive south.
A crosscourse is about due there, judging by what
happened at upper levels ; and tin values mostly improve
near a slide or crosscourse.

Expected up-North that Vulcan tin (N.Q.) will
come along with 2s. divs. very early next year, if the
market keeps good.
• • • •
Queen Cross, Charters Towers, has struck
another heap of trouble, its fine mill and plant having
been wrecked by explosion. Affair is another Charters
Towers mystery. At the beginning of last year shares
were £3 ss. ; last week, about Bs. The collapse was
so sudden and complete that it probably turned a head or
two. That is the only explanation visible. Once before
the Towers had a similar disaster; and bad feeling was
supposed to be at the bottom of that also. In that case
the idea was that one firm was controlling the
mine, and that the firm had enemies ; in the present
case, though nothing of that sort has happened,
directors have been justly blamed for neglecting
development work, and thus precipitating a crisis. Divs.
in 1903 were £215,000, and £220,000 in 1904 ; but while the
directors rooted that out, pretty well all they did in the
way of prospecting was a bit of diamond-drilling; and
the drill has been a ghastly failure on the Towers.
Result was that when they could not pick up the expected
Victory stone, returns collapsed. But the principal
sufferers were English and not Australians ; former used
to hold the stock heavily, then sold out for a song, and
afterwards bought back at boom prices. Nevertheless,
to show that some ex-North Queenslanders considered
the stock worth holding, even at the big prices it
went to, W. D. Casey, of Sydney, the biggest holder, held
on to his bundle, and is holding on still. Present value of
stone known to exist in the mine is about 4s. or 4s. 6d.
per share.
* • * *
Brilliant St. George, Charters Towers (N.Q.),
must soon put its shutters up, as far as regular dividends
are concerned. In 13 years the co. has got through
314,125 tons of stone, averaging £4 13s. 7d. per ton, and
has paid £755,400 in dividends. Half-year just finished
saw 12,876 tons crushed for £27,776 ; cyanide bullion and
concentrates brought the value to £3 17s. per' ton.
Term before last stone averaged £4 4s. Bd. for 13,315
tons, and £36,000 was paid in dividends ; last term
only £IB,QOO. Expenditure was £29,555 ; receipts,
£49,693. After paying £lB,OOO in divs., and £9OO
in div. tax, there was a surplus of £1238, which
was placed to reserve, and built it up to
£19,521. As soon as the underhand stope is finished, and
it won’t be long, a start will be made in earnest upon the
pillars. These will assist the mine to keep up appear-
ances for some time, as there is a considerable tonnage,
from No. 16 right through to No. 4. Of course there are
possibilities in the shallow ground, and a cross-reef un-
worthy of notice in the good days is now being tested. The
Victory has put a winze down some 130 ft. on the George
boundary. Reef showing may averagea footof 12dwt. stone.
Prospecting is being carried on from the George shaft, but
there are some doubts as to the level being on the right
thing. Co. has a treatment plant of its own two miles
away from the mine. As there is no likelihood of sufficient
stone from outside sources being forthcoming to keep
the mill going, the directors are thinking about securing
and developing some new property with the £19,521
in hand, less 6d. dividend declared for October. As
something over £5 per 10s. share has been paid in divi-
dends, holders would do well to practise self-denial for a
term, because £20,000 is hardly sufficient capital to open
up a new property with. Costs in the well-run George
have amounted to £2 ss. lid. per ton.
* * * *
Encouraging reports are made of the Oaks,
Mount Hutton, 25 miles from Rockhampton (Q.). Pros-
pectors got first gold last February, and traced the prob-
able source to the crest of a hill, where they found a
formation. Good specimens were got in trial shafts.
Capital of the co. which was then formed is
£24,000 in 10s. shares; half fully paid, the balance
to ss. Already £llOO in dividends have been paid.
Co. has not been able to exactly fix the thickness of the
workable material ; there is a patchy rubble seam, apart
from the regular lode material. Lode consists of quartz
veins, with ironstone. There are traces of cobalt, man-
ganese, nickel, and other metals in it. Lode has been
proved to a vertical 130 ft. Reward claims are 1500 ft. by
400 ft. ; and 16 tons crushed from the eastern ground,
1100 ft. from the main shaft, gave 260 z. A Huntington
mill is being put up ; plant will then treat 500 tons per
month.
* * » *
Difficult to get the real strength of the Ohillagoe
position under altered system of management. Ex-
general manager Greenway in his own sanguine way was
satisfied before he left that the metallurgical operations
were going along nicely, and continuing so would pull the
co. out of the mud. (A lot of mud would, however, stick
to it.) In addition, he did not think the Etheridge railway
scheme of any account whatever ; in fact, was of opinion
it would cripple the co. Railway-superintendent Back had
different views on the railwav question, and also conflict-
ing views concerning the part the metallurgical opera-
tions should play in the whole Ohillagoe scheme, and
that’s how the publicly-reported friction happened. From
present North Queensland developments, and not over-
looking the possibilities of the Etheridge field, Ohillagoe
concern appears to more and more assert itself as a pro-
position for a railway man.
* * * *
Candlish, the Georgetown, Etheridge (N.Q.),
king is a typical Queensland goldfield boss. Over 20 years
on the field ; is a storekeeper ; finances and feeds the field
in bad times, and just about owns it in good times.
Queensland miners do not place much faith in technical
training, but the storekeepers do, and in the end they
become the boss delvers. Process of successful evolu-
tion in N.Q, is something like this Arrive ; keep a
store; keep up your pecker; keep the miners;
keep the mine. Then go South, where the mine, or what
you have got for it, keeps you. At present, Candlish,
who is a much esteemed man, owns the Havelock, Nil
Desperandum, the Queenslander, half each of the Better
Luck and the Dry Hash, besides a battery at Struggle
Creek. He evidently knows the Gulf country, for he
scorns the idea of rail communication with Normanton.
If a line is built, it must connect either with Almaden or
Mungana, or at all events must give communication with
an eastern port. There’s no possibility of a port in the
Gulf.

Economist Curie estimates Mt. Morgan (Q.) to
be worth £2,900,000, or £2 18s. per share. He figures it
out this way :
Five years’ profits on old mine .. £650,000
Net profits on gold-copper ore .. 2,100,000
Cash in hand 150,000
Total .. .. £2,900,000
This assumes copper at £55 per ton. When the new
copper development at 750 ft. was exhaustively reported
on, the manager, taking copper at £6O, estimated that
there was £2,489,000 profit in that section.
* * * *
W.A. State batteries during July treated 8600
tons for 94400 z. bullion, valued at £34,000. Total treated
to date, 272,850 tons for 319,2000 z., worth £1,184,200. The
batteries are worked at a loss of something like £lO,OOO a
year; but, indirectly, they are a great benefit to the
state, and keep a lot of men employed. Chief reason
they do not pay is that they are nearly all obsolete plants
—most of them so old that they are constantly breaking
down and under repair.
* * * *
At Tuckanarra, on the Murchison (W.A.), a
party of miners some time back abandoned a show, after
about 300 tons of stone. There was no battery
would not pay to cart the stone that distance, so went o 9
in search of something better. A State battery has since
been put up at Tuckanarra, and the other day a couple
of men pegged out the deserted claim and put the stone
through. It went 14dwt. to the ton and gave % clear
profit of over £5OO.

Randalls (W.A.), of which great things were
once predicted, has turned out a very low-grade field.
The district is intersected by large strong lodes, but
values all round have been under lOdwt., and not payable
under existing conditions. One mine, the Surprise, has,
however, got a rich bunch ; 3 tons have returned 481 oz.
Owners have kept their own counsel, as, until the parcel
was treated, no one had any idea that such ore existed.
Average of other parcels treated during the month was
only Sjdwt.
* * * *
Southern Cross (W.A.), very little heard of for
years, is looking up again, and will shortly add consider-
ably to the gold yield. The Greenmount, Fraser’s South
Extended, Transvaal and Northern Blocks Syndicate will
all shortly be running 10-head mills, and among them
should easily treat 5000 tons monthly. Manager Hamilton,
of Boulder Prop., has a working option over a show at the
Cross, which is now being developed; so this long
neglected field may again make a noise in the world.
i* • * •
Princess Royal Central, Norseman (W.A.), is
still being energetically developed by manager Hender-
son, but with poor results. Hundreds of feet of driving
and crosscutting have been done at 950 ft, but no pay ore
has been located so far. This is the first deep level
mine tackled in W.A., and owners deserve better luck.
* * * *
Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, once so sensational,
is now a very humdrum show. The rich shoot has either
petered out entirely, or had a break, and the mine is now
an ordinary affair. While the shoot lasted it gave 483
tons, yielding 79860 z. of gold and 37,3220 z. silver, worth
£35,700, and it paid £32,400 in divs. Driving is still being
done at 200 ft., and the shaft is being sunk to 300 ft. No
show in the vicinity has struck pay ore, and the feverish
working of the north end of the Kalgoorlie field is over,
though prospecting is still going on.
* « * *
Great Ophir, Davyhurst (W.A.), is being
equipped with a 20-head mill from the defunct Lady Loch.
Mine is already adorned with a Krom roll and cyanide
plant, but no amalgamating tables, and consequently the
coarse gold is all lost. Co. was recently reconstructed in
300,000 shares of ss. each, of which shareholders in the old
co. were entitled to two for each old share held. Public
were offered 100,000 shares with a 2s. assessment; but,
although a month has elapsed, no information has been
given as to the result. A pretty good thing to leave
alone.
* * * *
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie, has long joined
the silent list, as Manager Hawkins is no boomster. He
is now leaving property to take chartre of Tarcoola
Blocks, and a new man is to be appointed. Property
seems to be hopeless ; any ore found is merely in
bunches and of very low-grade, as the test crushings put
through at instigation of Tom Hewitson proved. All the
other co. ’s owning shows in the vicinity have given the job
best, and the Junction should follow suit.
* * * *
Chaffers, Kalgoorlie, is now being developed at
400, 600, 800, and 1000 ft. Manager Cape still reckons that
the ore, though low grade, is payable. He says that his
predecessors took out the ore too wide, and consequently
the test crushings from the dump were a failure. For
position (next the Horseshoe) the mine is unassailable;
but, unfortunately, the pay shoot petered out before
reaching it. The area is too small and too much cut up
by railways and roads ever to be much of a mine.
* * * *
Boulder Deep Levels (W.A.) look less hopeful
At 1100 ft. the lode is broken up in the west drive, and
is of no consequence. Going east it is rather better, but
certainly not good enough to pay. Marvellous assays
and samples again come from the mine, exactly as at the
time of the scandal; but, though mine is open to inspec-
tion, no such stone can be seen. Where does it come
from? Many shares were bought on the field on the
strength of these specimens, and the buyers are feeling
very sorry for themselves. Who sold ?
* * * *
Associated, Kalgoorlie, still has a lot of possi-
bilities. In the west crosscut at 1200 ft. a lode 18ft. wide
carrying streaks of telluride has been cut on the Perse-
verance boundary. This development is of great import-
ance to both mines, as the underlay is slightly to the
west. The Oroya shoot is opening out well at the three
points intersected, and is adding greatly to the value of
the reserves. Developments since Manager Roberts took
charge in May have entirely altered the outlook, and, once
the alterations to plant are completed, co. should be a
leading div. payer.
* * # •
Fingall Associated, Day Dawn, floated in Perth,
in January, 1904, is said to have been passed on to Bull.
The working of the mine by the Perth crowd was a
ghastly failure. After muddling along for some months
it closed down for want of funds. The vendors did not
even get the £5OO cash stipulated for, but only 125,000
fuily-paid shares, which were never negotiable. The
mine adjoins Fingall Consols on the North, but so far as
tested does not seem to have much of a future, though it
must be admitted it hasn’t had a fair run. If Bull puts
up enough working capital, and gives’ a reliable man
charge, something may be heard of it.
* * * «
The two recent discoveries of gold below 4000 ft.
in Bendigo have given mining there the greatest fillip it
has had for years. In New Chum Railway, at 4225 ft.
good prospects have been obtained, and the other day,
in the Victoria Quartz, gold was got at 4100 ft. It is
likely now that half-a-dozen other mines will
set to work to send their shafts down another
quarter of a mile or so. This new phase of
mining will necessitate almost a revolution in Bendigo
methods. Already the shafts —generally 10ft. by 4ft.—
have proved too small for work below 3000 ft. New Chum
Railway has a 10ft. by 4ft. shaft. For 800 ft. it is merely a
skeleton with the compartment not boarded up. Yet the
management complains of being cramped. There is no
doubt that the mines to go any distance below 4000 ft. will
require new shafts. This would be necessary, in most
cases, for ventilation purposes, anyway. Rock tempera-
tures at these levels go as high as 106 degrees, and the
ventilating capacity of alO x 4 shaft is too small. Most
of the shafts were sunk by rule of thumb in the
old days, and many of them are screwed, or
twisted, or bent, or something. Nothing but constant
survey can keep a shaft accurate for three-quar-
ters of a mile, and it is only within the last few
years that Bendigo has seriously considered the surveyor
as a factor in mining. The cost of the new mining is, of
course, high. Where 4dwt. and sdwt. paid dividends at
1500 ft. or 2000 ft., it takes pretty well Joz. to pay expenses
below 3500 ft. Nothing under Joz. is good at 4000 ft. or
below it. So the stone in the deep levels has to
be better than that in medium and shallow levels.
Every truck of deep-level dirt has to be hauled
three-quarters of a mile. That takes to 3 minutes.
When men are being hauled it takes 7 to 8 minutes.
The plant has to be powerful, and the wear and tear
on it increases out of all proportion to distance
sunk.. The rope alone of a 4000 ft. shaft weighs two tons ;
allow another three tons for cages and trucks, and it
must have a breaking-strain of 50 tons. Then men at
4000 ft., in a hot, enervating atmosphere, cannot do any-
thing like the work of men at 2000 ft. It takes three men
to do two men’s work. Another innovation for Bendigo—
one that must come, and should have come years ago—is
the introduction of two-decker cages, so that two trucks
can be hauled instead of one. At present, the deep mines
wind one half-ton of dirt at a time three-quarters of a
mile. It is a long time since Ballarat saw the extrava-
gance of that system, and adopted two-deckers. Still, as
far as writer knows, there is not a two-decker cage in
Bendigo. An efficient spray to lay the smoke and dust
after firing is another thing badly needed to save time
and, consequently, money. With improvements such as
these there is no reason why mining should not be con-
ducted successfully at far below 4000 ft., provided good
enough stone can be got.
* * * *
There are already upwards of 20 barges and
dredges working in the rivers and creeks of north-east
Victoria, and for the moment all the public co.’s are
making a profit. Private concerns are owned by Mrs.
J. A. Wallace and Fletcher Bros.; and, as they are putting
up more plants, there must be a bit in it. The Fletchers
are building a new pontoon on the Yackandandah Creek.
The warden is kept busy hearing applications for for-
feiture of unworked leases, applicants being usually suc-
cessful. A Maorilander is securing much ground,
presumably for a Maoriland syndicate ; the other day he
secured the forfeiture of a lease at Osborne’s Elat, and
Lockardt’s Creek. Up at Bright there will be 10 more
plants at work shortly, and from there eastward, for
nearly 40 miles, ground is pegged out in spots.
* * * *
Glenfine South, Pitfield (Vie.), has gone back to
first intentions. Originally an alluvial show, it blazed
out as a quartz mine, paid a fair swag of divs.,
collapsed, and now is re-appearing as an alluvial
mine. It might be left alone to work out
its destiny if the good of the public did not
demand one or two things. First, it needs to be
pointed out that none of the adjoining mines could be
classed as rich, and the extent of wash in them was
limited. In the Glenfine South old alluvial, the
average of the dirt was low, and the mine could
never have paid divs. if it had not been for the quartz.
The present deep run has to be proved as to extent and
richness. The co. has certainly got on a rich run of wash,
but the rich dirt is only in one working. The Ballarat
crowd is making the most of this rich patch, as is its wont.
Dirt from said crosscut will go over 2oz. to the fathom,
but there are plenty of signs that the rich run is petering
out as drives advance. In any case, to pay 10s. for shares
at present is a gamble, which the history of the field fails
to justify.
• • * *
If Victorian coal co.’s are feeling the pinch of
hard times, they have no one save themselves to blame.
It is not that the coal is bad —as a matter of fact, it is
good—but the seams are narrow, the faulting is tremen-
dous, and the railway carriage is a handicap which even
a State grant cannot compensate for. Outtrim-Howitt
Eassed on a lot of scrip to Bull, so he nurses that baby;
ut other shows had not the same wisdom. Jum-
bunna balance sheet just out shows that, although
output went up from 22,000 to 27,000 tons for the
half-year the co. increased its debit balance rather badly.
Mine wages amounted to £14,000 for the six months, and
the value of the coal sold was over £17,000; so that the
margin left was small. The whole position seems to
resolve into this : Gippsland co.’s cannot, on account of
natural conditions, pay divs. without concessions from
the Railway Dept., so long as there is hard competition
from Newcastle. But it would pay the State of Victoria
to work the mines for the railways and to sell the surplus
to the public. Seddon hadn’t half the same chance with
his coal mines, but he took the risk. Isn’t Labor leader
Prendergast able to show Bent the way for a live deal?
* * • *
Alluvial mining in Victoria, outside the dredging
shows, presents a long vista of fading possibilities. To
start with, the J. Bull mines at Moolort and Charlotte
Plains are not interesting the universe, save in the
estimates of the richness of the wash at the Victorian
Deep Leads. At the other end of the Berry system
there isn’t a mine, outside Madame Berry West, on
the dividend list—calls look perilously near in the
Extended. Cathcart Prop., at Ararat, is languish-
ing ; the Avoca shows aren’t a-going; at Pitfield,
Glenfine South is giving a flash in the pan; at
Rutherglen, Great Southern isn’t doing a great deal
more than holding its own; and ditto at Chiltern
with the Chiltern Valley Mines. Then at Maryborough,
Duke United can’t strike the lead; Burnt Creek
is too poor for divs.; Duke and Main Leads
Consols have to beat the water ; Chalk’s No. 3 wants gold ;
and there is a big fly in the Main Lead North, which per-
haps will be disclosed now that dividends look like being
endangered. It is very sad to write, but from north
to south Victorian deep-lead mining has never been less
prosperous than to-day.
* * * *
It is hard to apply the ordinary rules of
analysis to the flattening out of the Briseis Extended
(Tas.) shares, because to do so would mean the un-
ravelling of a lot of market intricacies, which,
perhaps, will never be disentangled. The first error
was simple. An estimate was made of a yield.
This was an error. The evidence seems to
show that what was thought to be a fair allowance was
made for iron and sand. It is not unusual to make such
estimates. Still it was an error to have refrained from
saying that the clean-up was an estimate. The next error
was in not making Northey’s letter respecting the yield
public the minute it was to hand. Thus two main errors—
first the estimation, secpnd the failure to publish. And it
can be said without the least degree of harshness
that it is the latter phase that shareholders are likely to
question about most closely. That the market had an
idea that the return was not likely to come up to the
figures reported is shown both by the persistent selling
and by emphatic assertions on the point days before the
exact yield canie out. What everyone wants to find out
is who sold and why. The mystery may never be solved,
but the fact remains that those who talked spoke accu-
rately, and those who sold made profits.
* * * *
Some day it will have to be made a misde-
meanor for a tin co. to report results in the form of
“black tin” or “tin oxide.” Briseis Extended (Tas.)
reported 15 tons 14cwt. “black tin”; over four tons of
this turned out to be black iron sand. It was then
estimated that 11 tons 6cwt. of stuff going 70 per cent,
tin could be obtained ; but about the only solid fact in
the recent report is that 10 tons were sent to the
smelter. The “ black tin ” method of reporting results
is one of the things that the people who are rushing into
tin specs, will curse by-and-bye.

Re rapid shaft sinking (“ Redcap,” B. 14/9/’O5),
W. Uren writes “ Years ago I was one of 24 who con-
tracted to sink a shaft, 12ft. x 4ft., within 3in. timber,
with a 12in. frame every 50ft. Our contract started 100 ft.
from surface : ground called for two kegs of powder per
week. In nine weeks we sank 234 ft. 6in.. Work was
plumb and true. Shaft was on Learmonth’s claim at
Egerton, now, I think, held by the Black Horse Co. I
have had over 50 years’ experience, and have not known
this record to be beaten.”
* * T *
“E. Hill” claims the Australian record for
quick shaft sinking for Charters Towers “ Queen Cen-
tral Block sunk one month 145 ft. and timbered'32ft.; size
15 x 4 in the clear ; four chambers ; rock drilling machines,
Ingersoll’s ; dynamite, gelignite. Last fortnight’s sinking,
75ft., which we claim a record for Australia.” Another
correspondent submits this as the S’African record
“ Jupiter shaft, Simmer Deeps, was sunk by Mr. Howard,
an American manager, 127 ft. 6in. in one month; shaft
was 28 xB, and in blue rock.”

The result of another liquidation was reported
to Melbourne Practice Court last week. After having
had its doors open for deposits and any other trifles that
might come its way for about 3J years, the British Bank
of Australia shut up in 1901, and ever since then it has
been in liquidation. It had thus been in liquidation just
four times longer than it was in business. The awful
thing was more or less an offshoot of the Anglo-Australian
Bank; and besides borrowing close upon £lOO,OOO from the
British Bank of Australia, the Anglo-Australian held a
heap of the former’s shares. In other words, the Anglo-Au-
stralian practically ran two businesses, and reached out
after two lots of deposits, and to a great extent used both
lots of deposits to prop up the one lot of rotten securities.
Then, when the bottom dropped out of the boom and the
Anglo Australian smashed, the liquidators put in a claim
against the busted A.-A. Bank for £515,365 for calls, and
guarantees, and other things, and told the depositors and
other creditors that they didn’t think the whole lot was
worth the paper it was written on. The liquidators were
right; the net result of the liquidation is that £933 is
available for debenture-holders and unsecured creditors,
and the latter can now get Jd. in the £. And after they
have borrowed a decent enough coat to go and collect
their ha’penny in, they will probably meet some old-time
land-boomer in a silk hat in Collins-street, and he will tell
them that Labor legislation is ruining this country and
that the foreigner won’t send money here for investment
on account of the Socialists and the six hatters.
* * * *
A prospectus witli the eye-filling title of “The
Co-operative Credit Bank of Victoria, Ltd.,” was issued in
Melbourne a few days ago. It turned- out to be just an
ingenious little scheme for running a sort of Mont de
Pi6t6 in connection with the Civil Service Stores. The
the Co-operative Credit Bank, pledges his salary or his
watch, and gets, not cash, but so much credit at the
stores. The scheme, which is to be run, more
or less, in conjunction with the Civil Service
Co-operative Stores, is a pretty severe reflection
upon either the pay or the providence of the Vic.
public servant. And it has a name that will still re-
call unpleasant memories to some grizzled Presbyterians.
There was a Co-operative Credit Bank in Glasgow in the
’7o’s. It gave 18 per cent, per annum for deposits, and it
was mostly, if not wholly, a certain Richard Banner
Oakley. The 18 per cent, interest was paid regularly for
a while, then two detectives stopped the bank as it was
getting into a train one night, and found all the
assets in its carpet-bag. Exactly how many months
or years the bank got, this writer dis-remembers, but it
delivered an eloquent address from the dock upon the
co-operative credit principle.
* • * *
“Weary Bull”: When “J. Pannikin” told
you that the most pitiful sight in White Cliffs opal field
was the crowd of women and children “ specking ”
from a lucky gouger’s claim, he didn’t say what forced
these people to do that sort of thing. Poor opal at
present is absolutely unsaleable, the market being con-
trolled by German buyers, who constitute a ring. There
is nothing to prevent them giving £5 for opal honestly
worth £lO an oz., and the higher grades accordingly.
What can the poor miner do ? Take it or starve 1 For
every gouger on opal 75 are on absolutely nothing ; most
of the other 24 who go to make up the 100 are on potch
and colors. As a rule, when a digger is lucky enough to
get on to a patch, he is not very particular if he loses a
few stones ; and those that go specking on his dump are
trying to get a stone or two with which to buy a tin of
treacle or jam to break the monotony of bread. I know
of a family, 11 all told ; the man has sunk 15 holes, all
over 30 feet, and seen nothing. The buyers are the only
people who do well; most of them don’t have to stay at
the game long. They work in with the Continental
crowd, who seem to control the market. It says very
little for the enterprise of the English-speaking race that
this should be so ; but that it is so everybody interested
in opal-mining knows. Yet, though it controls the
market, Germany produces practically no opal itself;
White Cliffs furnishes about two-thirds of the world’s
output, and still has not the ghost of a say in fixing
values.
* * * *
Parts of Argentina appear to be booming just
now with a wild land drunk at inflated values. Phil
Hardcastle, an old Christchurch (M.L.) boy, who holds a
big position at Bahia Blanca, says there is a feverish rush
to buy and unload properties. One property for which he
paid £2OOO he sold for £5OOO, and in a fortnight it was
priced at £12,000. Another property was sold for £llOO
and resold next day for £4OOO. “ But the crack will come
sooner or later. A couple of bad harvests will alter the
whole aspect of things.”

it incurred (£10,000) in buying the patentees out.

Aboriginalities.
“T.F.” writes
Re the wild-honey aboriginaliars. I have often lined
and followed wild bees to their nests. Modus operands
is : Have a little wadding worked up to a point, and
some sticky substance—bicycle solution, for instance, but
the juice of a thistle is best. Wait till the bee is drink-
ing, then stick the pointed end of the wadding on to its
back, taking care that the wings are not touched. The
bee rises and makes a line for home. If your wadding is
the right weight, you can sight the bee all the way to the
tree ; if it is too light, it will out-distance you ; if it is too
heavy, it can’t fly at all. Experience only will teach you
the right weight, as bees in some districts carry weight
much better than others. One peculiar thing about the
bee’s flight is that it always rises suddenly straight up
just before reaching the tree, and then descends. As
soon as you see the bee rise you know it has reached
home, even though you don’t see it descend. The other
bees always kill the weight-carrier. Why, I don’t know.
Possibly the wadding is such a disguise that they don’t
recognise their fellow bee.
CRUSHING.

TRAMPEDIAN : “ Name it, madam.”
MADAM : “ The bathing stage.”
“ Val ” : It is curious how anything ap-
parently uncanny affects the stomach of a nigger.
Was travelling along the Murray with a friend,
when we fell in with a few natives. One was
very persistent with his “Baeea, boss.” My
friend, who is blessed with a perfect sat of false
teeth, turned sharply on him, at the same time
opening his mouth wide and allowing the top row
of teeth to fall on the lower one. The fearson.e
sight was too much for Binghi, who turned pea-
green, and distributed what breakfast he had had
that morning on the parchedroad. Immediately
after he turned and fled.
“W.L.D.”: In answer to “R.J.E.’s”
request (B. 21/9/’O5) for personal information of
the pleasures of snakebite, here are two vivid
recollections. While camping up Middle Harbor
(Sydney) some years ago, I carelessly grabbed a
30-inch tiger snake in mistake for a harmless
young diamond. Having evidently just cast its
skin Hoplocephalus Curtus, junior, was a bit
sick and not in first-class working order. It
showed a marked reversal of form by biting first
and puffing after. Had the angry expansion of
the neck preceded the bite, I might have dis-
covered my mistake in time and cast the reptile
away, but as it was, it drove its fangs in
thrice between my left forefinger and thumb.
Then I knew. Whipping string tightly
round the wrist, my companion wanted to
cut out the bite, but the shakiness of
his excitement made me snatch his knife
and jab it into the wound myself to make it bleed
freely, while he rushed to the nearest habitation
for assistance. As I walked bade to meet assist-
ance the parts around the bite began to blacken
and swell, and a numb feeling overspread the
hand until it reached an intense degree of pain.
The wrist began to puff, and a tingling ran up the
arm, followed by a sharp, pricking pain behind
the eyeballs. Then the forehead gradually
tightened across, until the temples throbbed as
though they would burst; vision became blurred,
and I stumbled along, unable to distinguish
obstacles in the track. A stifling feeling crept
round the heart, which pulsated with a series of
convulsive throbs, followed by mere flutters, till I
lost all consciousness. This proved to be about 15
minutes after the poisoning. The first-aid party
my mate had rounded up saw me fall down, and
rushed me with a profusion of their snake-bite
remedy—rum in lemonade bottles. What with
rum, exercise, and the fact that the venom did not
come from a full-grown factory, I pulled through ;
but for a fortnight my arm remained painfully
swollen, the skin itself cracking with tension, and
the flesh discoloring as with one big bruise. An
ugly black blister formed at the elbow, burst, and
all was well. On the second occasion, a short,
thick, reddish snake—variety not discovered, but
not a deadly one—fastened on my right forefinger
when I was gathering wild flowers, and made things
extremely uncomfortable. The resultant symp-
toms were the same as before, only very much
modified, except as to the persistent swelling of
the arm. This was more pronounced, and for
three weeks the individuality of the fingers was
almost completely lost, but the discoloration was
not so great as before. As instancing the exist-
ence of a peculiar belief, some people invariably
inquire when snake-time comes round each summer
if I do not feel a recrudescence of the pain.
“Alec Zander” : The pure-bred dingo
not only doesn’t bark, he also bites differently
from the domestic dog. To this fact a friend of
mine was indebted for retaining a portion of his
beauty. Riding through the sandhills on Inna-
inincka station (S.A.), we came across a dingo
hole with fresh tracks. My friend, on the spur
of the moment, stooped to look in ; as he did so a
dingo bitch looked out and bit him clean through
the two cheeks, but it being a snapping bite the
holes were clean punctures and soon healed. A
tame dog would have removed most of the face.
He had the satisfaction of killing four pups,
and doesn’t look into holes any more, barring the
hole at the top of a tumbler.

“ The Lyre ” : At a hare drive recently on
Currandooley Station (N.S.W.;, several Gussies
and as many Reginalds who had come from the city
to shoot were persuaded tojoin the “drivers.” This
was after the host had seen them handle their guns,
by which handling he had lost several valuable
sheep, some temper, and a boundary rider. They
were accommodated with quiet mokes, and
managed to hang on most of the time ; though
the horses seemed to think they had struck some
sort of a lunatic asylum. On a halt being called
for refreshments, the horses Were tied up to a
fence, and when the signal for re mounting was
given, the asylum made a wild rush to see which
of it could mount first. The nags, of course,
showed their horse-sense by snapping their bridles
and going off in a land-agenty way. Once again
they were ranged along tbe fence, when the long-
suffering host said, “Now, you fellows mustn’t
rush your horses. Go up to them quietly and
speak to them in an off-hand way—as you would
salute a human being.” It broke him up con-
siderably when all along the line he heard —
“ Good day, old boy ! ”
“ How do, ole fellah ! ”
“ Naice weathah !”
“ Awfully faine day ! ”
And the most pronounced idiot of the contingent
lost his nag again through raising his cap at close
quarters, because, as he explained afterwards, it
was a lady horse.

“ Borig ” : Re barkless dogs. The Chinese
edible dog, if he is a dog, does not bark, and one
I knew would only eat vegetables. A friend was
keeping him in the country for a Sydney gentle-
man. He (the dog) was the surliest, crankiest-
looking beast I ever saw, and had a blue-black
tongue like a goanna’s. The cattle dogs and tykes
around hated him with a deadly hatred.
* * * *
“Ed.”: State forests and river flats in
Victoria are leased for grazing purposes, and
bring in a few pence per acre per year. The
new trees as they come up are eaten off by
stock, and saplings never grow to any height
because the sheep and cattle nibble the young
shoots. Living on the edge of a State forest, I
have planted box and ironbark seedlings, but only
with good tree-guards do they make any headway.
By a careful thinning-out and cleaning up of these
forests, profitable work would be found for the
unemployed. Quick-growing pines should be
planted in South Gippsland in sandy country
where the rainfall is good (there is much of this
land between the two Gippsland railway lines).
These pines would provide soft woods in place of
those imported from the Baltic and elsewhere. By
the way, cork-oaks flourish in gardens round about
Melbourne, and if these were planted on a large
scale they would in a few years provide a supply
of Pork bark for stoppers and corlc-dust for fruit-
packing. Already in the Mallee and northern
plains there is a scarcity of timber ; these at one
time heavily-timbered districts will yet have to
devote land to tree plantations in order to provide
firewood and fencing timber.

“Jim Pan”: Curious tastes some of the
Far-Outers have. At Mulgabelah a mail-change
groom, who never buys or borrows meat, but eats
rabbits wholesale, had four fowls, which used to
worry old Mother Threeteeth, of the little roadside
pub. She complained and complained that the
fowls ate her fowls’ feed and ruined her seed-beds.
Friction arose. One day the lady caught the four
fowls and took them straight home to the groom’s
gunyah. He chopped their heads off and threw
their bodies on to the ash-heap. “ Aint ya goin’t’
eat um ? ” “ No, I can’t a-bear poultry,” said the
devourer of rabbits. The crows had a rather
choice feed. '

“Paul Cupid”: Re snake-bite (B. 21/9/’O5).
Having been in company with a person from the
time he was bitten till he recovered, I would
describe the symptoms as : (1) on being bitten, he
displayed an extraordinary activity and a great
command of language; (2) on seeing two teeth-
marks in his leg, a sudden pallor, a loss of interest
in mundane things, and a mumbling as if in
prayer ; (3) complained of no pain, only a slight
numbness at the bite, which was very slightly
swollen ; (4) a great desire to sleep; and (5), on
recovery the next day, the general appearance of
a man who had no recollection of how he got
home the night before.
