Sydney D.T., in its “On the Land
weariness, tells how to scald pigs. Inter alia: —
Do not put the hog in the water until life is extinct, or
the skin will be reddened by the cooking of the blood in
the capillaries beneath.
Nothing about the feelings of the hog! But
don’t add torture to death, or you will spoil the
look of the bacon.
* * * *
The annual Sydney stud rabbit sales loom
near. Already it has been seriously suggested to
N.S.W. Government that, instead of trying to ex-
terminate the rabbit, its breed should be improved
by the introduction of Angora rabbits. The
Angora rabbit is said to be as much superior to
plain bunny as the Angora goat is to the seces-
sionist politician.
* * * *
“ Yarrum ” : One of our oldest natives
told me that he frequently noticed that the
coastal blacks —living chiefly on fish and oysters—
were full-blooded and weighty, whilst those of the
table lands and further back, feeding on wallabies
and the like, were lanky, narrow-chested, and had
legs lilce pipe-stems. My old friend knew one
dusky savage—tomahawked at Brisbane Water by
his gin—who was considerably over lflst., whilst
ho never met an out-back darky that went over
12st. The active lives of the hunters for food
may have had something to do with their spare-
ness of body. The fishermen, from mostly sitting
about, would naturally become corpulent. When
they got to war the lean and lanky men used to
get all over the fat fish-eaters.
* * * *
A S. Australian Sunday thirst is mighty,
and must prevail. The police were busy watching
the pubs, for Sunday trading. They were deter-
mined no beer should be sold that day. A local
man was thirsting. He promptly gave a drunk in
charge for “insulting behavior” in front of his
premises. It took two constables to run the
drunk in, and as that is the limit of the force in
the town, the thirsty one rushed for the first pub.
and got his beer while the police had their hands
full.

“ O’-Fipp ” : I have found a Fatman’s ideal
worker. He is working on a Vic. Western District
station for 12s. a week, can neither read nor write,
and boasts that he has never had a bath during
his 60-odd years of life. According to him “the
doctors say baths is bad for yer.” He believes in
witches, and suffers from corns, Freetrade vievvs
and Yellow-Pupism. He says the Labor party is
trying to bring in the “minyonmum label ” and
the “ union franchise,” and if it brings in theso
weird monsters all immigrants will be thrown into
the sea. He has a vague idea that the “squire ”
he is working for discovered Australia ; also that
William of Orange beat Bonyparty at Waterloo.
Says he doesn’t know what this place is coming to
when children fail to tug their forelocks at the
squire’s approach. They were always taught to
greet their “ betters ” humbly in his native village
in the Cold Country.
A PEG ON WHICH TO HANG A CASE .
The Federal Capital site question was alluded to in
the Legislative Assembly by Mr. W. Young, who
asked the Premier whether Mr. Deakin had yet carnea
out his promise to drive in a survey peg', with a view of
bringing about a breach of the peace, so that the ques-
tion might come before the High Court for decision.
Mr. Carruthers replied that he did not desire to bring
about a breach of the peace. The proposal was simply
that a peg should be put in on which to hang a case.
GREENHIDE JACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bullock drivin’,
Pound a week,
’S ’ow I live on
Lubra Creek.
Good enough for
Greenhide Jack ;
Times were rougher
Down the track.
Thin an’ lanky,
Turnin’ gray,
Gettin’ cranky
Like me dray.
Wimmen gammon
They don’t know
Old Jack Hammon
Down below.
Once they angled
Bold an’ free,
To get tangled
Up with me.
Pilin’ jam on,
Scent an’ fan—
“ Mister Hammon ”
Was their man.
Had me harried
With their tricks,
Could ’a’ married
Five or six.
In the long run,
As men do,
Got the wrong ’un
Didn’t you?
Ginger’s dorter
Fiery tart,
Reg’lar snorter,
Broke me heart.
Spliced—instructions—
Lack-a-day 1
N aggin—ructions—
Got away.
Steer me wide o’
Such a shroo;
Good off-sider
Old black Loo.
Kiddies rather
Ohoco-late,
Call me father,
’T any rate.
Want no tweeds, or
Boots to walk,
All they need’s a
Tomahawk.
An’ old Loo’s a
Model spouse,
Doesn’t booze or
Nag an’ rouse.
Fire an’ baccy,
Waterhole,
Plus old Jacky,
Soothes her soul.
Guess I’ll thrive on
Lubra Creek
Bullock drivin’
Pound a week.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

4 ‘ Gilrooney ”:
The rabbit is a
target for all sorts
of pastoral and
agricultural ana-
themas, but he is
doing something
for this country
which Australia
might well be
thankful for —he is
teaching the rising
generation how to
shoot. Go to any
home in the bush
to-day, and you’ll
find the little sport-
ing rifle regarded
as an indispensable
part of the house-
hold parapherna-
lia. Walkthrough
the paddocks after
school, and you’ll
see young Austra-
lia dodging from
tree to tree, or
creeping on his
hands and knees, or
formulating some
strategical move
ment to bag the
wonderfully elu-
sive bunny. It is
quite a common
thing to see the
whole family, from
mother down to the
six-year-old imp of pre-
cocity, astoundingly proficient with the rifle.
And yet, just because an accident happens
occasionally (very occasionally, considering
the number of rifles in use), some of our
grandmotherly pressmen and idiotic legisla-
tors want to restrict the use of firearms.
J3ah ! the rabbit, instead of being a curse
to Australia, may yet prove to be an all-
pervading blessing. And after all, a fatality
now and again is a very light price to pay
for teaching the young Australian idea how
to shoot. For, mark my words, the pre-
cocious bush-boy, who is to-day dodging from
cover to cover in quest of bunny, will be a mighty
valuable asset by-and-bye when the invader tills
his cartridge-belt and begins shooting for keeps.

“Watty” : Can support “ Jlay X.” (B
10/8/’O5) re the wonderful indifference of the
blacks to pain. Some years ago, when they were
plentiful along the Bight, a fight took place
between the Esperance tribe and a combined
team of Jerrimungup and Cocanarup warriors,
the visitors had travelled 150 miles. About 200
bucks were in the melee. They fought where the
town of Ravensthorpe now stands. Before hos-
tilities commenced each tribe lit a fire. Spears,
war kylies and nullas were used., As the battle
progressed casualties were numerous. Many
penetrated thighs and calves. If they went
clean through, the wood was snapped off and
drawn out, and a fire stick was then thrust
through the wound. The spears were all barbed
a ll i° n ? S *^ e were extricated from the
fiesh by passing a long-bladed knife along the
shaft until the barb was located and both drawn
out together. This saved the wound from being
too jagged. After the bore hole had been cauta-
rised with the fire stick, the wounded again rushed
into the fight anxious for revenge. Such rough-
and-ready surgery produced good results, as these
wounds invariably healed. Stomach and body
wounds received similar treatment. It takes a lot
to kill a nigger.

“ Thurla ” : In the matter of those people
who have been writing re Old Sofala (B. 24/S/’O5) :
Sergeant M Carty succeeded Sergeant Meagher,
who was stationed at.Cowra (not Corowa) about
three years. He was removed to Walgett in 1870.
Sergeant Sutherland was shot by George Grey, a
Cotton Valley native (Goulburn district), ’at
Horan’s Hut in 1868. Several Cotton Valley
natives were taken on in the Force, in the hope of
arresting Grey ; but he was never caught. I have
the foregoing on the authority of Police Senior-
Sergeant Ewing, who was a member of the gold
escort mentioned by “ Burrowong.” A few years
ago I met a bushman who spoke of meeting, on
the Paroo, a semi-lunatic named Grey, who used
to boast of shooting Sergeant Sutherland at
Horan’s Hut, on Benni Creek.

“ Wilbea” : Re “H.G.’s” wail (B.
13/7/’05). If he is referring to the ancient pestle
and mortar discovered on the Yodda goldfield
(B.N.G.), this find was given by the owners,
certain miners, to the resident magistrate, who
promptly sent it to the British Museum for
identification. These two implements are of
granite, and roughly carved. The natives have
never seen anything similar, and have no record of
any race prior to themselves. Fragments of orna-
mental pottery have also been dug up on the north-
east coast; they were totally different from any-
thing now in use. By the way, last year, at Fisher-
man Island, Port Moresby, at low water was seen
the almost complete fossii of a man, embedded in
the coral. Immediately some very zealous Govern-
ment officials proceeded to blow the figure out
with dynamite, and, as a consequence, only a few
fragments were recovered, which were promptly
sent by one officer to his sister in London. Every-
thing goes to London.'

“ D.H.R.” : A deal of money used to be
wasted by the old-time squatters in the matter of
green-hide. Bullock after bullock used to
be skinned and the hide flung raw on the
fence from sheer habit, till at length there
would be an accumulation of a hundred or
two. Then they would be hauled down and
burnt to make way for others. No one ever
thought of selling the hides in those days of
recklessness and plenty ; and even had they
done so, the lack of transport service would
have effectually precluded any such practice.
Also hides were many more in a week in
those wicked dark ages than now. Nobody
killed his own calf then, and salt would
often run out six months before a fresh
supply of rations arrived, and so a neigh-
bor’s bullock was slaughtered every time
a pound of steak was wanted. On one
station, on the Warrego, over 170 bul-
locks were slaughtered in 10 months, and
on another ranch on the Upper
Darling, where there were over 30
white men and two or three tribes
of aborigines to feed, 282 were killed
from one burning of skins to another
—eight months. Fancy the land kings
nowadays throwing the hides away on the
odd occasion on which they kill a beast ! They
would never be able to pay those £6OOO cheques
to land agents if they indulged in such waste.

“D-H.11.” : The alleged benefits accruing
from the Shearers’ Accommodation Act are
not very apparent. Round this (Walgett, N.S.W.)
district, most of the old huts were preferable to
the new ones. The old ones were built of slabs,
and with a little ingenuity on the part of the
shearers could be made fairly weather-tight, and
were always moderately warm. The new ones
are mostly built of galvanised iron, and, as
shearing commences hereabouts in July and early
in August, they are little better than refrigerating
chambers. The shearers and shed hands are
constantly affected with bronchitis and other lung
troubles. There are no fireplaces in the huts, and
as the floors are mostly boarded, the manager
especially stipulates that no oil-drums or other
improvised fireplaces shall be used.
Nulla : ‘ C.H.R.” says “all Tasmanian
lizards are harmless.” But, barring a rare Mexi-
can and S. American lizard, are not all legged
reptiles so ? That is, as far as poison glands are
concerned. I know the go.’s bite often turns into
a nasty sore, but the irritation is probably caused
by the virus on the teeth, as in the case of a rat’s
bite. Solomon Islanders poison their arrows by
sticking them into a decaying body, a chief’s for
preference. Well, what price a go.’s teeth ? Con-
sidering the carrion he surrounds, he should be
deadlier than a deat|i-adder.

“The Prooshan” about an unusual
animal
Some time in the seventies, the proprietor of Mumble-
dool Station purchased a swamp buffalo bull and cow at
one of the Aquariums, trucked them to Devlin’s Siding,
and had them driven to the station. The bull was a low-
set model of a beast, and hairless, save on the point of
his tail, which resembled a pig’s. He was noted for other
special qualifications of course, the chief being his love of
water, and a strong smell, peculiar to his kind and not
unpleasant altogether.
One day, Rambulate, the overseer, struck a traveller
making a fire in the lane to boil the billy. His mate was
just coming back at full speed from the dam, his hat
gripped in one hand, an empty can in the other, and
with wild eyes and hair on end. He declared he’d seen
“ the orfulest blanky thing in the blanky tank a man could
clap eyes on, come up outer the water, just where I leans
down to dip the billy, and sniffs at me.” “Pooh, that
wouldn’t hurt you, that’s only a buriyip,” said Rambulate,
but it was a long time before he could coax the man over
to the dam. Then, stamping on the bank, he shouted,
“ Billy Billy ! ” Whereupon the buffalo climbed out and
started towards him, but the swaggies shifted like
greased lightning, and, gathering their substance, fled.
Some weeks after, a drover camped on the station-
fence with 490 cattle. Rambulate said to the man in
charge: “ There’s a buffalo bull in that paddock, and if
he takes it into his head to come down to-night look
out.” Drover said “ He’d like to see the bloomin’ buffalo
that ’ud shift hie camp ; ” but about 2 a.m. that bull did,
come down, like a smell on the fold, and a stampede fol-
lowed on a very magnificent scale. After the fragments
were collected, and the news passed round, drovers
camped elsewhere.
One day, after Billy had been on the run six months, a
new hand started ringing in Bill’s paddock, all unaware
of the bull’s presence, and was working round a big tree,
when, hearing a sniff behind, he looked round and con-
fronted William. His dart for home was meteoric, and,
on meeting the overseer, he said : “ By ghost, I got a
nice fright up there to-day. Yer might ’er told me about
that blanky injy-rubber bull yer got there—frightened
hell outer me.”
• » • *
“T. A.”: The S.A. Cold Water partyis right
when it claims that there are too many licensed
houses in the State. Nearly every country town pos-
sesses unnecessary pubs.—mournful, desolate piles,
generally kept by disappointed pessimists, and
frequented by the village dead-beat and a dissi-
pated dog. They are the monuments of the beery
borrowing days, when the country was too irre-
sponsible or too drunk to worry about details, and
much of Cohen’s “ little allowance ” went into the
till of Bung. Port Augusta, the dead northern
seaport, is the awful example. It possesses 11
pubs., paying among them about £7 10s. in weekly
rent. Some of the “licensed caretakers” pay no
rent. In the “good old days,” every house did a
flourishing trade. To-day, trade is rapidly be-
coming a matter of barter.

“M.A.0.U.”: “San Remo” is correct
about morelia varigata (carpet snake) attaining
17ft. For a morelia that is not a record by
a long chalk. It is no novelty here (Cape
York) to be startled in the night by the
fowls, and on investigation to discover a monster
of 14ft. or 15ft. long with half-a-dozen birds com-
fortably packed away in its copious stomach. Our
record here is 19ft. sAin. in length, with a girth
measurement of 27in.
A temperance yarn :—-
A coterie of hard-faced fogies were discussing “ Ro-
mances of Fortune,” as suggested by the series of articles
in the Argus. One aged liar had just finished his con-
tribution to the entertainment, when a humorless-looking
person with a long wilderness of upper lip cut into the
talk :
“I know a darned sight better romance than that,” he
said. “You’ve all heard of Bill the Buster? Well, old
Bill used to go about the bush pretending that he was
prospecting—leastways, he did when he wasn’t drunk.
He never found nothing except once, and that was the
skeleton of a blaJc gin. Bill used to pitch fabulous stories
about the strikes he was going to make. While waiting
for them he was a sort of shocking example through over-
indulgence in liquor. This had gone on for years, and
although Bill never earned any money but a bob now and
again for odd jobs done for worthy citizens of the town,
he always proudly described himself in official documents
as a mining speculator. Well, at last the local constable,
the criminal season being kinder ebb-tide like, was
obliged to speak to him. ‘Bill,’ says he, with dignity
and sternness, ‘if you don’t sober and take some legiti-
mate exercise instanter and forthwith, I’ll be compelled
to do me unpleasant duty and run you in on a charge of
being a vagrant and a confirmed and unmitigated ine-
briate, contrary to the statutes, and with insulting
behavior to the force.’ Bill was staggered. ‘l’ll go out
to my mine in the morning,’ says he, ‘and pursue the
elusive goddess fortune with a pick.’
“ Next day Bill contrived to get hold of a time-payment
pick, shovel, and dish and slid into the first easy-looking
abandoned shaft he could find on the outskirts of the
field, to make a show of doing something and satisfy the
law. Bill started to pick at the bottom of the hole.
Presently the pick hooked into something. Now Bill’s
nerves wasn’t too good and the light in the shaft was dull,
and, as the thing on the pick kept coming and coming out
in great coils, Bill’s hair began to rise, tie had before
seen many varieties of snakes, but a reptile which seemed
as endless as a deep sea cable was anew experience. In
a clammy panic Bill broke the record in getting out of the
shaft, and fled wildly and blindly through the bush until
he dropped from exhaustion. Next day he took the
blessed pledge, and has never touched a drop of alcohol
since.
“ But the most curious thing is what happened next.
When Bill had fully regained his normal condition of
mind, he one day went back to the shaft to get his left
property and see if there really was a monstrous reptile
in it. He then found that on the previous occasion he
had unearthed a great length of rope left there by the
original owners of the mine. He also found that the side
of the shaft had fallen in, and Bill was electrified to see
the ragged edge of a rich gold lode exploded. Eventually
Bill floated the mine, called it the ‘ Jim Jam Block One,’
and from first to last took £91,427 3s. 9fd. out of it.”

FIT FOB BARNUM'S.
LITTLE BOY (reading the news): “Say, father ,
how many hands did you have when you
were a sailor ?”
The intelligence of the dog
sometimes seems almost supernatural. Hogan
belonged to a lodge composed entirely of perfervid
“ greens,” and Brown to one of equally rampant
“ yellows.” The lodges meet weekly at the local
town hall. Each man possesses a dog which
attends him to the lodge meetings, and (until
lately) if the animals chanced to meet there was
instant fight. But Brown (who was yellow) fell
in love with a colleen, and for her sake he ’verted
and joined Hogan’s' lodge of good Romans. The
first night that the ’verted Brown put in an
appearance at the new fold, Hogan's dog emerged
from under his master’s chair and, running eagerly
to Brown’s quadruped, welcomed him with every
sign of gratification; and in a short while the
pair were playing together in perfect amity, and
ready to make common cause against White’s dog
if it turned up.

From the Family Reader (England),
8/7/’O5 : —
Tarantulas are bred in Australia for the sake of their
webs, the filaments of which are made into threads for
balloons. They are much lighter than silk.
Possibly the Booth emigrants wish to come out to
our tarantula farms, having invented a process by
which the webs can be made into big-drum
material.
A CONVERSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A revival epidemic, like typhoid or the ’flu’,
Spread out as fat as Boiling- Downs, upon the far Barcoo.
Amongst the greatest converts who were brought to hope
and grace
Was Peter John Macpherson, who was owner of the place.
He owned all Boiling Downs, and all the land for mile 8
around,
And he saw his chances in the sky were anything but
sound.
When opening his Bible, he of other rich men read,
He marked with consternation all the things that volume
said.
To yield up all his riches was a thing he could not face,
So he questioned many parsons on the horror of the case—
Of the camel and the needle, and the things that they im-
plied ;
And he asked them of his prospects when he reached the
other side.
But he asked them with much cursing, and the parsons
mostly fled,
And said they would not gladly take his place when he
was dead.
They felt that harps and halos, and wings and golden
crowns,
Were not for men who swore like Peter John of Boiling
Downs. i-
. $ "
At last he found some comfort, for the Reverend Jonas
Bray , f :
Explained the Bible stcry in a soft, consoling way.
“ The needle was a gateway through which passed the
folks who sinned ;
Any camel .could get through it, if the beast were only
tinned.”
So Peter kept his riches, and he made no further search,
But went away in haste and joined himself unto the
church ;
But sometimes with anxiety his scanty locks are thinned
As he dreams that he may also have to go to heaven
tinned.
N.S.W.

MIXED POLITICS.


A scientific gentleman has discovered
that the world Is steadily growing colder,
and sooner or later the time must arrive
when the whole globe will be ice-bound.
The question then arises, 'how Is
any kind of life to be preserved ? But
Mr. Martin, the savant in question, re-
minds us that alcohol congeals at a much
lower temperature than water ; thus. If
alcohol could be substituted for the water
which constitutes the greatest portion of
living matter, it might be possible to
prolong the existence of life for a few
hundred thousand years after the Oulf
Stream had become a glacier.
Well, that's something to took forward
to I How delicious It will be a few years
hence to sit on a nice cool glacier In Syd-
ney Harbor and keep yourself alive with'
good, wholesome, nourishing grog, and
to know that all the never-touch-lt
cranks and prohibition agitators had been
frozen out long ago.
What an age' to live In !

CHIEFLY IN N.S.W.


George Reid’s misreading of Opposition to
mean Obstruction—with a big o—makes him an
unconscious incentive to the abolition of Party
'Government. The lolly-sucker never admits that
there should be only one party in the Common-
wealth, the party of D.C.L.—the whisky of that
ilk being the principal power in expanding the
principle of progress. The D.C.L. brand doesn’t
appeal forcibly to George. It is the Whisky of
the Wise, and he will never be wise.*
PERSONAL ITEMS.
What a world of difference between Peter
Close and Peter Open !
Ethel Mills, the best' short-story writer
among the literary women Australia has pro-
duced, is now in London doing well. She has
had short stories accepted by the Pall Mall and
other magazines,
“Fifty years ago I was a member of a
church choir,” said Vic. Premier Bent the other
day. He added that “Praise God from Whom
All Blessings Flow ” is his favorite hymn, and
Tommy regrets it is not so much sung now as it
used to be.
Speaker Cowley, of Queensland Assembly,
is a man of endurance. During the recent all-
night debate on the Land Monopoly Tax Bill he
didn’t once rise from the chair from 7 o’clock at
night till the same hour in the morning, when the
House adjourned for breakfast.
Tommy Burgess, claiming to be the oldest
Australian-born compositor, died lately at the
Royal Park Institution for old men (Vic.). He was
a lightning type-setter, but saw visions, and was
well established in the mussel and periwinkle
trade long before the lino, invaded his calling.
Mr. E. Riley, appointed Labor represen-
tative to the Arbitration Court, has had a useful
Union career in Victoria and N.S. Wales. He is
Scotch by birth, like his predecessor, and is a
sensible, moderate man, who will undoubtedly be
a success in the Court if Joseph fails to assassinate
it.
Little Cohen (S.A.) has climbed half way
towards the summit of his ambition, for he is now
Deputy Leader of the Opposition. He is a ver-
satile young man, for although a Jew he once led
a Wesleyan choir in Fiji. It is almost necessary
to have led a choir of some sort to succeed in S.A.
politics.
London Spectator announces the appear-
ance, in its pages, of a series of “How it
Strikes an Australian ” articles, by young Aus-
tralian author J. H. M. Abbott, son of late
Speaker Abbott (N.S.W.). Abbott has done
excellent work in journalism, and wrote the best
Australian book on the S’African war.
Ancient Captain William Jackson Barry,
of Maoriland (the man who rode on a whale), has
been left a handsome fortune by a relative who
died in Hampshire (Eng.), so he will be able to
spend his declining days in comfort. Barry’s idea
of comfort consists in bringing out another book
of hoary reminiscences.
Nigger labor advocate GOVERNOR LE HUNTE,
of S.A., and the HON. “LITTLE RED
RIDING ” HOOD , his secretary.
The removal of the remains of W. C.
Wentworth from Vaucluse, near Sydney, is pro-
posed, owing to the subdivision and sale of the
property. The “ Father of the N.S.W. Constitu-
tion ” died in England, but the body was taken
out and buried at \ aucluse to satisfy local senti-
ment. James Martin’s speech over the grave of
“the first of first-born Australians” was declared
by those who heard it one of the finest orations
delivered in Australia.
The lowly Christian modesty of some
people is a beautiful thing. The four pictured
“Men of the Hour” in “ Bleeds ” Fitchett’s Life
for September appear in this order: W. H.
Fitchett, LL.D., editor of Life ; W. W. Jacobs,
author of “ Many Cargoes ” ; the late John Hay,
ex-TJ.S. Secretary of State ; the Hon. Elihu Boot,
John Hay’s successor in the U.S. Cabinet. But
quite a number of things in the same issue aren’t
about Fitchett.
Jeremiah Twomey, ex-M.L.C., and pro-
prietor of Temuka Leader, apologised to the
squad of New Liberals (Maoriland’s “ New Libel ”
party), after they had, sued him for £lOOO apiece
for defamatory remarks in an article called “ The
Soiled Doves ”; also, Jeremiah paid all exes.
Twomey had faced an action brought by another
politician just previously. Quite lately it was
Jerry’s boast that he hadn’t had a libel suit in
22 years. He has since had five in about five
weeks.
The Glen Innes (N.S.W.) school exhibit
tion, at which Inspector S. H. Smith will consti-
tute a camp of teachers., and give daily expositions
of how to conduct a wayback school, recalls the
exploits of C. Wesley Caddy, afterwards a Sydney
journalist, in a similar capacity in the Palace
Garden grounds at the great Sydney Exhibition.
Caddy, a highly efficient country teacher, was
brought to Sydney by the Department to show
visitors to the Exhibition how the master of a
country school performed his duties. He held an
imaginary school and taught imaginary children,
but he was a great success as a show. Few Sydney
people had seen a bush schoolmaster in harness,
and they took Caddy’s burlesque quite seriously.
So did the authorities for that matter.
Sam Smith, late workers’ representative
on the Arbitration Court Bench, now very ill, has
been in politics one way and another since he was
14 years of age, one of his duties at that period
being to call together the colliers for meetings in
a Scotch village. Ever since, on sea and land, he
has helped labor movements wherever he found
them. He is still under 50.
Some birthdays in October are : Editor
Bonython, M.H.R. (S.A.), 57 on the 15th ; Judge
Hartley Williams (Vic), 62 on the 15th; James
Fairfax (N.S.W.), 71 on the 17th; ex-wicket*
keeper A. H. Jarvis, 45 on the 18th; and Presi-
dent H. J. Wrixon (Vic.), 66 on the same day ;
Kingston, M.H.R. (S.A.), 55 on the 22nd; ex-
Premier Neil Lewis (Tasmania). 47 on the 27th;
ex-Judge Boucaut (S.A.), 74 on the 29th.
Died at Mosman (Sydney), the other day,
aged 65, W. W. Johnson, timber merchant, rail-
way contractor, pastoralist, mine-lessee, news-
paper proprietor, steamship-owner, and other
things too numerous to mention. He constructed
the tramway from Newcastle to Wallsend ; held
stations in the Scone and Singleton districts, and
was lessee of the Cardiff (N.S.W.) colliery. He
was a pioneer of the steamer traffic on Lake Mac-
quarie ; also he was part proprietor of the New-
castle Herald and a director of the Wallsend Gas
Co.
A Victorian National League speaker,
advertised as a University she, expatiates at
length, but to little purpose, on the evils of
Socialism. One inquisitive country person one
night casually inquired of what University she
was Bachelor of Arts. The dame had to own up
that she had never taken a degree of any sort,
and that the cap and gown she wore denoted
nothing but a desire to look learned and superior.
The Stinking Fish, anti-Socialistic party seems
unreliable even in its clothes.
There must have been a spacious curse
somewhere about the family or person of the late
unlamented heroine of the Richmond (Vic.)
tragedy—the dwarf, Rosina Hubbard. First, her
sister's baby died of suspected poisoning; then
her brother, ditto ; then she poisoned her mother;
then her lawyer met with an accident and lay
in the hospital for weeks; then the solicitor,
who took up his legal brother’s case, suicided ;
finally, Rosina herself passed out. Now the
lawyers who are fighting over her will feel them-
selves carefully every morning for unhealthy
symptoms.
So far the N.S.W. Lands Commission has
dealt with 91 payments to land-agents. Of these
W. N. Willis, who left for South Africa, is said to
have collected 51; Peter Close, who rounded on
his alleged mate, seems to have got 23; Charles
Bath, who wouldn’t explain who signed the bill
for £l5O, has five ascribed to him; and R. D,
Meagher, who wants to get back into the honor-
able profession of the law, is credited with four.
The other eight are divided among six men. The
biggest fee mentioned (£5554) is charged to Willis ;
so is the smallest (£10), which shows a great mind
able to deal with large things and also to descend
to infinite detail.
A story which may be true :
David Syme, of the Agb, has been interesting himself
in spiritualism lately, and his gaunt, hard figure and
Carlylean face have been conspicuous at recent Melbourne
seances where a potent wizard produced relics from Troy,
articles of interest from old Babylon, and brick records
from ancient Egyptian tombs, along with such modern
trifles as weird fresh fish wet from the sea, and curious
Indian bakements hot from the original oven. Syme
beheld these things with Caledonian "caution. Then the
other night he arose, and asked the Indian spirit then
under control to produce an Indian vernacular paper of
that day’s date. The spirit discovered a hitch in his
business arrangements. He asked if the gentleman
wouldn’t prefer a pickled herring from Yarmouth or a
cockchafer from Thebes, as his Indian communications
were cut or something. Davy persisted in a sad, Scotch
way, saying he wanted the paper badly. Then the spirit
got angry, and said it would see him in a helmet first, or
sounds like that, and a Caledonian stern and wild drifted
from the seance, saying it would be a verra gude show if
it were a great deal better.
Melbourne Herald lately fossicked out
the oldest Melbourne natives, three of whom are
Mrs W. Hogg, of Inverell (N.S.W.), born at
Captain Brown’s station, eight miles from Mel-
bourne, July 13, ’4l; Mrs. K. T. Churchman,
born where Richmond Town Hall now stands,
January 1, ’42; and Mr. W. Cheney, born on
what is now the Royal Arcade, in January, ’42.
There doesn’t seem anything remarkable about
these elderly persons except that they made an
early start, so to speak. Possibly they have heard
more about the “great natural resources” than
the rest of the citizens; and doubtless the
“Socialistic trend of existing legislation” has
grieved them more and more every year. Cer-
tainly, Mrs. Hogg was stolen by blacks when she
was a baby a day old, but as those harmless
nonentities returned her as a useless encumbrance
the day following, she had barely time to catch a
cold, or any other complaint.
Water-jug Foster’s long endurance was at
the bottom of a good deal of the feeling shown
during the discussion in N.S.W. Assembly upon
Waddell s Bill for the abolition of j udicial pensions.
But it should have been remembered, on the other
hand, that several Judges—such as Windeyer—
died promptly on coming into pension, and most
Judges died in office. Whether the system is
right or wrong, the length of the pensioner’s life
should not figure as an argument one way or the
Other. When Gavan Duffy proposed to leave
politics, the Vic. Parliament was prepared to pass
him a gratuity of £5OOO ; but Gavan’s friends said
his health was so bad that a couple of years would
end him, so a pension of £lOOO a year was substi-
tuted as a matter of economy. Gavan drew
the amount regularly for some 40 years. This
looked like getting at the public, but it was merely
an incident in a gamble—the public might have
won.
Thomas Woolner, pre-Raphaelite sculptor
and poet, mentioned as an old-time Melbourne
resident (B. 7/9/’O5), wrote to English friends in
1853 that he had “a good prospect of getting a
statue of the Queen to do for Melbourne.” A
hitch arose in the arrangements, and Melbourne
managed to do without Her Majesty’s statue
(which James White now has under way) for 32
years. Woolner came to Australia with another
sculptor, the late Bernard Smith, members of
whose family still live in Victoria. Like most
immigrants of the period, Woolner and Smith put
in a spell at the diggings, but they had no luck.
Melbourne National Gallery contains four of
Woolner’s busts and medallions. When he re-
turned to London in 1854, he was promised the
commission for a statue of W. C. Wentworth
(playfully called “Wentworth the Lag” by Ford
Madox Brown, who records the incident in his
diary). After various delays, another sculptor
got the job. W. M. Rossetti, annotating Brown’s
diary, surmises that “the Lag” refers to Went-
worth’s lagging habits. But all Australians were
‘ ‘ lags ” of another kind in the slap-dash estimation
of English writers half-a-century ago.
THE DEVELOPMENT OF AUSTRALIA.—A TORY DREAM.
The Victorian Eight Hours pioneers are a
long-lived brigade. Next year the jubilee of the
movement will be celebrated in Victoria. Of the 52
pioneers only 16 are on record as having “ crossed
the bar.” Great preparations are being made for
the celebration of the 1906 anniversary, and Labor-
leader Prendergast is compiling a history of the
movement.
Some anthropological observations. In
proportion to stature, Spruce Myth has the
smallest foot in the House of Representatives,
Willimotor Kelly the largest. O’Malley is the
hairiest man, Higgins the baldest. The largest
noses (among the Gentiles) are those of Wilks and
Glynn, and the biggest mouth is hidden by the
whiskers on the face of ex-parson Ronald. John
Forrest has the greatest weight and the thickest
neck, and Pumpkin Smith has the most remark-
able beard.
The relatives of an Australian Judge, now
dead, say that, during the last days of his life,
there arrived, addressed to him, many letters in
which the writers stated how glad they were at
his Honor’s sufferings and approaching death, and
stated that there was no doubt that his Honor
would go to hell. Almost the last words of the
Judge were : “ Painful as my duties were in many
instances, I believe I am leaving behind me no
enemies.” So little do some men know what other
men think of them.
J. T. Walker and W. J. Lyne are the only
active N.S.W. politicians of the Federal Conven-
tion who stand up with fair consistency for the
Constitution they agreed to and helped to devise.
Reid, even when in power, sneaks out ways to
undermine the Commonwealth and enhance the
power of the States, his Own. State in particular ;
Carruthers spends his sour and uncivil soul o’
nights searching for imaginary grievances against
the Australian Parliament; Brunker thinks the
new-fangled system? awfully bad for N.S.W., his
cow; M‘Millan sulks, snaps and snarls; Abbott
is dead; Wise is abroad; and Barton and O’Connor
have left politics.
On one of the small old mining townships
in Victoria there is a man who, some years back,
went with a party of zealots of both sexes to assist
in converting the Chow to Christianity. When
he left his native place he was a young fellow of
fine physique and cheerful disposition. He re-
turned a mental and physical wreck, and nbw the
very sight of a Chinaman either throws him into a
fit, or brings on an outbreak of lunacy. Then the
poor wretch displays abject terror, and runs to
hide himself, screaming pitiably. Hidden under
a bed he witnessed the revolting massacre of a
mission party by Chinese fanatics during one of
the risings a short time back, and his present con-
dition is the result of that experience.
John Mathieson, former Commissioner of
Kanakaland, and afterwards of Victorian rail-
ways, seems to have settled down very comfort-
ably to his position as general manager of the
Midland line (England). Since taking up the
work his own salary has been increased by a thou-
sand or two, and now stands at about £4500,
whilst his company the other day decided to give
him a lieutenant at £2OOO a year. While the
Midland mileage of lines is not astounding, the
train mileage beats that of all Australia put toge-
ther, and, as Mr. Mathieson explains, the greater
the number of trains, and tlm less the line accom-
modation, the more complex the job. Also the
joint use of lines with other companies is the
biggest trial English managers have. John
Mathieson, in fact, avers that his old billets in
Australia were just child’s-play when compared
with his present one. At the same time, he
carries responsibility well, and is as spruce as ever.
In pursuance of its recent penny ’orrible
policy, the Argus published last week a bush--
ranging story of the roaring AO’s, for the particular
purpose, perhaps, of introducing the name of Peter
Snodgrass, father of Mrs. Janet Clarke. The
paternal Snodgrass is described as a Melbourne
clubman, and an “adventurous spirit ” who fought
two duels without drawing his adversary’s gore.
In one case he was so valorously excited that he
fired downwards by accident, and put a bullet
into his own big toe. However, Snodgrass helped
to kill a bushranger in the Argus yarn (his aim
being fairly accurate at very short range), and
after the surviving desperadoes had been cornered,
he and the other adventurous spirits signed a
paper promising them “lenient treatment” by
the Law. This document proved worthless,
legally and otherwise. The three prisoners were
finally carted to the gallows, each seated on his
coffin, and in the presence of an admiring Mel-
bourne populace they were hanged by the neck.
If they got any lenient treatment it must have
been in the next world.
The report that Nicholas Willis has gone
amissing in South Africa seems to blow through
the atmosphere once a day these times. When
last heard of, Nicholas had made limited progress
at convincing a judge in Natal that the warrant
sent over for him was bad, but he had got his bail
reduced. People who know the one-time bush
Nicholas reckon that if he really wants to go he
will go, and that if he doesn’t want the local
police to find him they won’t find him. Nicholas
is still a mighty active man on the kopje and the
veldt, and trifles like a kloof or a donga are
nothing to him.
Captain Ernest Gaunt, who has been
appointed to the second-class cruiser Cambrian,
newly-commissioned to the Australian station, is
a son of Judge Gaunt, of Yictoria. He joined the
navy in 1878, and, as Lieutenant, was Commis-
sioner at Wei-hai-Wei (China), and Administrator
of the Province in 1898-99. For his services
during the Boxer unpleasantness he was thanked
by the Austrian and German Commanders-in-
Chief. Later he went to Somaliland what time
the Mad Mullah was very mad thereabouts, and
commanded the landing party from the Mohawk
which “ avenged ” the death of Italian naval
officer Lieut. Graban. For this he received an
Italian military medal, and was thanked by the
Italian Chamber of Deputies. So he can fairly be
classed as a much-thanked man. It isn’t every
man of his age to whom three great Powers have
addressed a few well-chosen remarks. Another
son of Judge Gaunt is also a navy man—Com-
mander Guy Gaunt, of the Cressy, stationed at
Portsmouth.
Ex-barrister Richard Colonna Close, who
died of heart failure at Sydney, the other day,
aged 65, was a native of England, and an Oxford
University graduate. He began life as an Angli-
can parson in charge of Pitton chapel, Devon-
shire, and was afterwards chaplain to the Life
Guards (Blue). He was an excellent linguist, and
during a lengthy spell in Russia was Professor of
Languages at St. Petersburg University, and
tutor of English to the late Czar Alexander and
the Grand Dukes Sergius and Vladimir. He
began his Australian career in a Brisbane pulpit,
and later went to Melbourne to edit the
Federal Australian. Next, he moved to
Sydney, and was called to the Bar in 1882, but
some time back he was disbarred at his own re-
quest, and admitted as a solicitor. The Law
didn’t treat him too well in either branch. He
was an earnest Federationist, a strong supporter
of the old age pension principle, and something of
a local institution.
Violent language from Melbourne :
Tom Bent, seeking opportunities of exposing himself as
a long-eared animal, never misses any events connected
with musical and literary competition that come off
within a comfortable motor run of the Vic. metropolis,
and of all the follies with which untoward success and
newspaper flapdoodle have swelled the Bentian head, the
idea that he is a connoisseur of poetry and general litera-
ture and a reliable musical guide is the most lamentable.
Bent on a public platform, singing snatches of execrable
songs in a voice like a cross between the complaint of a
scorched dog and the lament of a bull calf, is only a little
less wonderful than the same massive person giving the
public points about literature and literary taste. He even
makes up wedges of shameless verse, and fires them off
with a complacent fatuity that sets the people shrieking
with laughter, although it never- seems to dawn on Mr.
Premier that he is being laughed at because he is a ridicu-
lous object doing monstrously idiotic things. It is now
quite a popular joke to lure Bent to prize distributions in
connection with these competitions,, and pull his leg till
he gets up and sings, or recites, or tells senile anecdotes
about his old-time theatrical excursions. When you hear
an unusually hoarse and horrid roar in the vicinity of
Melbourne now-a-times, you know it is either put up by
the crowd at an exciting football match or by the push
fooling Mr. Premier to the top of his Bent.
“ Talking of bushrangers,” said an M.L.O.
the other night in the N.S.W. Parliamentary
library, “ always brings up to me poor old Hoddle
White. He sat beside me over there in the
Council for years, and the last conversation we
had before the great shadow fell on him was about
the sticking-up at Deniliquin. White, as you
know, was there in the Bank of New South Wales,
the Deniliquin branch, and the ’rangers fell on
him armed to the teeth. They roped him up, and
made off with £8000; but blest if the young
fellow didn’t break free and make after them, and,
what is more, he took back most of the money and
landed the outlaws into the hands of the police.
He did something almost as remarkable on the
Gympie road, in- Queensland, on another occasion.
He was a white man every inch, and the pink of
honor ih sport. I think I was the first to notice
anything wrong with his mind. Over a oup of
tea in the refreshment room he persisted in calling
his famous Oup horse by the name of his yacht.
He wasn’t drinking, and I set him right a couple
of times, but he had the ideas twisted, and I left
him, not knowing what to say. That was fully
six months before anyone thought of Callan
Park.”
Holland is the latest country to propose
the adoption of the universal militia service
system of army defence, which is just now being
urged in Australia. European peoples generally
are finding that conscription is too burdensome,
and even France now moves towards the Swiss
militia system.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 3, 1905.
My Dear Arini,—
Lord Alastair Mungo Graham, the prize 19-
year youngster of the Euryalus, was carefully
hedged round during his stay in Sydney.
He’s a son of the house of Montrose, and
painfully valuable. Lady Fanshawe feared
an Australian alliance. And she had good
reason. Pretty Mrs. Forbes Sempill used to
chaperone the most dangerous damsel, who is
also very pretty. Those shore luncheons worried
Admiralty House, and the lordling was invited to
stop there during the few days the Euryalus
would be at Port Melbourne. He declined
without any show of regret. Consequence: he
was commanded to leave when the Euryalus did.
And that was next morning.
Mr. Peter Close, whose evidence before the
Lands Commission caused such sensation, is a
well-known social figure. His wife raises Persian
cats. She is a daughter of the late Judge Joseph-
son, and married first a Mr. Bowyer, a school-
master. Some daughters (Bowyers), small, dark
and foreign-looking, adorn the Close mansion.
One is married to a fashionable doctor.
Pilcher, K.C., who had the (doubtless) disagree-
able task of putting the painful questions, is a
personal friend of the family.
Acting-Governoress Darley floats about opening
little shows and presenting prizes, and generally
filling in the vice-regal gap till the Rawsons arrive.
Her sister, Mrs. Cockshott, assists. All four
“Rolf Boldrewood” Browne ladies made what a
London Society paper calls ‘ ‘ charming marriages.”
The other two are Lady Peter Scratchley and
Mrs. Molesworth Greene.
Miss Eadith Walker has been giving the pina-
fored and knickerbockered members of Pointer
families a taste of her hospitality. Sixty young-
sters assembled at “Yaralla,” and the party
proceeded along acquisition lines. When the
guests weren’t taking food they were ac-
quiring presents. Small spades were handed
to them, and they dug for buried treasure—nor
did they dig in vain. A small Milner Stephen,
dressed as a postman, delivered letters that had
come through the Concord post-office addressed to
every little guest. There was no scamping of
details. And every letter contained a present of
value. Miss Walker goes to Europe in February,
and takes with her the nice-looking girl, Miss
Atkinson, who is always about with her these
days.
A globe-trotting lady who headed for Europe
some time ago left behind her a record for
generosity mixed with severity. I presume
she had what may be called the Spasmodic
Temperament, Ax all events, during her stay in
Australia, she adopted a girl of financially-decayed
parentage, and made her life a dream of happi-
ness. The girl had only one moderately present-
able dress when the dream commenced, but she
immediately had ten, and in addition it rained
bangles, and pocket-money, and motor-car rides,
and theatres. With it all, the adopted daughter
was ruled with a strong hand, and the respect and
obedience due to a parent were rigidly exacted
from her. Still it rained as I already mentioned,
and almost anything on earth that the new daugh-
ter asked for (with the respect due to a parent)
came her way. But at last she forgot the respect
due to a parent, and, instead of asking for the
11th pretty frock, she ordered it herself. The
adopted mother said nothing, but there was a cold
look in her eye. Two days later, the girl came
back to the hotel from an outing to find the
adopted mother gone. The beautiful clothes and
jewellery were gone—all except what the girl had
on her and the worn dress which reminded her of
the days of her poverty. The hotel bill was paid,
and on the mantel-shelf were two articles—a letter
which spoke severely about the sin of disobedi-
ence, and sixpence to cover tram-fares back to the
decayed parental roof.
I want to meet a woman who has good eyes yet
does not roll them ceiling-ward every time
you speak to her. A minute ago I asked a fine-
eyed woman the time. Her eyes sought the roof
while her hand went to her belt where her watch
was ambushed. Then, after reluctantly dropping
her gaze on the dial, she again looked heavenward
arid replied “eleven.” Sometimes a pig-eyed
lady fancies she has soulful saucer orbs and pro-
ceeds to do devotional antics with them. Then I
feel as if I want to be horrid with a hat-pin.
Latest matrimonial engagement of interest is
that of Mr. Ashley Teece, a son of the A.M. Pac-
tuary, to Miss Giblin, a Tasmanian girl. Bride-
groom-elect is studying for the Church. Talking
of. weddings: three Sydney girls are to marry
men-o’-warsmen next month. Miss Mosely,
Miss Gollin, and Miss Reading—last is one of
the Fairfax-Readings, and Mrs. James Fairfax
will give her a reception at the mansion on windy
Bellevue Hill, where even the earth has to be
nailed down, lest it should blow away.
Sydney University Dramatic Society gave a
performance of “The School for Scandal ” at the
Palace Theatre last week. The matter is too
painful for further mention.
A man who signs himself “Ne’er-do-Weel,”
and who writes like it, sends me this:—
Of all her insane notions, woman’s craze for having her
clothes fastened where she can’t get at the fastenings
seems to be the insanest. A registry-office keeper told
me that a woman, badly in want of work, refused a good
place as housekeeper to an old couple because, “ there
bein’ no servants kep’, there’s no one I could ask to button
up me blouses.” In a tram, a few days ago, noticed the
woman in the seat in front of me had four buttons of her
blouse undone ; result, about one square foot of bare skin,
stays and frilly things. She was young and pretty and
serenely unconscious. I hesitated for a moment. I re-
membered “ Do unto others as you would they should
do unto you,” and I tried to imagine myself a young
and pretty girl with my stays on public exhibition. Then I
begged her pardon (the hypocrisy of man !), told her what
was the matter, and proffered my assistance. She smiled,
blushed most charmingly, thanked me, and accepted, and
I covered her nakedness and other things. But next time,
When I offered assistance, the lady froze me with an icy
glare, stopped the car, and got off, the sun glinting on
her undressed parts as she stepped down. Which shows
the variable nature of the sex.
That obnoxious word of the marriage service
—“obey”—has been excised by the Method-
ist Episcopal Church. Henceforth a bride of
that faith will be called upon to “love, honor
and keep” her husband, and if she doesn’t
want to obey they can arbitrate. The substitu-
tion of “keep” for “obey” may lead to more
trouble than the original obnoxious phrase. Men
of inactive temperament will say that “keep”
means to maintain, not to retain, a husband. Some
of the 80,000 men that Coghlan and Dr. Arthur
say are in excess of women in Australia may now
marry—women of the Methodist Episcopal label.
An Australian, polishing her notes in London,
tells_me that a lady at whose parties she has been
singing, on gratis principles, promised her “a
treat.” The promise, when fulfilled, took the
form of a dinner at the famous hotel known as
Claridge’s. As the girl has to follow the strictest
of dietary rules, the treat didn’t amount to much.
Dry biscuits, apples, and a table water made up
her meal ; but, she adds, “ I was interested in
three men and two women at an adjacent
table. Didn’t notice them till one sympa-
thetic waiter told us that they were Mr. William
Rockefeller, the Standard Oil magnate, Mr.
Westinghouse (the famous brake man), and Mr.
George Gould. The ladies were Mrs. Rockefeller
and Mrs. Gould —the most plainly dressed women
in the dining-hall. A third lady joined them at
coffee. The duke who waited on us didn’t know
her, but opined that she was an Australian.
‘ When she goes out driving she carries an Aus-
tralian parrot chained to her wrist, and
amongst her luggage is a wallababy.’ But to
return to the millionaires. There were five of
these gold bugs at the hotel at the one
time the three mentioned and a Vander-
bilt and a Whitney. A daily paper told
London next morning that when the lift boy
hoisted the quintette he was lifting something
that represented £50,000,000 sterling. With
servants of various grades they swamped 30 rooms.
I may mention that my hostess has quite dropped
me for talking to the waiter. When she pointed
out (in the carriage on the way home) that no
English gentlewoman would behave so, all would
have been well if I had seemed penitent, but I
asked ‘ Well, if I hadn’t behaved so where would
the treat have coma in ? ’ I sing no more at her
parties ! ”
When a departing amateur artiste gives a con-
cert in the Town Hall, how the expenses eat
into the takings. Rent of hall, £2O; erecting the
temporary timber platform, £110s.; stopping the
chimes, 10s. ; and eight attendants (no matter if
the victim wants only three) at ss. each. And
the money has to be paid by noon on the day of
the benefit, else the doors won’t open.
Mrs. Yorke Syme, sister of our own Yes-No,
and wearing her famous diamond buckles, is in
Sydney, to attend the marriage of her niece, Miss
Ewan, who to-morrow weds Dr. Hall, of Parra-
matta. Then four Ewan girls will have married
medical men. Another visitor here is Mrs. C. 0.
Lance, whose husband ran lectures and glorified
magic lanterns in England in the interests of this
continent. Mrs. Lance will be remembered as Miss
Christie Fuller, a favorite pupil of Madame
Christian.
Madame Maggie Stirling, looking very fresh
and unchanged (save for the better), in a
Clamorous blue hat and grey serge tailor-
made, discourses modestly (over her aprieot-
tait and black coffee at the Australia) on
the kindness of ex-Covernors and consorts to her.
Actually an ex-Governor has his uses. The
Ha. ; p lens especially always gave her the close
han 1 clasp. Madame Maggie has the latest news
of the Australian colony in England. For the
rest, she opens her Sydney season to-night, and
this afternoon the Highland Society puts on its
kilts and gives her a bagpipe and haggis reception.
Miss Eva Champion, the fluffy little Maori-
lander, raised higher the already high hopes of
friends for her future prospects by her singing at
Mrs. Quesnel’s concert.
Two picturesque harpists at Amateur Orchestral
concerts are Signor Hazon’s daughters, Eugenia
and Rita, aged 16 and 13 respectively.
Mr. Jack Scantlebury, who is now part-owner
of the Ebitish Australasian, comes back here
before Christmas to fix up for correspondence.
Re fascination of legs. Heard a man say that
he had gone to the recent performance of “The
Bohemian Girl” just to see Miss Yiolet Mount in
the quite short skirts of Arline, the gipsy girl.
His opinion was that “ they ” were very straight
and pretty. He didn’t mean the skirts, you know.
Actor Albert Gran, who opens here with Tittell
Brune on the 14th, is walking from Brisbane
to Sydney. He is not sobbing, or begging, his
way—he’s just padding the hoof for the sake of
exercise and new scenery, and perhaps advt.
(which is hereby given), and at intervals he sends
wires to the management of Her Majesty’s brag-
ging of the number of miles he has flung behind
him since last wire, and telling how his boots have
gone back on him.
Hobson, the stockily-built actor, who plays
the part of George, in the “Fires of St.
John,” used to be Miss Nance O’Neil’s brother-
in-law. He was Miss Lillie Lamson’s first
husband, but the pair proved to be not com-
patible in disposition. Only that and nothing
more. They were just not accurately mated, and
the marriage bond tore in consequence. Now
there is a new brother-in-law, but Miss O’Neil’s
opinion of the ex-one is of the highest.
The best house of Miss O’Neil’s season in Sydney
was that drawn by “The Fires of St. John.”
Very few persons seem to have read the drama,
and the rush of Society hoping to be almost
scorched by the “Fires” nearly knocked down
the box office. The moral law was challenged,
but not so obviously as Society and a crowded
giggling gallery expected. A man at the back of
the d.c. summarised the opinion of many when
he said loudly, “Look here, the whole of this play
is a milk biscuit compared with the balcony scene
in ‘Romeo and Juliet’ or with ‘Mrs. Tanqueray.’”
And then he breathed hard through his nose.
A certain theatre manager, now departed, once
told me that “he couldn’t stand Dobson and
Cummerbund and them sort of playwrights.” He
meant Ibsen and Sudermann, I believe. I don’t
think there is a dramatist named Cummerbund,
nor one called Dado, but I feel somehow that
there should be. Anyhow, the writers in question
gave way, on Saturday night, at Her Majesty’s, to a
farce on the stage and to Mrs. H. Kelly’s Japanese
theatre coat in the stalls. No one wanted the
farce to start while they had that kimono for an
objective. The wearer obligingly made an excur-
sion or tw’o so that the back of the vivid crimson
garment Was on show. It expounded at the top
of its voice, in gold and silk emblazonery, the siege
of Port Arthur from a Jap. view-point, a dragon, a
cock-fight, Fuji Yama and chrysanthemums. But
it suited the Canadian immensely. One of the
Harry Rickards family got a novel effect from
concentric rings of coral in her back hair. They
hung from either end of a jewelled spike run
horizontally through the knob. Two (apparently)
sisters had turquoise combs in hair that was like
the blackest of black velvet. The blue drew the
eye to the black and vice versa, and evoked some
admiration. The Frank Gardners were boxed
with the J. C. Williamsons. In evening fluffery
and jewellery, and showing a perfect neck, the
French visitor is a beautiful creature. The
Gardners were to have started for the pleasant
land of France and Frank’s automobile works last
week, but the illness of their child delayed them.
Whilst artist Frank McComas (who married
recently a Californian belle) and his bride were at
breakfast, a morning or so after the wedding, a
Bohemian friend of his who considered he had
been coldly received by the artist, shut a.kangaroo
in the room. The ’roo bounded in a way which
put to shame the bounder responsible for its
appearance, and there were some hysterics.
McComas writes to a Sydney friend that he is
going to buy a place in England and make that
country his home.
You’ve heard of the Potts Point ’bus? It’s
driver is much loved by Potts Pointers. When
he wants your fare he just slips his hand down
through a hole. A lady, who had taken a monthly
ticket, asked him, “How shall I let you know I
am in the ’bus ? ” “ Just squeeze my hand, Mum,
instead of putting a penny in it. That’s how the
ladies who have season tickets lets me know.”
Rather a curious coincidence that the two pro-
fessional beauties, Mrs. Brown Potter and Mrs.
Langtry, have done the drop-scene act fairly
simultaneously. Both are faded beauties, and
both are going in for vaudeville, and each is
bankrupt of her ranches, her steam yachts, horses,
and villas, but, worst of all-in such cases—her
youth. Yet if Mrs. Langtry’s reminiscences were
published—and if she told the princely truth in
them—they ought to buy her much Kidderminster
carpet to keep her off the cold, cold world.
M. Eugene Lucciardi, the Vice-Consul for France
(he succeeded the Narcissus Count, who now makes
Society arabesques and cuts fleur-de-lis capers
among the ’Friscan elite), gave a beautifully-
chiselled lecture on the “L’Aiglon” of Rostand
before the Alliance Francaise some nights ago.
With brows bent and firm intent to know at
least what nouns he used, divers Australians
assembled and riveted their gaze on the gentle
Eugene’s ferocious moustache. Besides the lec-
ture he recited a poem, and Mrs. McMillan
took the place in recitation of some-
one who was ill. Mrs. McMillan is one
of the best speakers in French that Sydney
possesses. Among others who say “ oui ” with
great success are Mrs. Arthur Lassetter, the W. P.
Manning girls, and Mrs. Harry Levy. When
foreign warships are in port, and the brazen
cannon form only part of a scheme of decoration
for At Homes on board, those who can speak
idiomatic French are sure of a large and admiring
following of officer and middy. The majority of
the foreigners, Japs included, find their way
easily about the Froggy language.
Latest announced naval engagement is that of
a Bathurst belle to a man on the Challenger.
Lady was staying with Mrs. Lassetter, of “ Red-
leaf,” when hand-holding started.
A Sydney artist in hair says that things are
looking up financially in this State. Orders for
artificial hair “and packets of ladies’own comb-
ings to be made up” are rushing in from the
country. During the lean years of drought time,
hairworkers had a thin, grey time. A transfor-
mation and a tail of hair that will hang up behind
the door are not so necessary as a piece of corned
beef.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
