MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 2, 1905.
Mr Dear Yarrie,—
Miss Talbot—niece of Victorian Gov. Reggie-
shipped for Europe last Tuesday as quietly as she
came. She made no pals in Toorak, but clung
close to her cold lengthy relatives throughout the
visit.
Prince Ferdinand Umberto, expected here
as a visitor shortly, is a cousin of the Due
of the Abruzzi, and comes of the most com-
plicated and inter-marrying stock ever raised by
royal Italy. If his tastes run on Abruzzi lines,
there’s a good time ahead for any notably beau-
tiful barmaid who happens to encounter him.
Dame Forrest comes back to Melbourne in the
train of the Gov.-Gen., and Bigjohn himself goes
to the Sainted City to meet her. All the Federal
dames snort at “the Westralian busybody’s for-
ward tactics.” But the Forrests do not care a jot
what is said. They just hustle steadily ahead.
The new Commonwealth G.-G. caddy joins his
chief at Fremantle. He comes with a hyphen and
a brave bachelor record. By the time all the aides
gather round the little G.-G., not a vestige of the
mighty atom will be seen. There are some advan-
tages about that; his Ex. may be reported as being
present at a show when the public have really only
viewed his bodyguard, who are really not sur-
rounding him at all but surrounding nothing,
while he is having a quiet snooze at home. That
would be a step towards the ultimate development
of the G.-G. as a purely mythical functionary.
The mayoral pasteboards for the dance of the
10th have as a tag the impressive intimation—“ to
meet their Excellencies the Governor-General and
Lady Northcote.”
The L. C. Mackinnons changed their plans
about returning early from the Cold Land, and
now loiter (bai Jove !) on Lord Clinton’s moors for
the season when the fowl finds himself suddenly
dead. Meanwhile the junior Lachlan Mackin-
nons spread the Scotch damask and the family
silver m their new Toorak residence for many
small and particular sprees. The Scotch bride
turns out to be a cheery hostess.
A bevy of the Tooraketty neighborhood gam-
bled a vay a recent afternoon, with Miss Dolly
Nicholson as ringleader. Bridge was the play, and
after a close run Miss Fairbairn and Miss Lang-
tree claimed the prizes.
A frivolous but gimlet-eyed dame is showing
a fine neck-pendant as the result of her bridge-
winnings for this year. All the cats of her
acquaintance are saying unkind things of her
“marvellous run of luck.” “I win now and
again,” says one fair dame, “but it all goes in
buying fancy socks or hair restorer for Fred, to
propitiate the brute. It’s the only way to meet
nis objections.”
Bridge may have its evils, but a gay he-butter-
fly has unearthed one of its virtues. The bridge
maniacs have no time for wicked philanderings,
and the men are out of it. It is the one sport at
which the trailing sex is sufficient unto itself.
A well-known bachelor here has been given
occasion to remember the fable of the Arab, his
Camel, and the Cold Night. He “flats” in
Collins-street, and from his sitting-room a dear
little balcony branches out. Fellow flat-dwellers
in the same building dropped into the habit of
begging leave to take the air on this convenient
spot. Some she-flatters heard of the bachelor’s
amiability, and drifted out by his leave on the
same healthful mission. Soon they took complete
possession, and the lawful tenant was too shy to
intrude. One day, coming home early from toil,
he hoped that none of the aggressive creatures
would be holding his balcony against him, peeped,
and was horrified to see—not any lounging females,
but a high line of long lace stockings flapping in
the breeze before the upward gazers of Collins-
street. Then he gave notice to leave the building.
Inquiries were made by the caretaker, and subse-
quently the bachelor was prevailed upon to with-
draw his notice to quit on the understanding that
the she-flatters withdrew their stockings and the
clothes-line and themselves from the balcony.
Mrs. Everard Browne keeps on killing the fatted
calf for her spouse. He has been back from his
globe-trot quite a month or two, but the delusion
of a fresh arrival is maintained, and the slaughter
of the calf continues. A feeling begins to prevail
that if the calf is killed much oftener it will soon
be dead.
Miss M’Carron, a daughter of the late printer,
revolves in a minuetting train at the Princess just
now. She experienced the break-down of a pro-
posed matrimonial alliance, and since then has
been sampling the joys and troubles of stage life
with the Knight-Jeffries Co. She’s of the cameo
type, pretty as a living picture.
The local detachment of the Australian Field
Artillery has scattered pasteboards for a final
military dance. All the Brass-and-Braid hops of
the season went off gleefully, and with consider-
able dash, because all the prettiest polka-trippers
hereabouts rolled up in gay competitive chiffons.
The Talbottles turned up to the Stirling matinee
with friendly intentions to admire the returned
warbler at all vocal costs. After the final song
they made a detour to the wings and purred over
the singer. After that the straight pair, backed
by an uncomplaining caddie, stalked off to talk
Epilepsy Colony funds to a set of Toorak Jellybys.
The untiring Austral Salon once again put
forth its gentle paw in greeting to a returned
singer last week. This time the paw was extended
to Miss Maggie Stirling. The Salon must be very
tired by now. It has sent away hundreds' with
trophies and good wishes, and it has cooed with ever-
ready gladness over the return of quite ninety-nine
singers who need not have journeyed so far as
Europe to train the top C that wasn’t worth
while. Perhaps the worst shock the good-natured
Salon ever received was the speech delivered by
one vinegary little penwoman two or three years
ago. The snuffy one expressed her joy at leaving
Melbourne, and very defiantly stated that she left
without a shadow of regret, and hoped never to
come back.
Musicians regard with increasing alarm and
disgust the many affluent girls who are training
their vocal notes in the way melodious. The
fashionable tootler was an asset in the eyes of the
professionals a while back—when the profession
was teaching her to toot; but the result of the
top 0 cult was not anticipated. Now it is mani-
fest. The Toorak warblers croon lullabies and
plantation ditties for their friends at entertain-
ments, and the hired song is dispensed with. Any
high-toned hostess can rally up her girl chums to
supply melody for her At Home, and the pro-
fessional of the exacting appetite and the fee
ceases to be a necessary part of the scheme of
things.
The cat hobby must have reached the stage of
lunacy in the Cold Country. In a recent Field,
I came across an answer to a catty correspondent
advising her not to use her own brush on pussy’s
diseased coat, as that might cause the parasite
trouble that affected the cat to spread to the
owner.
Prefer Bert turned out to the Findon Harriers’
meeting, the oilier Saturday, in the State motor.
He wore his shiny top-piece and Sunday flogger,
and the dust was bad ; so he landed at the
Moonee Valley course looking like a comic baker
who had been rolling in the flour-bin.
Fancy hops for kiddies have had a disastrous
effect. Youngsters of studious habits have caught
the polka craze and throw syntax to the winds
when there’s a chance of a festive fling in fancy
rig. Several school mistresses are protesting
against such ball-room dissipation, and in one or
two instances a personal appeal has been made to
intending hostesses without success. So a consulta-
tion of pedagogues is mooted to wrestle with the
evil of the hop.
The T. H. Paynes have a new motor at which
all the small brown dogs in the Toorak neighbor-
hood yelp viciously. The car is a gorgeous one
with many silver gleamings about the body, and
looks like Aladdin’s lamp in a hurry. It is guaranteed
to preserve its terrible pace in the teeth of a gale
and to be a calm composed vehicle no matter how
serious the emergency.
An unusual incident marred some rather fine
bridal proceedings a few weeks ago. The ’groom
drank his own health so often, and responded to
so many flattering toasts, that he became totally
unconscious of the blushing bride by his side and
his festive friends in general. He refused to
budge from the table of rejoicing, and could not
be induced to change his wedding garment for his
travelling kit. The bride wept, and the honey-
moon trip had to be postponed.
It was worth Mrs. J. Fisken’s while to go down
before an attack of bronchitis for a few weeks to
enjoy evidences of the widespread concern as to
her bill of health. ’Phone messages arrived by
the hundred, and floral attentions were served up
with the pasteboard of inquiry day after day.
Complete recovery was hailed with applause.
Mrs. Fisken has been far more popular since her
marriage than when, as the wealthy widow
M'Caughey, she carried things off with a high
hand, and made many of the frilly ones snort. A
bland, cheerful hubby is the reason of such popu-
larity.
Suburban Mayors and Mayoresses are doing
well out of this year’s revelry. After one return
hop a sufficient sum remained to purchase a grand
diamond jewel for the Worshipess, and a collar
stud or something for himself. Only in two
municipalities did the Mayors refuse the return
hop and gewgaw.
Some scrupulous dames in a big hostelry ob-
jected to certain busty portraits of a theatrical star
being displayed all over the place. The manager
refused to cast them aside, and the dames pro-
bably are now wearing winkers to shut out sight
of the obnoxious articles.
Mrs. Watson, one of the frilliest of frockers,
tried matrimony a second time last Wednesday,
and had a limited but choice spree at the Grand
after the marriage. Her groom bears the Greek
name of Lazarus, and has large interests in the
big up-country town of quartz and voices. The
little dame’s bridal turn-out was a dream of shell-
like chiffon with Irish crochet laid on plain over
the most attractive contours. A lovely toque of
small dimensions went with the toilette. Nothing
neater has been seen in this State for a long time.
I hear of a champion mean man. The girl of
his heart is blessed with a heavy purse. When
the ring of approval came to be discussed, he
begged her, as a great, dazzling, blinding compli-
ment, to accept his late mammy’s dress ring for
the pledge of their betrothal. Hather dubious of
the intended flattery, she accepted. Then play-
fully he started to borrow her tram-tickets. In
the fullness of her newly-engaged heart, she
regarded this as one of the intimate blandishments
of love. Later, he hinted for a gift of the new
chiffon ties. Still dazzled, she spent her sus-
pender-money pn the gift. But when accompany-
ing her to kirk, one Sabbath, he tenderly asked
her if she had any small change to loan out for the
soup-plate that receives the widow’s mite and the
bachelor’s button, she waxed indignant. Now the
engagement is cried off. _ “To support a man
after marriage is not a circumstance,” says this
heiress, “to paying for a lover’s halo on the
strength of his departed mother’s dress ring.”
The theatrical engagement that leads on to
wedlock is not common, but that of Claude
Bantoclc is one to date. Early last week the
criers were out proclaiming the fact to the
town and the newspapers. Claude, it seems, is
the youngest hope of Dr. Granville Bantock, of
Upper Hamilton Terrace, London, and the
charmer of his choice, Miss Ada May Cale, a
Ballarat native.
The conveniences of life for a flutterer are on
the increase. Boudoirs are being opened up
where one can be thoroughly and freshly crimped
and powder-dusted for a trifle. One goes into a
renovating parlor looking greasy, dusty, limp,
One reappears the very glass of fashion.
The thatch has been brilliantined and trimmed,
the complexion renewed, the hat adjusted, the
veil hoisted to the stylish height—in short, the
whole feminine gear set trim and taut. The she
club isn’t in it with a boudoir that effects all this
m ten minutes.
Mrs. Norman Menzies is said to be hankering
after the footlights again. They all do. On a
Saturday night she hangs over them from her box
like a guileless, sublimely unconscious school-girl,
getting almost within range of the lime. Her idea
is to skip to the front in “ Yeronique,” a musical
farce that she has been studying for more than
drawing-room purposes. The Menzies are not
objecting. Their Dolly, they think, can do
no wrong. So well is she beloved.
The Ladies’ Work Association had all Toorak
stalling 1 it at the Saturday Bazaar, ready, as your
Dan O’Connor would say, for their work of “ non-
felonious robbery under the most lovely of
ar-r-rums.” They were in white muslin rigs and
mostly paper hats. Miss Cumming wore a hat
the size of a duck-egg above her pert face. Miss
Atherton was too severely laundered to look as
huggable as usual. Miss Fairbairn and Miss
Dimple Anderson shared the cake for the most
spotless and summery turn-out. Some of the white-
decked took to furs and cloaks, for the, breezes
round the hall were eager and nipping. Miss
Lesly Madden, as the cashier, must have had her
fingers pinked and polished at frequent intervals,
for at the very end of the show they were the
admiration of all who dealt with her in small
change. The Chomleys, the Brodribbs, and the
Armytages gurgled and fussed round their particu-
lar domains with any amount of starchy frill,
while the onlookers gaped on their antics.
The commotion at the coming of the Stonington
Dame to that Needlework Bazaar was wild. At
the very moment she was climbing the Town Hall
steps with a pink Aide in tow, the presentation
orchids couldn’t be found. Everybody tore round
for the missing blooms. At the 59th second of
the last minute Mrs. Harper clutched the
bouquet from its hiding-place and bounced
to the door brandishing the vegetables in
her excited paw. They were thrust on the
cafcn Ex. before she could protest any.
Then, in the stress of kow-tow, someone caught
the Talbottle dame by her haughty shoulders and
swung her round —to receive the gushful smile of
an over looked matron. And for the rest of the
afternoon her Ex. wore a chilled look of amaze-
ment.
A swagger widower, whose crepe-band has not
braved the breeze for any length of time, is look-
ing round again. Up to the present, most of his
attentions have been served up to a dame whose
own weeds are a shade worn by time. The widow
in question has much wool-wealth and a figure
more youthful than her top-tier.
Big breezy Miss Clara Clifton is resting during
“The Cingalee” season by taking any amount of
pedestrian and equestrian exercise. She has a
big chestnut, and rides into the regions of gums
and wattles whenever the weather is smiling.
Miss Muriel Cumming sees the race carnival
out, then goes to her gorgeous sister in Fog City.
The Rupertswood dame has always been a trump
to her people and a right bower to the Cumming
girls.
Talma, the lady who is a valuable distraction
and aid to the magicians at Rickards’, is 'the most
vital spark that ever adorned an illusion. She
flashes on and off the stage during the whole of
the amazing turn, talking, laughing, cake-walking,
in glistening serpentine raiment and contagious
good-humor. Her own turn of coin spinning and
minting while you wait is dexterously executed.
Further incitement is given the A.N.A. song
and recitation trophy-struggler by having his or
her photograph published and scattered broad-
cast. But the youth who elocuted “Casabianca”
in his salad days may live to regret it, and will
then be haunted by camera-proof of his early
foolishness. It will be almost as embarrassing as
having preserved against you a biscuit-picture
showing your nude babyhood set out on a shell,
like an extra large joint on a very small dish.
The wealthy widow of Councillor Tom Fogarty
embarked by the Himalaya for London last week.
One of the objects of the large dame’s visit is to
collect cats and dogs of high degree from ducal
and other noble fanciers. Some Pomeranians are
to be selected from an aristocratic doggery, and
Lady Marcus Beresford’s corner in cats is to be
encroached upon for the pussy fancier of the
Fogarty family. The late Councillor Fogarty
made his wealth in the wholesale grocery line and
hoarded it carefully, which fact enables his widow
to have a pretty taste in cats.
The leading epicure of this State pronounced,
on returning from a 27th jaunt over the world,
that it becomes a dear joy of the palate to taste
meat, fruit, and vegetables as raised in Australia
after the kind of stuff served up in the crack cafes
of the earth. Geelong asparagus in the opinion
of this man is the finest raised in the world.
The latest poodles to parade the block are to be
seen in green art covers—like the chintz coming
into vogue for morning-rooms. A St. Kilda-road
Miss of uncertain age takes a pair of the chintz-
covered poodles for a promenade on fine mornings.
Another who drives a Victoria takes a linen-
covered cushion for herself, and leaves a maize
satin one, with wisteria delicately trailed thereon
in the finest silk thread, to the thing of bark and
bite. And the man in the street who hasn’t a
whole coat, or a cushion, or a pair of sound boots
to call his own, looks on in jaundiced disgust.
Foremost to the cry of “ Tally-ho ” dash Miss
Alison Fairbairn and Miss Dolly Nicholson. The
pair have the showiest mounts of the season, the
trimmest figures, the sportiest boxers, and most
slap-dash coats. The best she-sport going
puts her equipment first among the joys of the
chase. Only when certain of looking trim and
taut and right in the picture does she give her
thoughts to the hounds and the excitement of a
gallop through bracken and gorse.
The loss of the Loch Vennachar brings back
memories of two other lost Loch liners—the Loch
Sloy, wrecked near Cape Borda, in April, ’99, and
the Loch Ard, whose tragedy happened over a score
of years ago. The sole survivors of the latter were
apprentice Tom Pearce and Miss Carmichael,
whom Pearce saved at great personal risk. Now
Pearce’s son, an apprentice, has gone down in the
Vennachar, almost within cry of his brother
Robert, an apprentice on the Loch Etive, now
lying at Williamstown (Vic.). From the Loch
Sloy three sailors and a passenger scrambled on to
the iron-bound coast, and subsisted anyhow till,
a fortnight later, one of them staggered into the
house of a bush settler. The settler hastily orga-
nised a search party, and succeeded in saving the
other two sailors, though the passenger was dead
when found.
There is a good deal of wrangling in reference
to the Government House garden fete at Adelaide
—at least so a correspondent informs me. The
British Nurses’ Association, one of the institu-
tions that is to benefit by the fete, desired to draw
out of it, but Lady Le Hunte requested that it
should remain in, adding “ When a request comes
from Government House, it is really a command.”
The latest arrangement is that all articles not sold
at the fete shall be distributed to charities as Lady
Le Hunte sees fit, and shall be distributed as
“gifts from Government House ” ! Stall-holders
say that, under these circumstances, there will be
very few unsold articles.
Miss Florence Hamer—the footlighter of the
eyes—has a liking for the Inky Way. All day
long she plies a grey goose-quill with great
industry. Members of the co. are puzzled as to the
subject. Some say she is preparing a seven-act
domestic farce, and others that she is in the throes
of a long jeremaid on the drama as it is played bv
the misleading people of the day.
Yours, TRYPHRNA.
A cheeky thing is the North Pole Road at
Keilor (Victoria).
Mr. Byron Brown has been giving the
Maoriland public some information as to how
Freetrade in kerosene raised the price to the
consumer:—
When the duty of 6d. per gallon was taken off kerosene
it was reckoned that the price of 9Jd. per gallon would
lu » ”*?• That was the case for about a month, but
then Mr. J. D. Rockefeller got wind of the alteration and
took steps to reap the benefit. Soon the price went up
and up, until now the quotation is 10fd. per gallon.
Last year M.L. imported 4,420,000 gallons of kerosene,
and Rockefeller is making out of the people £110,512 by
comparison with the returns when the duty was in force.
A few days ago an Adelaide horse-tram
ran over and killed an old man. Ifc was stated by
the driver at the inquest that the vehicle was
travelling at about three miles an hour when the
aged me got in the road. And yet the driver was
not cautioned for furious driving, like Messala in
the chariot race, or Jehu, the son of Nimshi, who
arrived at Jezreel with his back hair streaming in
the wind.
Melbourne Age remarks that the deter*
mination of his parents to remove from the Loch
Vennachar, recently lost off Kangaroo Island
(S.A.), a lad who recently completed his appren-
ticeship “ would appear to have been arrived at
by Providential intervention.” Is one to under*
stand that the Age’s Providence also intervened
to cause the men who remained on the vessel to be
drowned ?

Combining business with pleasure, Vic.
Premier Bent opened theßallaratSouth-street com-
petitions, got a verse of alleged comic song off his
chest, and then strolled round and bought two
million bricks for the State at 295. a thousand.
His just-signed agreement with the Brick Com-
bine allows him to buy anywhere over 30 miles
from Melbourne, but as the aforesaid combine has
the sole right to supply the 4,000,000 bricks
required for the new station, everybody is won-
dering what Thomas is going to do with this
Ballarat purchase. It is but a week since Thomas
and Tory McKetchup were patting each other on
the back for having got the Combine’s bricks at
355. Now it appears they could have got them at
295. all the time.
An inventive genius in Melbourne is
a PPlymg for a patent for a contrivance whereby
wheels are fitted to the human form in a manner
that will wipe out the bicycle in all its shapes as a
means of conveying mankind up and down the
face of the earth. The method of connecting the
man and the wheels is said to be quite simple, and
the man’s weight is to do the rest; therefore the
heavier the man the greater is to be the velocity,
and when Bent starts nothing in all Australia
will be able to overtake him, except George Reid
and Arthur Morgan. Thus does the perpetual
motion craze rise from its grave for the
1,197,824,813,946,218 th time.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Australian postal revenue would look up
if a more effective check were kept on the regis-
tration of newspapers. Adelaide Stock and
Station Journal, for example, is purely a circu-
lar of stock agents’ weekly sales with no right to
newspaper privileges; and there are many such
publications in the Commonwealth.
If N.S.W. Parliament retains in the
Liquor Bill the clause which provides for fining
a publican who serves a “prohibited” person, it
should, in fair play, provide that the prohibited
person shall wear a badge. The mere record of
a name (often Smith) on a list is not enough ; the
prohibited person will use other names, and go to
publicans who don’t know him.
Rep. Robinson wishes the Australian
Government to issue as a Parliamentary paper a
digest of the Canadian laws relating to immigra-
tion which, he contends, are more liberal than
those of Australia. If Robinson really wants the
public to be enlightened as to why Canada is pre-
ferred to Australia by the emigrant, let him call
for a digest of the Canadian tariff. He will find
the whole story there,
South African war medals were inquired
after in the House of Reps., the other night, by
Paddy Glynn, the S. Australian Demosthenes.
It appears that they are not in great demand.
In fact the honor is so little appreciated by those
to whom it is due, that the authorities have still
about a peck of the base metal gauds on hand and
unclaimed, and it is thinking of charging storage
on them.
Australia is now entertaining as a visitor
Dr. F. Otake, director of a Jap. woollen mill.
He cames to Australia to see about buying wool
which will be made up in Jap. mills by workers
paid from 6d. to 9d. per day, and then, in part, be
re exported to Australia —where it should have
been made up in the first instance by workers
earning from 6s. to 9s. a day ; and would be, if
Australia had a sane fiscal system.
In many centres the post-office is not run
by the Government directly, but is sub-let to a
storekeeper, and there have been several instances
cited of sweating under this system. It is the
duty of the Australian Government to see that
every postal employee is honestly and decently
paid, and it cannot “ contract out ” of this respon-
sibility. Close supervision should be exercised
over the working of contract post-offices.
The usual crop of suggestions had sprung
up around a clause of the Shearers’ Accommoda-
tion Bill, in the S. Aus. Assembly. “ I wish you
would let this clause through just as it is—Mr.
Kingston drafted it for me,” said Premier Price.
And promptly every suggested amendment was
withdrawn, and the clause was adopted exactly as
it stood. Then the House cheered.. A small
thing, but a unique tribute of trust and affection.
From “Gr. Ell”:
Our variegated brother in the Holy City threatens to
emulate the worm that is walked upon. A choice collec-
tion of turbans and pigtails has held a meeting in the
vicinity of Light-square and formed an ■“ Asiatic League.”
The precise objects of the association weie not stated, but
Mr. Chumbuddy Earn said that they had submitted
quietly to repressive legislation long enough and “ the
time had come.” Messrs. Curree Singh, Bad Ehum, One
Sing and other conservative gentlemen made various
sounds in support, and the meeting closed amidst much
enthusiasm and smell. It is believed that the new society
will be affiliated with the National Ass and Parasitic
Producers’ Union, and will vigorously co-operate with
these organisations in their efforts.
A recent Sydney 'Telegraph article,
written with tears in the inkpot, asserts that, if
Australia were not so hostile to colored immi-
grants, quite a lot of Chinese students might come
here, and spend £1 a week -each in the country.
This loss is terrible. If we opened our ports, and
let in 1000 Chinese students, and if they spent £1
a week here, that would be £52,000 a -year ex-
pended in the country, equal to 3d. per inhabitant
per annum. N.S.Wi alone loses more than twice
that amount by importing the one small item of
bags and sacks instead of making them locally, as
a sane and energetic community should do.
It says a lot for the mess that ex-Premier
Daglish made of things during the brief period
that Labor was in office in Westralia, that when
Premier Bason and his new Tory colleagues went
up for re-election each one of them was returned
by a larger majority over his Labor opponent
than he achieved at last general election. If
Daglish had been a Kidston or a Price he would
have captured Rason’s seat instead of losing his
own portfolio.
Rason, the new Premier and Treasurer of
Westralia, after announcing in a brisk, cheerful
way that the estimates showed a deficit of £323,000,
said that his predecessors had proposed taxation
to reduce it. But as the proposed tax would only
bring in £31,000 during this year, he wouldn’t
have it—he would rather have nothing than
£31,000. Which also apparently means that
Rason would rather have a deficit of £323,000
than one of £292,000 any day.
Proposed now that the N.S.W. Govern-
ment should spend half a million in rescuing from
the clutch of land monopoly, the Peel River
estate. But why not make half a million by
doing so? A stiff progressive land-tax, with a
50 per cent, surtax on absentee-owned holdings,
would force the Peel River and other such estates
into use, and at the same time bring in revenue.
Besides their economic dishonesty and extrava-
fance, partial resumptions with cash will always
e open to suspicion of corruption and land
agency.
As a reason why Westralia should live on
loans instead of revenue, Rason, its new Premier,
declares proudly that it is the richest and best
State of the Commonwealth. This is good
aboriginal logic. The bigger and stronger the
nigger, the less he carries himself, and the more
he makes his gin carry. And Rason proposes to
run the country on the lines of our despised and
shiftless black brother Bill. The only difference
between black brother Bill and Rason is that Bill
throws his load to the lubra ; Rason throws his to
the piccaninny.
A WILD GOOSE CHASE.
Bichard Edwards., member for Oxley (Q.),
should., on his performances, be elected leader of
(the Stinking Fish party. He was in Japan a few
months ago, and while there a perky Jap, who
evidently subscribes to the Argus, asked him,
“What about the six hatters?” This sank deep
into the soul of Edwards, and when he spoke in
the Australian Parliament, the other evening, he
attacked the Immigration Restriction Act with
surprising fury. His remarks occupy 35 columns
of Hansard, and they are thickly sprinkled with
references to six hatters, six potters, Mrs. Tozer s
maid, and somebody else’s groom. And Edwards
actually voted himself for the very clause —the
contract clause—which he now curses.
No political party calling itself “ national ”
ever occupied a much meaner position than the
Foreign-trade party in Federal politics. Its head
and front, its elected leader, the representative of
its political existence, declines to represent it in
Parliament except at times when his private
business permits. Fora Prime Minister s salary
he will represent it; he has done so ; but he will
not lead his party as an Opposition on a mem-
ber’s allowance of £4OO, and his party is content
to do with odds and ends of his spare time rather
than subscribe as much as will satisfy his
demands. And while things are thus, the so-
called leader and his party pose _ as the only
patriotic and self-sacrificing politicians in the
Commonwealth.
In N.S.W. the country person appears to
have a quite incurable idea that he shouldn’t pay
for his own roads like the city person, but should
loaf and cadge for them on the loan funds or on
the general community. Therefore the Local
Goverment Bill is met at every stage by the cry
of Twadded, M.L.A., that the country districts
want to be quite sure —dead sure —that it wi l
mean no t ixation, and by the yelp of Nielsen or
Griffith who want to know “why people should
have Local Government shoved down their
throats."’ It is questionable if two candidates
h ive been returned to N.S.W. Assembly in the
la t 40 years who weren’t elected on the under-
sti iding that they were just perishing for Local
' Government,-but apparently most of them didn’t
mean it.
Mr. John Meagher, the medieval, op-
posing the Smoke Nuisances Bill in N.S.VV.
Legislative Council: —
It was Scripturally announced that where TherewM
fire there must be smoke ; therefore, the present would
have been an absurd Bill to have passed.
It would be interesting to know where Meagher
gets his Scripture from. This looks like public-
house Scripture—not the Bible variety.
The International Harvester Trust having
attempted to extend its operations to Maoriland,
to the prejudice of local manufacturers of agricul-
tural machinery, Seddon promptly promised .Par-
liamentary intervention to “prevent the trust
injuring a promising industry in the State and
throwing out of employment a large body ot
skilled workmen.” There is no concession to the
Foreign-trade fad about Seddon. He votes for
Maoriland industry every time.
Half the time of the average commercial
man seems to be spent in faking some cheap or
fraudulent substitute for a genuine article of food.
The other day Customs Minister Lyne ukased
that henceforth “koffee” should pay the same
duty as coffee. This vile substitute for an honest
beverage is prepared from wheat, barley, and rye
—burnt grain, in fact. When Australia really
decides to jump on commercial immorality it will
levy a treble duty on such messes.
One Sydney morning paper calculates that
the N.S.W. Government, on the terms of its con-
tract with the Lithgow ironworks, will pay away
£26,661 to establish a new iron, industry ; the other
estimates the payment at nearly £46,000. The
amount, whatever it is* will be spread over a num-
ber of years, so it doesn’t come to very much per
annum ; still the Freetrade conscience is a good
deal broken up over it. N.S.W. pays over
£1,000,000 a year in reduced railway rates to keep
the “great primary industry” of agriculture afloat,
but that is a different thing. Agriculture is the
blessed old “backbone of the country’’—though,
on the figures, it looks more like a parasite than a
bone.
Premier Price promises, if he retains office
for a year, that Adelaide shall have electric trams.
Presumably he means to alter the present ridicu-
lous Act, which prohibits the Government from
carrying out the work of electrification, and only
authorises it to buy the trams from the present
syndicate and then lease them to a new syndicate.
The alleged supporters of Bent in the Yic.
Assembly are just about as weariful and
obstreperous as the Federal Opposition. The
passage of the Milk Supervision Bill has become
so; gradual that it now averages about one and a
half clauses a day ; in fact, without the help of
the Opposition Swinburne would not get his bill
through at all. The education of the farmer and
the improvement of his herds and dairies will not
only advantage the community generally but will
also yield a huge profit to the farmer himself ; but
the farmer steadfastly refuses to be washed—at
least, his special representatives say he does.
The rejection by iST.S. W. Legislative
Council of the Bill which was to re-christen the
suburb of St. Peters as “ Brampton 1 ’ has done
more to convince the people of that brickmaking
locality that one House is quite enough in a State
Parliament than the Council’s half century of
opposition to democratic measures. St. Peters
Mayor Spackman thus rises to the situation and
rages at large :
If the Upper House, which in no way represented the
people, treated them thus ... it would be necessary to
takeaction. It would then be the duty of the people to rise
and demand that their wishes should not be thwarted by
an irresponsible', non-elective body.
The “struggling settler” in N.S.W., in
addition to all the aids and bonuses he now
receives, is to be further subsidised by being
supplied with rabbit-proof wire-netting on credit.
The best subsidy the struggling settler could
receive would be the establishment of a dozen
manufacturing towns in different parts of the
State, so that he might have a market for his pro-
duce close at hand, instead of having to send most
of it to Sydney or to England; and the best
remedy for the rabbit is population. But N.S.W.
votes against both these things, and prefers to buy
wire netting with borrowed money to fence in a
Freetrade wilderness.
The Opposition in the Australian Parlia-
ment has fought savagely against the provisions
of the Commerce Bill for securing the honest
branding of exports. They want freedom of ex-
port—for stinking fish yarns, stinking butter and
bad apples. And this in the interests of the pro-
ducer ! As a matter of fact, there is no measure
which would better help the honest producer than
an Act which subjected every item of export to
rigid supervision. That would, in a very short
time, make the word Australian mean “prime
quality ” in all the markets of the world.
“This is one of the most undesirable
immigrants you could possibly find, 1 ’ said Mel-
bourne magistrate Panton lately, referring to an
expressionless-looking German who had been sent
out to the care of the head of a big foreign
firm in Melbourne, and, getting a billet at £1 a
week, had straightway set out to rob his em-
ployers. Panton didn’t say anything about the
big man who got him the billet, or the other firm
which employed him at £1 a week; yet he was
probably quite right about the immigrant himself.
America would have promptly returned him
whence he came—as being a pauper and a person
with a criminal countenance —but what sort of a
noise would the Stinking Fish press have made
had the Australian Customs officers refused a
landing to this German?
That public misfortune, Tom Mann, is
again preaching his favorite remedy for lack of
employment—the cutting down of the working
day to six hours, so as to make the.little scrap of
employment go round, There is £30,000,000
worth of well-paid work per annum waiting to be
done even now in Australia if only this country
had the sense to shut down on needless iinpqrtg.
There is about £220,000,000 of debt to be paid
off. There are several dozen million acres of good
land to be put under cultivation as soon as the
land monopolist is got rid of. And Mann, the
undesirable immigrant and apostle of loafing, has
one remedy to propose for all evils—that Aus-
tralia should sleep, eat and declaim about its
“ chains” for 18hours a day six days in the week,
and all the 24 hours on the seventh day.
According to the Sydney daily papers of
Wednesday week, Mr. 0. Lane, the new Presi-
dent of the Baptist Union, said a lot of severe
things about the laws of the Commonwealth—this
among the rest: —
The legislation which committed to gaol the skilled
artisan who comes to our shores to teach us more intelli-
gent methods, lest he should have some share in our pro-
sperity. . .was evidence of self and self-interest which must
be conquered before we could enter upon the path of true
progress.
This paper would like to ask Mr. C. Lane if he
was correctly reported in the above statement,
and, if so, who was the skilled artisan who came
to our shores to teach us more intelligent
methods,” and who was committed to gaol ? For
if he did say what the Sydney Telegraph charges
him with, and unless he can point to some artisan
that this paper doesn’t remember, he is
an unpatriotic slanderer, and anything but an
ornament to the Baptist community.
One of the most humorous incidents —if it
hadn’t the possibilities of tragedy in it—in con-
nection with Booth’s proposed export of 5000
families, is the announcement that Westralia is
ready to receive its parcel now. Poor old Booth,
who evidently thinks all land is the same, told a
Daily Mail person that he had been offered
20,000 acres of “lovely land, in Westralia, at &.
an acre.’' “It has a river running through it,”
said Booth, joyfully, and he went on to picture
the delight of the new settlers when they peached
this garden of Eden. It is only a few month®
since several Victorian farmers, lured by the state-
ments of a paid agent from Westralia, went across
to take up land ; and, after an exhaustive search,
came back declaring that there were only
about 1000 acres of decent agricultural soil in
the whole State, and that John Forrest’s friends
at Bunbury had hold of that, and had held it for
50 years or so.
After an absence of nearly a month,
George Reid turned up again in the House of
Reprehensibles las,t week. When the round,
stout man with the .eyeglass returns to the House
after an interval there is always the same weary
old programme. The large individual stands at
the table, and in a loud, high-pitched voice makes
a general mention of everything that happened
while he was away, and runs querulously through
all the old myths and legends that have been
exploited by the Tory press for a year. The bony
spectre of Anti-Socialism, the draggled old
stuffed dummy of Constitutional Government, are
the leaders of a two-hour procession wherein the
Six Hatters grin, and the Six Potters gape, and,
the Ticket-of-leave-man rears his dusty head, ror
two hours he shrieks and perspires, while Johnson
and Williemotor Kelly cheer wildly. Then the
talky hero, damp and steaming, is led forth from
the chamber and given a large bottle of lemonade,
and he vanishes for the rest of the sitting.
Sydney papers and politicians had hardly
finished expressing their indignation at the state-
ment of Prime Minister Dealtin, that the bad
land laws of the States were one great obstacle to
immigration, when three things happened : (1) It
was announced in the S.M. Herald and lele-
graph that a number of farmers were leaving
northern N.S.W. for Chili because, though there
is still good State land in the northern district,
the lazy, wretched and incompetent Administra-
tion won’t make it available, nor will it force the
great private estates into the market; (2 ) five
farms—the largest only 394 acres—were thrown
open for selection at Dubbo, and there were 660
applications for them ; (3) Premier Carruthers
decided that the best he could do for the immi-
grants he is inviting from England is to send them
out to the Pilliga scrub, about 450 miles from
Sydney, far away from markets, away from rail-
ways, on the edge of the Great Drought, an,d
half-a-mile from perdition. This is mostly a bar-
ren region. It depends on the chance of artesian
water being found, and when the artesian water
is found it is often so loaded with mineral that it
kills all vegetation.
A SEOESSIONAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Speaking at Seymour, Mr. Graham, M.L.A., said he
was hoping that something would come along to make
the Federal Parliament mend its ways. It not, he felt
certain that something would arrive to dissolve the
union.” —Melbourne Age.
Thus an angry M.L. A. takes his unlamented way
To found a rival Commonwealth of one,
And by filling up each groove, for a time should make it
prove
The best-conducted country ’neath the sun ;
He’ll be Premier and porter, P.M., and letter-sorter,
And tax himself to pay himself the pelf
Allotted him by him ; but his quaint secession whim
Soon will make him try seceshing from himself!
Then as private M.L.A. he’ll complain, in just the way
That he is now complaining hereabouts,
That as Minister his daring drives him almost to despair-
ing,
And his recklessness doth fill himself with doubts ;
And he’ll rise in Opposition to condemn his own position,
And tell him he’ll secede and make him rue it,
And as Ministerialist he’ll reply, “ You won’t be missed !
Then he’ll try to leave himself. But can he do it ?
vie.

Recent Adelaide Medical Congress was
able to see at A. M. Bickford and Son’s establish-
ment proof that the Australian can manufacture,
if given a chance. That firm showed several
locally made surgical instruments including a
“ Glover’s crutch ” —a very intricate affair.
Lawyer Jellicoe, of Maoriland, has been
explaining to the English press that Australia is
losing population, and that “the only State in
Australia prospering is N.S.W., thanks to the
statesmanship of Mr. Reid.” During the Reid
period (1894-1899) N.S.W. sent away about 1900
more people than it received. Jellicoe says in a
lofty way that Agent-General Coghlan, who
objects to some of his statements, “should first
inspect the official records,” but apparently
Jellicoe himself is not on speaking terms with the
official records.
In the case Rae v. Rae, N.S.W. Divorce
Court went rather against the common-sense con-
clusion from the facts. The Rae marriage was an
utter failure. The husband sought relief on the
ground of desertion, and seemed to have estab-
lished his case, but the Judge held that the wife’s
mental condition barred him from a remedy.
“ There was ample proof of mental derangement,
and of actions making it clear the unfortunate
woman was not always responsible for her
actions by reason of her mental condition.”
The theory of the divorce law is, or should be,
that when connubial relations are no longer possi-
ble, the marriage contract should be voidable on
the application of either party. At present insanity
is not a cause of divorce, for the reason that there
is a lingering trace of the old ecclesiastical law in the
idea that divorce is a “punishment” and it would
not be fair to “punish ” for insanity; but there is
no doubt that, with the growth of common-sense,
hopeless insanity will be recognised as as good a
reason for the dissolution of a marriage as the
death of one of the partners. It is death in a
sense-mental death. Meanwhile there is no
sense in stretching the law so as to make a
“mental derangement” have the same effect as
insanity. A very great number of women are
hysterical, and hysteria is a mental derangement
which it would be dangerous to recognise legally
as equivalent to insanity.
After all the bitter curses it has thrown at
the Labor party, S.M. Hekald, the other day,
printed this refreshing bit of truth from F. A. A.
Russell’s article, “ Australian Problems” :
It is difficult to see how any fair-minded man can with-
hold admiration for that great party. Anyone who
believes in that sound motto which, as Carlyle points out,
expresses the feeling in which all great revolutions have
had their national basis: “ The tools to the man who can
use them ” —anyone who believes in that motto must see
that it was inevitable and fitting that Labor should play
such a part in Australian legislation as it has done for the
last 10 years. The writer has no sympathy whatever for
the lay citizen who comfortably spends his evenings in an
armchair, behind a cigar and a long whisky and soda, and
curses the Labor party. Labor does not spend its even-
ings in inaction, but understands politics, organises, and
uses its vote. By virtue of energy and attention to prac-
tical affairs, the Labor party has enjoyed the confidence
of all its natural supporters ; and, having a large follow-
ing with clear and definite aims, well-disciplined, it has
been for some time past the most powerful and definite
factor in our politics. The leaders of the Party have
deserved well of their followers, nor ha.ve they deserved
ill of their country. No great financial scandals have
enmeshed the real leaders of Labor. Contrast the solidity
and perseverance and self-confidence of the Labor party
with the dissolution, apathy, and distrust prevalent in
other parties, and the spectacle is not pleasing. What
member of a Reform League in New South Wales puts
any trust in Mr. Carruthers ? Compared with Labor, all
other parties in Australia are sick, indefinite, and incom-
petent. j
“ D’Harum ” keeps an appointment:—
When in Sydney, a little .time back, I accidentally made
the acquaintance of a very charming girl, and arranged
to meet her, at the George-street corner of the G.P.0., at
8 o clock, one Thursday night. The Post-office, to me,
seemed to be the meeting-place of the majority of Sydney
couples. I stood on the steps and waited patiently for
my girl. Scores of fellows came along and were found by
scores of girls, but I waited on. I was new to Sydney,
friendliness of the ladies who passed quite
startled me. Though I had never been introduced to any
of .* he “) ( dor > fc remember seeing any of them before), they
said, Good night, dear,” stopped and talked to
me, and invited me to call. The idea of me going
to their homes without knowing their fathers or
mothers. I don’t know if there is anything taking
about me, but quite 30 said “Good night” tome. A
gul, with a feather in her hat as large as a good-sized
eagle, kept passing me there in the gloom, and gazing
into my face in a puzzled, inquiring sort of way. At
last she stopped and asked in a very bashful way, “ You
ain’t the bloke I ’ad t’ meet to-night, are yer?” I said I
thought not, whereat she observed she thought not too,
and confessed to me that she had mashed a bloke on a
Manly boat, and had arranged to meet him, but she had
forgotten his face because she had never got a good view
of it. I assured her that my open countenance was not
that of the bloke. During the next half-hour eight men
asked for matches, and 14 street arabs came begging
cigarette cards. I began to get angry at the girl not
turning up, and when a policeman who had passed and
repassed me almost 50 times, came by once more, winked
knowingly, and said, “ I don’t think she’ll come now, old
man,” I decided that Sydney girls are fickle, and that I
should henceforth shun them as I would one or two
serpents.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
BHYME OF A JERSEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A strip of tartan across the blue,
Those are the colors we used to wear.
I had not seen them since ’B2,
Till I found the jersey lying there.
Tattered and torn beyond repair.
Shrunk by the wet, and stained with clay—
Moth-eaten relic of days that were,
It has served its turn. Throw it away !
You see that patch of a darker hue ?
It marks the spot where a scar I bear,
The place where the bone was splintered through,
When somebody threw me fair and square.
But I “ passed ”in time—and who would care
For a rattling fall? We won that day
Finished the game with a goal to spare—
That was in ’BO. Throw it away !
Take it away 1 It has naught to do
With the dull to-day with its tret and tare.
Take it away, for it makes me blue,
To feel but fit for an easy chair.
Bellows to mend if I mount a stair •
Whist is the only game I play—
Shrunken of muscle, and gray of hair,
Why should I keep it ? Throw it away 1
Damozels, dainty and debonair,
Watching the game in your silk array,
It’s little you know and less you care
For us—poor players of yesterday.
m i Taiwa.
M.L. *
* * * •
Sydney’s last football flutter for the season was
the re-played finatfor the Gardiner Oup under Association
rules between the Balmain and Kozelle teams, which had
tied the previous Saturday in spite of 20 minutes’ extra
play. Both clubs put exactly the same men into the field
for the second tussle, and excitement ran high. Both
sides were nervous and over-anxious, still it was a great
game to watch. Rozelle attacked violently, the Balmain
goal-keeper fell in the act of saving, and Rozelle secured
the leather again and shot a goal. This was followed by
frenzied attacks by Balmain, but the defence was sound,
and the first half ended without the score being altered by
either side. The second half found Balmain attacking
more furiously than ever. Play raged all over the ground,
back and forwards, and then, out of a sudden mix-up,
Balmain equalised the score. Flushed with this success, it
attacked again from the kick-off. Rozelle, momentarily
dejected, did not rise to the occasion, and before it had
time to think, Balmain had shot another goal amidst tre-
mendous excitement. Then Rozelle pulled itself together
and played the game of its life ; but Balmain struggled as
though its immortal soul was in danger, or its rates were
likely to be raised if it lost, and held its lead to the end.
This is the fourth time Balmain has won the Gardiner
Goblet, the former occasions being in ’95, ’96, and ’97.

In spite of numerous free counter-attractions
28,057 persons (as against 32,668 last year) parted
£lO6l 17s. 6d. to see the Yic. Football League final on
Saturday. The Railways put on specials from Ballarat,
Bendigo and Geelong, and the country came to town to
see a great battle between Collingwood and Fitzroy. Up
to half-time they got their, money’s worth in a fair,
strenuous, open game. It was so hard-fought and so
close that at quarter-time ’Roy had notched but three
behinds to ’Wood’s one, while at half-time the scores were
level—l goal 3 behinds each. It was a pretty game to
watch, the bigger and heavier ’Woodsmen never able to
rush or overpower the lighter, but quicker, ’Roys. But
after the spell ’Wood couldn’t get its system going properly
—the chief exponent thereof, Condon, being useless with
a sprained ankle —while ’Roys put in several brilliant
exchanges that rattled their opponents and brought them
three more goals and three behimis, while ’Wood could only
tally one of each. (This latter was from a lovely low kick
by Peers, who, the night before, had driven 40 miles over
the western plains to catch the morning train from
Geelong to Melbourne.) The last quarter was mostly a
fierce struggle by ’Wood to score the needed 15 points.
The whistle, for breaches of the rules, was going continu-
ously ; and thrice hot-tempered players exchanged fistic
compliments, a handy constable having to threaten
summary arrest to stop one scuffle. Outside, barrackers
raved and bellowed, a few getting as far as the
black-eye stage. But all ’Wood’s endeavors gained
them only one poor behind ’Roy’s defence was
too solid, and when the bell rang it found latter pre-
miers by 4 goals 6 behinds to 2 goals 5 behinds. ’Roy
first flew the pennant in’ 95, and has been top-dog on four
occasions since. Undoubtedly, the best team won, and
the victory was due chiefly to concerted play.
* * * *
Across, the park, at East Melbourne, some
15,000 spectators mildly applauded the Association
leading clubs Richmond and North Melbourne-
fighting their “final,” but as both had the right
to challenge there was always a dim suspicion
that they were not straining themselves. On
the play it looked quite a straight game, the
chief trouble being that North seemed to have
a notion that a behind was as good as a goal,
while Richmond put a new man on the forward
line, and was mightily pleased when he kicked five goals
out of the 7 goals 6 behinds scored against North’s
3 goals 10 behinds. This habit of kicking the wrong side
of the post is fatal. As a result of the previous Satur-
day’s “ scraps ” the committee had wiped out one player
for the whole of next season and another for half, so
Saturday’s discussion was of a distinctly peaceful charac-
ter. Next Saturday North “challenges” Richmond for
the premiership—the last match of the season—and if the
rain holds off, and Bent quits bellowing, and Yes-No will
stop in Sydney, there should be a great uproar and a
large house.
* * * *
North Sydney, despite its wholesale dismember-
ment, beat Balmain at cricket, hands down. Its two sawn-
off limbs, Gordon and Middle Harbor, were soundly wal-
loped, but their downfall was a great moral victory'com-
pared to the awful crushing that Glebe, Burwood, and
Redfern gave Paddington, Sydney, and Central Cumber-
land respectively. Talk about Togo and the Baltic Fleet,
listen to the scores:—Glebe, 3 for 205 (innings closed),
against Paddington’s 76, and 9 for 25 ; Burwood, 3 for 227
(innings closed) against Sydney’s 83 and 94 ; Redfern, 366
against Central Cumberland’s 68, and 9 for 122. Nobody
reached a century, but there were some dandy bowling
figures. _

Pye, the Parramatta cricketer generally credited
■with being unjustly used by the great Noble during the
past two or three years, was also badly' treated by a recal-
citrant motor-car which jibbed or shied or something.
A broken wrist was among the injuries, and will affect
his cricket this season. Anyhow, Pye is seized with the
golf mania, and is more interested in “putting than
“cutting,” and “foursomes” have more charm for him
how than “fivers.” Appointed to a place on N.S.W.
second team selection committee, he promptly resigned
therefrom. ,
* * * *
Alick Bannerman, who received in 1878 the first
ball ever bowled to a white Australian in England, the
bowler being Alfred Shaw, was bowled by Bowden in
each innings this week for one and two. Does not the
veteran lag superfluous on the stage ?
“Slip” : Clever little stumper Carter, son of a
• Sydney suburban undertaker, started last Saturday with
a modest 25, marked by strokes to every point of the
compass. Without a peer in Australia for the last three
years in his own special line, his rejection for Kelly and
Newland was just about the most glaring of the crimes of
the last boodle-hunting syndicate’s engineers. Carter is
on the Blackham-Pilling plane, and this year should see
him behind the sticks for the No. 1 N.S.W. eleven,
permanently fixed there for some time to come.
A willow enthusiast writes: A brutal day
marked the opening ot N.S.W. cricket season last Satur-
day, but Labor Monday, when the games were resumed,
had its compensations. Pity the authorities don t start
at 11 a.m. on this holiday instead of 2.16 p.m. The oppor-
tunity for a full day’s play should be seized, seeing that
the majority are limited to 3J hours per week, on Satur-
day afternoons. The objection that an extension of time
for play in that particular round might unfairly influence
the destination of the champion flag should be over-
weighted by the extra benefit of one whole glorious
day’s cricket. And, after all, the play’s the thing—hang
theflagi # „
N.S.W. Cricket Association will continue to
try and instil vim into country cricket by bringing teams
to Sydney in November. Unless the country sloggers
show better form than they did last year the effort will
be a vain one. The trouble is, of course, that there is
such a swarm of good cricketers in Sydney waiting for a
show in representative cricket that country members get
no chance. As the Scripture saith, it is easier for an ele-
phant to passthrough the eye of a needle than to push
an old player out of the N.S.W. team, and while that is
so Country Week will continue to be a wearisome failure.

N.S.W. Cricket Association and Sydney Cricket
Ground Trustees are squabbling over the appointment of
a trustee. The Cricket Association has invariably ap-
pointed its president a trustee, and when Eeid (he of the
eye-glass and dog) was president of the Association, a
couple of years ago, he was placed on the trustee-
list. Now there is a new president, W. J. Trickett.
and Reid has been deposed from the body of trustees,
upon which the Association is trying to place its presi-
dent. The other S.O.G. trustees are wroth, and are
nominating Reid for the billet again ; hut the Association
is ignoring them altogether, and has appealed to Minister
for Lands Ashton, fiver since the Cricket Association
had to drag the S.C.G. trustees to law in order to teach
them their business the trustees have been unpleasant.
• » * *
It ia stated that the home-coming Australian
XI. hasn’t been a very happy family, and the fact
that the team is dragging itself in pieces towards
Australia gives color to the report. As to the financial
result of the tour, the amount per head to each cricketer
will easily top four figures—that is, including the Maori-
land part of the tour. As the Board of Control will take
over the management of all future teams, this bonanza
will probably be the last.
The idea of alloting three points for a win out-
right, and two for a first innings victory, is likely to
produce greater interest in Sydney first-grade cricket.
Hitherto, when a team was disposed of, the opposing side
waded in to flog the bowling unmercifully, and remain at
the wickets for the full time ; but the alteration of points
will cause the closure to be applied, and the bowlers given
a better chance. It is bowling talent that needs brushing
up in Australian cricket generally, and the Association is
on the right track to stimulate a branch of cricket which
has been considerably neglected in the efforts of players
to score centuries and gain the vanity of a headline in the
daily papers.
• • * *
Outside of the test matches the Australians
had very useful luck as a rule—winning the toss against
the strongest of the counties, and getting the best of the
wicket. What might have happened to them in two or three
instances nearly did happen at Bournemouth, where a
“ scratch” England XI. had brought the scores to a dead-
heat before the last Australian batsman (Bill Howell) went
in. With the wicket wearing badly the team that won
the toss was nearly strong enough to win the match, and
had it been Lancashire, Yorkshire, or even Surrey playing
under the same conditions the game would probably have
gone against the visitors. Considering how many tricks
are played by the weather in England, it is a rather lucky
Australian XI. that doesn’t get slipped-up by one or other
of the strongest bowling counties. A respectable score
on a fairly hard wicket, after which a heavy rainpour,
followed by bright sunshine —and the thing is done.
At the opening of a new turf wicket at Singleton,
the other day, the member for the district (Mr. James
Fallick) bowled three balls to the mayor (Mr. York), and
then he bowled three balls back. Didn’t transpire if the
wicket was constructed out of loan money. Probably it
was. _ .

Understood that should English captain Jackson
bring a team of cricketers to Australia the local Board of
Control will select a man from the two-up schools to spin
the coin with him.
* * * *
Christchurch and Wellington (Maoriland)
Cricket Associations propose to inaugurate electoral
cricket with residential qualifications. Under the present
system the crack clubs get more players than they want,
and the weaker ones cannot get enough to make a decent
showing. The electoral system introduces a slight element
of chance into the business—even a cricketer cannot
always choose the locality of his own residence.
* * * *
Sturt (S.A.) lacrosse club has finished up an
astonishing season, in which it has played 12 matches
with other Adelaide clubs, and won them all, beaten St.
Hilda (Vic.’s premier club), and also, single-handed, beaten
combined Westralia (in Adelaide) and combined Victoria
(in Melbourne). It is not likely that any club in Australia
ever before had the chance of taking on two whole States in
one season at any game whatever, let alone beating them.
The Sturt figures are nearly all records: 200 goals in the
season by one man, Humphris, and H by him in one
match. This last record was once equalled by Phil
Newland, who is a better lacrosse player than wicket-
keeper. # # . ,
Ancient oarsman Richard Green, who won the
Sydney National All-comers’ Outrigger race in 1867 and
’6B, announces that his son Frank is going to reach out
for the championship of the world. Francis certainly
looks, in his portraits, a hefty young man. The most
remarkable thing about him is that he will use a fixed
seat, both father and son holding the sliding apparatus in
hearty contempt. If Francis, on a fixed seat, can down
men like Stanbury and Towns, then he is the most remark-
able rower Australia has seen for many a long day. At
present he is located in Wellington, Maoriland, but will
shortly be in Sydney with a challenge to Stanbury to
come out where the ducks swim and show what he is
made of. _ _
* • * *
Stanbury hasn’t yet covered the deposit put up
by George Towns for a return match for the champion-
ship, but is' waiting a reply from Durnan, the Canadian.
Towns’challenge, before the previous match had grown
cold, has had the effect of keeping the champion in his
outrigger on the Parramatta River, as he desires to avoid
growing too bulky. Towns plugs away at boat-building.
A huge crowd of the sons of Erin gathered at
the Friendly Societies’ Ground, Melbourne, to watch the
N S.W.-Victoria inter-State hurling match. Hurling is
the Irish national game, and although this writer is a
Sassenach, he has no hesitation in declaring it a very
good game indeed —one of the best, in fact. N.S.W. was
very confident (the game is younger in Victoria than on
the Sydney side), but the Victorians were apt pupils, and
although less scientific at the start they had a lot more
energy than the visiting team. Moreover, the presence
of the Church, in the person of Father Rohan, was a
tower of strength to the local men. The N.S.W. men
caught beautifully (the game allows the players to catch
the bung with the left hand and slog it with the
hurley, which is carried in the right), and, moreover,
were able to do much more effective work than
the Victorians with double-handed strokes. But Father
Rohan and the Victorian base man, Bourke, played
sound defence. At the end of the second quarter the
Cornstalks were leading by 2 goals 3 behinds to 2 behinds,
and things looked blue for the men of Bent’s State. By
this time the Victorians had got accustomed to one
another, or got over their shyness, or something, and
played a much more systematic game. Some beautiful
passing rushes took place, and the N.S.W. fortunes began
to sink, and the end of the third quarter saw the scores
2 goals 3 behinds all—a most, remarkable thing. The last
quarter was desperate, but the Victorians, prevailed, and
the game ended in the defeat of N.S.W. by 4 goals
6 behinds to 3 goals 6 behmds. Just possible that another
match may be played next Saturday. Hurling, by the
way, is one of the most ancient field-games of the Celtic
rale, and has been played for nearly 1000 years under
practically the same rules as govern it to-day.
With a hope that the process will reveal the
best amateur athletes to represent N.S.W. in the cham.
pionships meeting in November, Sydney University will
battle against the combined clubs and public schools in
an all-day programme shortly. Considering that the
Uni. contains whales like Barker, Waddy, andTFutter, not
to mention other leviathans, the result of that days
tussle ought to make sole selector Dick Coombes task an
easy one. _ „
* * * *
The death of Charles Tudor Bird, a one-time
celebrated long-distance ped., who came out here with
Frank Hewitt, recalls to mind that though he was
credited with being able to run 11 miles within the hour,
he never succeeded in any of his attempts at the featin
Australia. The best he ever did was 11 miles in 63min.
from the 14-mile post on the main road, Tasmania, to
Cooley’s, at New Town. A reputed successful attempt m
Victoria was proved a fraud, three or four feet having
been cut out of the centre of the measuring tape. A
contemporary of Bird was another long-distance runner,
Mat Higgins, who could run 10 miles in 60 minutes.
Higgins has been for many years, a member of the
Tasmanian police force, and has risen to the rank of
sergeant.
# • • *
A guessing match at the recent Live Stock Show
in Adelaide showed results that were cut down very fine.
The victim bullock was duly slain, and weighed when
cold 115541 b. A leading squatter had guessed 115441 b.,
and two other shots (one by a lady) were within 241 b. of
the exact figure. The 350 shillings received went to the
Children’s Hospital, less £7 for prizes (nominally, but the
squatter returned the fiver he won). The £l7 which the
bullock sold for also went in the sacred interests of
charity, so the dead animal may reckon that he perished
in a good eause.

According to evidence before the Victorian
Police Commission, one city constable was transferred to
Dim Distance for “improper interference at a football
match.” It was deposed that this man found life so
unbearable after his rash act that he prayed to be sent
right out into the desert where he wasn’t known, ana
where he could start life afresh. It is.assumed that his
“ improper interference ” averted the dismemberment of
an umpire. # #
Nine out of 14 pigeon-slayers at Friday’s Gun
Club contest, Sydney, preferred to be noticed in the press
under assumed names. Pride in bird-murder appears to
be dwindling.
# * * *
The ancient Hebrew gentleman who wrote that
the race is not to the swift was evidently acquainted with
bike racing of the road variety. As in the Goulburn-
Sydney race, so it was in the Warrnambool to Melbourne
contest. Mighty Jack Arnst, who rushes along at the
speed of a locomotive, and the rest of the back-markers
never had a show. W. E. Hawley, of Victoria, who
finished first, was on the 25min. mark, and is the same
rider who, the week previously, won the 25-mile road
championship of Australasia at Campbellfield course
(Melbourne). R. Finlay and E. J. Turner, second and
third men respectively, both had 60 min. start,
and even in a 165-miles run such handicaps tell.
The road was heavy from the effects of a night’s down-
pour of rain, and of the 196 acceptors 42 re-
tired at the last minute. Then 38 dropped out on
the road, including those who met with accidents.
Falls were numerous. Two riders collided, also one ran
down a local cyclist, whilst another rode over a young
lady. The back men, flinging themselves along at top
speed to overtake the field, had the most spills, and the
constant battering of the King’s (or Bent’s) highway took
the steam out of them. It is said that Hawley finished
quite fresh. Well, his actual riding time was only 9min.
26sec. more than Jack Arnst’s record time for the same
race, and if he got so close to the record on a wet road,
with competitors falling around him like ninepins, and
finished “ quite fresh,” then he got ahead of the handi-
capper. #
The Sydney Summer Nights-Amusements syndi-
cate has decided that there shall be a Sydney Thousand
this season, to take place, as usual, in March, 1906. The
first fortnight of November will see five nights’ racing at
Sydney Cricket Ground, which will be illuminated as
usual. Up to date there are no particulars of what foreign
riders are likely to be here, neither has there been any
notification of what particular measures will be used to
keep the bookmaker out of the soup.
* * * *
Cycle racing in Victoria has not been too clean
at the best of times, but, during the last couple of seasons,
the roguery and thieving carried on by a section of riders
and their “ trainers ” would shame a Petticoat Lane Jew.
Saddles, shoes, tyres, wheels, chains and, finally, whole
bicycles disappeared. The sufferers complained to the
League officials. And at last the L.V.W. is makings move
to clean out undesirables. Hitherto, all that so-called
“trainers” had to do, was to present themselves at the
League office with a shilling, and they received in exchange
a trainer’s ticket, no questions being asked. The class of
riders these so-calledtrainers” look after are, for the most
part, as undesirable as the trainers themselves, and the
only “prize money” they ever get is that which they
receive from just as undesirable bookmakers, for interfer-
ing with the favorite and so spoiling the latter’s chance of
winning. How some of these riders and trainers live on
the game is a mystery, for they do not honestly earn
enough money to keep a Chinese skeleton in rice. The
League is now submitting applicants for training tickets to
a searching examination, and only those who are known
to the officials as decent-living men are registered. Several
“professional” cyclists who are known not to be too
straight have also been assured that their doings both on
and off the racing track will be closely watched this
season, and that they must walk the chalk line of un-
swerving rectitude to avoid lifelong disqualification.
* * • •
The tournament for the Victorian amateur
billiard championship produced some very bright play
towards the finish. Jacoby, last year’s winner, failed
quite brilliantly in the game that put him hors de combat.
His two biggest breaks topped 60, and two more were a
shade higher than the best of his opponent, who beat him
by 16 points. But whereas Jacoby is rather an “ in-and-
outer,” the other fellow, Haley, is a first-class plugger,
and in a subsequent game he averaged 9‘6 per innings-
very fast billiards for an amateur on a championship
taffie. *
In refusing “ Tote ” Wren’s nominations for the
next meeting at Flemington, the V.R.C. committee seems
to have acted on very ancient suspicion, without stating
any suspicions whatever. What’s the matter with Wren
nowadays that wasn’t always the matter with him ? Ten
months ago, rumor had it that the victory of Wren’s
Murmur in the Caulfield Cup race was partly achieved
by the same tactics that won an Austral Wheel race for
Wren’s Bill Martin—tactics that subsequently came to
light in a Melbourne law court. Truly or otherwise, it
was stated as within the knowledge of many people that
nine of the jockeys in the Caulfield Cup field had been
“deadened” in the interests of .Murmur. A careful
official inquiry, with a wise expenditure of cash, would
put the “ authorities ” iir sufficiently full possession of the
facts of such yarns. And having got the facts, it would
be their duty to trot them out instead of uttering myste-
rious indirect pronouncements.
• * • •
Tote-man Wren is exercising the V.R.O.
intellect over a lordly sort of scheme. Subject to that
body’s approval and patronage, the Collingwood person
says he will purchase a site for a new course at Eltham,
and guarantees £lOOO for each day’s sport of six events.
As a side inducement he casually mentions an intention
to associate his proposed ground with an annual classic
event carrying a £lO,OOO stake. The V.R.C. will probably
look askance at a proposal which may ultimately relegate
its own classic Gup to a second-class seat; and, anyway,
are there not too many races already, and isn’t that land
at Eltham wanted to settle Booth’s undesirable immi-
grants on ?
“ 0.K.” ; Where is the justice of income-taxing
a racehorse owner —say G. G. Stead—upon the stakes he
wins in any Australian State? If his racing-stud be
regarded as a trading concern he is fairly subject to a tax
on the annual profits, but the money gained at one
particular meeting is not annual income. A merchant
doesn’t pay tax upon his successful deals. His working
expenses and losses in “ job lines ” are set against them ;
the profit (if any) for the whole year counts as his income.
To tax Mr. Stead upon his N.S.W. turf winnings as though
they r presented a clear business profit is a manifest
imposition. A man may lose money by his racehorses
every year (irrespective of betting transactions) whilst
receiving considerable sums in stakes from the clubs he is
dealing with. Suppose he buys a gee for £2OOO, and it
wins £5OO, and then breaks its neck. His loss is £l5OO
and he is taxed on £5OO profit.

The supplementary edition of Miller’s Sporting
Pamphlet brings the list of Cup-horses’ performances up
to September 18, and throws the light of the recent
A. J.O. meeting upon this guide to “form.” A devout
student of “form” can at least arrive at a number of*
rough conclusions re the oldest and hardest worked of
the gees after sitting up all night with the Pamphlet and
the necessary stationery.
* * * *
York, the crack Australian hurdle-racer, was
shipped to England last week, and is likely to win the
next Grand National Steeplechase in the Cold Country.
There’s no patriotic objection to York being exported, for
he could have no bearing on the breed of horses here-
abouts. He will, doubtless, finish up between the shafts
of a cockney hansom.
* * * *
Re the agitation for a really Australian Derby.
Why “ Derby ” ? Most of the important Australian races
are labelled with slavish copies of English popular events,
except the Cups. Why should this be ? Why the blessed
“St. Leger”? Why “Epsom” and “Newmarket”?
Worse still, why the “ Grand Nationals ” ? If the V.R.C.
holds a Grand National jumping race one week, how can
the V.A.T.C. hold another equally “Grand” National
next week ? We have the Melbourne “ Cup.” Why not
call the next most important race a “ Saucer ” or a
“Boomerang”? Don’t let us call it a “ Plate.” “Plate”
is reminiscent of the heirlooms that Jeames, the flunkey,
cleans in the hereditary halls of the Duke of Over-Wallop,
at Wallop, near Bogley-under-Lyne.
* * * *
The mantle of Annette Kellermann—if so brief
a garment can be called a mantle —has fallen upon
Beatrice Kerr, the amateur Vic. girl champion, who made
her first appearance in the public tank at the opening of
the Melbourne Shoot on Saturday evening. Beatrice is
pretty, athletic, and brown, but otherwise bears no
resemblance to her illustrious predecessor. Despite her
muscular arms and legs, she exhibits no signs of the
plumpness which threatened to destroy the contours of
Miss Kellermann. She is a little Venus. Without a scrap
of fragility, she is graceful and dainty and essentially
feminine in all her movements, and the black and silver
snake-like bathing dress she filled without a crease could
not have been coaxed on a prettier little figure with a
shoe-horn. Beatrice is only about 16 or 17, and hails
from Albert Park, where she carried off many champion-
ships and won enough medals to make a bathing costume
—as bathing costumes go.
• • * *
“Uppercut”: Remarks (B. 28/8/’O5) re Wren
and pugilism were obviously written by somebody who
doesn’t know the tote proprietor. To express fear that
Wren's personality may corrupt pugilism is much like
saying that the promoters of Tatts. would corrupt the
Sydney land agents or the Melbourne butter brigands.
Until recently pugilism in Victoria smelt like the
speech of a Tory politician on Australian products.
The management’s aim was to beat the fighters out of as
much as It could, and the fighters in turn took it out
of the public and rigged things shamelessly. Wren
stands to many people as synonymous with Satan, but, in
fact, he is a business man pure and simple. _ He is out for
cash every time, and his whole success is due to ms
appreciation of the fact that honesty, towards those he
has direct dealings with, is the best policy. His Colling-
wood tote was built up by the free advertising it obtained-
about his honesty. Once, when Wren was fined on the
evidence of a disguised policeman who entered the tote
and backed a winner, he sent along the amount of the
winnings to the police station, and. the incident was-
trumpeted from Dan to Beersheba. With Wren running
pugilism, there is every probability of fair-fought battles
—anyhow until the game gets important enough to make
a crook fight worth big money.
• * * •
The Sydney public did not roll up in over-
whelming numbers to see Messrs. Gould and Cullen
damage one another at the Gaiety Club on Saturday
night. Bruising is a game at which a rose does not smell
as profitably by another name, and yet, in the majority
of cases, it is the unknown men, the unsophisticated
beginners, Who put up the best scraps. When they have
been at the game a long while they grow wary, they
learn how to avoid punishment, and they develop
a business instinct which, even in the absence of
any formal understanding, gives the paying public
as much frill and as little gore as possible for
its money. Oullen and Gould were very unso-
phisticated, and for four bitter rounds Mr. Gould raged,
trying to dismember Oullen, Esq.—who kept his chin out
and hit back for all he was worth. Then the ferocity o.
his attacks began to tell on Mr. Gould, and he slackened
off somewhat; but Mr. Oullen grew enthusiastic, and
commenced to make the pace. Mr. Gould rallied occa-
sionally, and tried to respond, but Mr. Cullen whirled
round him, bashing, jabbing and poking till, at the end
of the seventh round, Mr. Gould intimated that he was
“ satisfied.” Anyone who wasn’t more than merely satis-
fied with what Mr. Gould had received must be just a
plain, unvarnished glutton.
