*«- * *
gives his experince of the toto in
Melbourne : A friend who was known introduced me and
I was made a “member.” Somebody took down my
name, address, occupation, and gave me a scrip worth 25.,
purporting to be a share in a company of so many thou-
sand shares. There was a number on the scrip which you
make use of in betting, and your name never need be
mentioned. I went down the room. There was a long,
high counter, and sitting behind this a couple of clerks-
with records of each race and the horses backed
for wins and places. A long platform at the back sup-
ported a huge blackboard, marked off for the different
races. A couple of men were on the platform armed with
pieces of chalk. I went to the counter and backed for a
win and a place. The clerk recorded it, called it out, and
the blackboard boy put a stroke each side of the name,
the left side representing a win and the right side a place.
Half an hour before the race the tote for that race closed.
Before this happened I scanned the room. All sorts were
congregated, high-collared dudes rubbing shoulders with
bottle-oh and barrow men. Some were eagerly looking
at weights, others telling of a “ dead cert. Someone
comes in cautiously and sidles up to the counter and lays
his five wins and five places. Others hear it and think it
must be good, and soon the tally of the horse is stretched
out, which makes prospects of good dividends look small.
The tote is closed, but not before someone has
picked up some friendless horse and put a little
on him to keep him warm. A bet of ■ that
sort sometimes comes off and brings a rich reward. After
some little time a bell is rung, and a bush falls on the
room that would do credit to any prayer-meeting. Slowly
come through the names of the scratched horses. A
groan goes up when some horse with something like 45
wins and 60 places gets crossed out. The next exciting
event is to see how your neddy is regarded on the course,
which is gauged by the betting. Your hopes rise when
the price of your favorite shortens. After two or three
flashes of brisk work in straight-out betting, all is quiet,
and then dead silence until a ring comes, and you hear
the names of the placed horses read out. The dividend is
soon calculated, and there’s a big crush for the cash.


Since the White Star Line placed in the Austra-
lian service five 12,000 ton twin-screw steamers with iden-
tical accommodation for one class of passengers only, the
Australian public have shown their appreciation by taking
whole of the accommodation during the passenger
Beason, which covers departures from Australian ports
between February and May of each year. Family parties
especially favor this line, which has established itself as
“ the holiday line ” for a large portion of the community.
Intending passengers residing in any part of the country
districts are supplied with plans of accommodation, and
all other information, enabling them to book as reliably
as if they were able to visit the steamers while in port.
Messrs. Dalgety and Co., Ltd., are agents throughout
Australia for the White Star Line.*

Brisbane Daily Wail was started origi-
nally to barrack for the Philp Government, but
that reckless combination was ordered to stand
down by a general election before the paper saw
the light. Now the fatherless organ is trying to
form a new party on its own account. The planks
of its platform mainly consist of “ WHY ?” The
Wail is waving its gamp and asking why Queens-
land has not a population of nine millions; why
there are not thousands of new agricultural indus-
tries ; why there are not thousands of factories;
why the country’s mining resources are not yield-
ing hundreds of millions every year ; and uttering
many other whys to the same effect. Presumably
the new party is to bring about all these hundreds
and thousands and loose millions, but, so far, it
seems to consist of one member, Somerset, who
has done nothing in particular save abandon the
Morgan Ministry and join old busted Philp over
the Land Monopoly Tax.
Over 40 sheep-breeders in N.S.W. were
notified by Government order, 90 years ago, to
call at the Government woollen factory at Parra-
matta to receive such quantities of cloth as they
were entitled to on account of wool delivered at
the factory. The Foreign-trade craze has caused
a lot of retrogression in 90 years.
On Lyne’s very sensible Commerce Bill,
which endeavors, so far as State Frights allow,
to force both local and foreign traders to be
passably honest, Joe Cook made about 300
orations. As each clause appeared the Opposi-
tion Talk Machine got on its legs and asked in a
loud tone of pained regret: “Is this a horrid
dream?’’ In one brief spasm the One Man
Pantomime from Parramatta scattered the fol-
lowing gems of mercantile wisdom :
Why add another needless statute to the many by
which Australia is now afflicted ? ... It all goods going
out ol the country should have the truth told regarding
their weight, size, quality and grade, I want to know
whether this is not grading in its very essence... A trade
secret ought to be regarded as sacred If the Bill were
passed in its present form it would place the Minister for
Trade and Customs in the position of a Czar of Commerce.
... In the harvester ease the Minister increased the
price of machines by an arbitrary act.... lam told that
the ordinary Chinese has no knowledge whatever of
weights If we alter the size of the tins in any way—
even by stamping the weight on them —the Eastern races
will not look at them.... The word of a reputable mer-
chant should be accepted.... It is a good principle,
which has worked, well for thousands of years, that when a
man buys an article he takes the risk of making a good
bargain. In this case, however, it is proposed to set up a
standard for him The Bill will strike a blow at the
commerce of Australia.
Already something of the glamor is being
rubbed off the Booth immigration scheme. The
latest news is that the immigrants, or a large pro-
portion of them, are not to “go on the land ” at
once, but are to work for wages for an indefinite
period, till they raise a little money. But until
Australia gets a tariff that will really help along
local industries—until it imports a great deal less
and manufactures a great deal more—there isn’t
enough wages work to keep its own people em-
ployed. Of course, the Bootliites—seeing that
they feed mostly on hallelujah and on the smell of
a hymn—can work for less than current wages,
and can drive out some of the men who are already
employed, which is what Booth’s hallelujah band
is doing wherever the Big Drum curse has pene-
trated. Still, that is not exactly the sort of thing
that was promised. This country wanted an
influx of sturdy, independent settlers—not a rush
of servile cheap labor, vacant about the eye and
retreating about the chin, with its soul in Booth’s
pocket, and the front of its cap worn out through
much touching of it to persons in authority, and
its ear frayed through listening for the commands
of its hook-nosed apostle.
G. 0. Klug, manager of the Boulder Per-
severance mine and the Fremantle smelting works,
has been telling the Tariff Commission in Wes-
tralia that “ when they wanted highest efficiency
they had to import machinery from abroad ” ;
wherefore machinery, fire-bricks, coke, and all
kinds of belting should be admitted free. The
young man who tells the Commission this
is an Australian, trained in Australia, mostly
at the Adelaide School of Mines, and in
the Broken Hill Proprietary Co.’s works.
But as “the highest efficiency ” is always foreign,
what is the Australian Klug doing in his present
billet ? And what are half-a-dozen others of the
most successful managers in Westralia doing in
their billets? Australia now produces as good
managers as any in the world, because it has been
in that business some time, and has schools and
mines and smelting works ; if it isn’t producing as
good machinery as any in the world (in some lines
it is), it is only because the works haven’t been
established. Obviously the brains are here—or
else Klug and the rest shouldn’t be where they
are.

A new way by which woman can earn a
more or less dishonest pound or two has been
shown in England—and it looks like a way that
may be spread to other parts. On the express to
Cornwall the Great Western Railway Co. has in-
stituted the lady’s maid. The male attendant
who roams the corridors, polishing boots and
bringing the cup of tea, and saying “Yessir,”
Australia knows ; why not the maid, who will
attend to the back-hair in the morning? A ship
without a rudder would have about the same
chance of getting women passengers as one with-
out a stewardess; and the marvel is that
some enterprising chick in a cap hasn’t pushed
herself into the corridor car long ago.

What with one thing and another, Australia’s old
enemy, Decline of the Birth-Rate .(it was on him
that the Stinking Fish party cut its teeth, so to
speak) has had to go and hide himself in a cave.
The old humbug got a nasty set-back when it was
pointed out that the real thing was, not the
number of births, but the excess of births over
deaths—it wasn’t much gain to a country to have
a boom in babies if it also bad a boom in corpses.
And the latest figures from that point of
view show Australia not anywhere near the
bottom of the list, but right _ away at
the ti\p, wearing proud feathers in its tail.
Taking the Australian figures for 1904 and
other countries’ figures for 1903, as far as the
Registrar-General of England gives them (and he
reckons that he gives all that are worth having),
little Tasmania stands absolutely ahead of all
others, with an excess of 18'55 per 1000 of average
population—an annual gain of 18'55 per 1000 by
excess of births over deaths. Westralia comes next
with 18 - 43; Maoriland third with 17'37. The Ger-
man Empire shows up pretty well with 15 - 7 ; the
only other European country that beats it is the
Netherlands, 16‘0 per 1000. Yet N.S. Wales
beats both of them, and has a small infant and a
large decimal to spare. The United Kingdom, as
a whole, with 12T, isn’t entitled to wear as much
frill as even Victoria ; and Victoria’s record is by
far Australia’s worst. From the way the original
Stinking Fish party used to talk on this subject,
Australia got the idea that its record was just
about as bad as France’s ; and France, even with
recent improvements, can only score an increase
of I'9 per 1000. It isn t crape, then, that Aus-
tralia needs to bind its births, marriages, and
deaths statistics with ; if they want any binding
at all, the sort of ribbon that they put on the
prize ox at the show seems the correct millinery
for the situation.
+
Willy Kelly, who provides comic relief
in the House of Reps., arose to hotly oppose
the provisions of the Commerce Bill, which
prohibit the exportation of deleterious foodstuffs,
remarking: “Surely, sir, we are only concerned
about the health of our own people.” This just
shows how substantial is the alleged Imperialism
about which the Kelly person is constantly
barking. As long as it only means spending
Australian money on condemned British ironclads,
on which Kelly’s Piccadilly friends may find
billets, he is whooping round with a Union Jack
like a dancing Dervish filled with bhang. Directly
it comes to protecting the rank and file of hard-
working British people from being poisoned by

Another hotel out of the three left in Port
Darwin (N.T.) is about to close up. Can’t make it pay.
Yet, in the main street, there are, say, 75 shops, many of
which sell grog. But these are all run by Chinese.
Practically no restrictions are put upon the Chows, and
the cunning heathen waxes fat at the expense of the white
man. And all the while ignorant Southerners say that
the way to make the white man rich is to let the colored
man in.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“S.” : The Bulletin, discovering the wiles of
wicked house-agents, neglected an impudent trick fre-
quently worked by the spielers in the game. House-
owners have a very bad opinion of the honesty of poor
tenants, based on the belief that these people almost
invariably skip by the dark o’ the moon, owing one or
two weeks’ rent; whereas it often happens that the left
tenant is discredited with the agent’s lt is a
common trick of the business to pouch the last payment,
and represent the late occupant as having neglected to
square up before lighting out. If the owner is a hard,
passionate person, who shows a disposition to hunt for his
money, the agent manifests his zeal in his principal’s
interest, explaining soon afterwards that he captured
the lost tenant at great personal risk and dragged the
amount from him ; but the house-owner who is reluctant
to run down a defaulting tenant generally loses a little
bit on every second occupier in this way. With the other
tenants he has a big chance of falling in for a few shillings
also, through the agent’s bad habit of shifting oil the
date of taking possession a week or so in the account
rendered.
* • * »
“San Remo” is right about the
chemist being, in reality, a conscienceless JBarabbas. Not
so long ago, for a joke, I asked a Melbourne chemist for
an ounce of sodium chloride.. I thought he would
“ notice,” but no—he solemnly put up and labelled about
a teaspoonful of common salt, for which he charged 3d.
Again, the country storekeeper will sell anyone lib. of
Paris green, for spraying’ trees, for 6d. ; the chemist com-
pels you to go to the trouble of getting a witness and
signing his poison-book, and charges you Od. for about an
ounce. Or spirits of salt (muriatic acid). Take your
bottle, and you’ll be lucky if you get more than a dessert-
spoonful for 6d. The ironmonger sells it at 3d. for a good
half-pint. Also there is something suspicious about the
regularity of their charges. For a prescription composed
of common drugs—probably costing less than Cd.—the
universal charge is 2s. 6d. Years ago I got a prescription
made up successively by about a dozen chemists. The
first charged 35., and not one of the others raised or
lowered it by so much as a “ thrum.” Finally, I wearied
of this monotonous 35., so I dashed down half-a-crown and
picked up the bottle. The chemist took the coin and said
nothing.
• • * •
“ Mirumbeck ” : There are few trades in which
tricks play such an important and profitable part as that
of the retail jeweller. Certain retailers arrange with the
manufacturer to turn out certain lines of brooches, rings,
and chains stamped much above the quality that the
articles will assay. When heavy-weight gold wedding-
rings and keepers, reputed to be 18ct. fine, are daily
advertised at less than the cost of a 9ct. article, the wide-
awakes should become suspicious, but nowadays the sale is
to the cheap, and as long as the article is fairly well-
finished and looks all right, customers come. One Cohen-
stein trick is to put a bar and swivel pretty near the
quality stamped, while the body of the chain is almost
base metal in quality. In the country any stamp passes
with most people. A firm has only to stamp letters in a
ring (say a.s.s. or f.a.k.e.) and the average purchaser
thinks it* stands for 15ct., or some high-class mark. Wr.ter
is pestered to the verge of insanity after the visits of a
tribe of colored hawkers, with customers handing in a
bulky type of gent’s signet ring, to know if it is gold. One
look from the expert suffices to convince him that the
thing is worthless, yet the purchasers, who thought they
were getting gold (and so they were —Assyrian gold)
because the article was stamped with one or two undeci-
pherable Asiatic hieroglyphics, will argue that the things
must be gold—as long as my patience lasts.

“ Robert Hap ” : My friend Umberto Peanutto,
had been heavily fined for allowing a chunk of lead to
accumulate under the right side of his counter scales. I
dropped in to condole with him in some measure. I
found him hard at work polishing, with an old sock, the
crimson-coat of the succulent apple. There was a sinister
gleam in his eye, and he seemed disinclined to talk until
I proceeded to denounce the tyranny of the Inspector of
Weights and Measures. Then Umberto exploded, and
covered me with the contempt that he’d bottled up
against the Australian nation for a long time. “ Ah-h-h !
You! Poke it the borax! Gam—play it the football I
You 1 Murder the men of the six hat 1 Put ’em in the
prison on the ’andcuff 1 White Australyah—ah—ah 1
Pee-u-tiful ’arbor 1 Yes, I know. Who make it? You?
You too much it the liar 1 Creesta 1 Garn 1 Shootit the
pleecemen with the load’ revolve’ on the dark night of
the public-house. Me Italiano —no carry the revolve’—
do I?” Now I understand that some people, at any rate,
are capable of a generous appreciation of the lies of the
Foreign-trade press.
• * * *
James Gray, secretary to the S.A. State Chil-
dren’s Council, told the Federal Old Age Pensions Com-
mission that he knew of a workman who, being in urgent
want of £47, borrowed it, agreeing to pay £6O in return
by weekly instalments of 10s. The man has since been
paying £2O per year for eight years, and still owes £6O.
A Usury Act is badly wanted in every State.
“Vulcan”: Michael was a commercial tra-
veller. An out-back compatriot gave him a sucking-pig
with which to appropriately celebrate March 17. To
travel to Svdney the grunter was placed in a sugar-bag
and stowed secretly under the seat of the railway carriage.
This, amongst other things, saved freight. At the first
refreshment stop somebody firmly stood on the prospec-
tive pork. Michael’s subsequent arguments with a polite
but determined station-master were mostly hot words and
pathetic references to “ a little bit o’ ashlip o’ a pig,” but,
notwithstanding, the bundle of grunts was charged
7s. 6d.— freight on live stock at mail rates. The “ shlip ”
was tenderly put in the family backyard. It couldn’t
keep quiet there, so in time the local inspector of nuisances
called. Within the next two days the cost of that sucker
rose to £2 7s. 6d.—the extra two sovereigns being the fine
for keeping “ noxious animals within 40 yards (or some-
thing like that) of a public place.” Michael was exces-
sively rude to the inspector outside afterwards, and this
made the inspector resentful. Within 48 hours he was
again rapping at the family portal. The door was opened
by Michael himself. “Oh 1 you distorted blank ; come
crawlin’ to see if that little shlip o’ a pig’s here yet, have
yer?” The inspector allowed that Michael read his mind
well. “ Well, see now,” said Michael with ferocity, “ may
you stew for all eternity in an extra special sheol if I
didn’t cut the variegated, crimson blank’s throat yester-
day, see.” The inspector lost all interest in pork just
then ; hitherto he’d been aggressive, now he became
polite. “ Will you show me your license, please.” “Me
phwat?” “Your license for killing animals on the pre-
mises,” said the inspector firmly. Michael was still so
stunned a day or two later that he hadn’t a word to say,
when a prosy beak held him up as an awful example of
wilfully lawless citizenship, and raised the total cost of
that sucker to £5 7s. 6d.
• * » »
A settler in the Stratford (Maoriland) district
borrowed £3OO on the security of a farm. In eighteen
months the lender foreclosed, and the farm was sold for
£440 by auction. The debtor asked for a statement and
got it. It was for £44o—the exact amount the farm had
realised. There were legal expenses some £lO, auc-
tioneer’s expenses £l3, a judgment debt and some rates
and taxes that together amounted to close on £4O, and
the rest was swallowed up in all kinds of things, chiefly
interest and interest on interest. These the sold-up one
could not dispute, but there was one entry which looked
as if it had been altered from 12s. 3d. to 15s. 9d., and for
which no voucher was produced. And the settler is con-
vinced that there was 3s. 6d. of abalance legitimately due
to him, and that the item had been altered because the
man who mopped up all the rest couldn’t bear not to
mop up that also. When a man gets the mopping up
habit it is practically incurable, like the habit of not
being born.

“ Nardoo ” : Is there such a thing as justifiable
assassination ? If so, where can I buy enough of it to
annihilate a firm of girdle-builders. I don’t want to bring
you in as an accessory before or after the fact, but if you
don’t know any firm of reliable family assassins that sell
murder in the bottle, I must refuse to take The Bulletin
any longer. From the aforesaid firm I have received
eighteen letters, all bent on encasing—with a battery—
that portion of my anatomy which is kept for food. lam
physically strong, and not looking for any such environ-
ment, but circular No. 18 (enclosed) tempts me. Out of
pure compassion (for my ailment which doesn’t exist), this
firm of complete strangers reduces aforesaid girdle from its
immense real value to a sordid 20s. The death of these
people is essential. I have looked for murderers in the
directory and can’t find any.

TIRED
Tired ! And supper yet to get for the men in the fields, and after supper
washing dishes, cleaning up, and preparing for the morrow, while the men
sit round and rest and smoke. The question as to this tiredness is : How
much of it is due to overdoing, and how much of it is due to womanly
weakness? The tiredness which comes from a hard day’s work is compen-
sated for by sound sleep, which “knits up the raveled sleeve of care.”
But when tiredness is only another name for weakness, sleep often refuses
to bring its comfort, and the new day finds the weary woman still aching
from the labors of yesterday.
Natural weariness is cured naturally by rest. The weariness which
comes from womanly weakness is cured naturally by using the Freeman
and Wallace’s Prescriptions, which make weak women strong and sick
women well.
Patients write in this strain
“At the time I began taking your medicine I was very weak. I
had a tired feeling all the time, could scarcely walk, pains in head and
stomach, and very bad taste in mouth on getting up in the morning, and also had
other troubles. Four different physicians attended me, but only gave me tem-
porary relief. I bad almost given up in despair when one of your little pamphlets
was handed to me. I sat down and read it carefully, and then wrote to you
describing my ailments. You replied, advising a course of treatment with your
medicines. I took each alternately, as advised. Before I had taken two doses I
commenced feeling better. My heart and stomach felt relieved of that burdensome
feeling. By the time I had finished with the two bottles I felt young as a girl of
sixteen. Concluding I will say X had taken three or four kinds of patent medicine
before trying .your remedies, which made me feel worse instead of better. I think
yours is the best medicine on earth for women.”
Such a cure is one of the many accomplished by the useof Dr. Elizabeth
White’s Favorite Prescription, and she is Principal of the Women’s De-
partment of the Freeman and Wallace Institute, and is registered by the
Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, New Zealand and America.
The statements contained in this letter have their counterpart in the state-
ments of numberless other women who have been cured by the same means.
She was weak, had a tired feeling all the time, suffered from serious trouble,
&c. She was attended by four different physicians, and received only
temporary relief. Took three kinds of patent medicine, and felt worse in-
stead of better. Had almost given up in despair when she was led to use
our Favorite Prescription. After using two bottles she felt “Young as a
girl of sixteen.” No wonder women think that our remedies are the best
medicine on earth for women. She has a right to think so ; the right of a
woman who, finding no help in other physicians or medicines turned in
despair to Dr. White and her skilful methods, and found a quick and com-
plete cure. There is no guess-work about such commendations of this
great medicine for women. It is the praise of women who are cured weak
women, tired women, suffering women, restored to perfect health and
youthful vigor. Here is another form of letter: —
“ When I commenced the use of your medicines I was quite run-down in
strength ; was weak and tired most of the time,” write other patients in different
strain. “ After taking six bottles of your wonderful remedies I felt like a different
person, and was able to do my work without knowing what it was to feel tired.
My complexion was also greatly benefited.”
There’s a point in this letter every woman will appreciate. Sickness
which undermines the strength, plays havoc with a woman’s looks. A run-
down, tired woman has no roses in her cheeks. It is reasonable to suppose
that when our remedies restore the health it should also restore the
appearance of health. It does. It rounds out the hollows in the cheeks,
restores lost flesh, and makes the skin clear and wholesome.
The methods of The Freeman & Wallace Medical Institute
are commended to women as safe and reliable for the cure of womanly ills.
Their remedies heal inflammation and ulceration, and cure female weak-
ness. They are the best tonic and nervine for weak, run-down women,
tranquilising the nerves, encouraging the appetite and inducing refreshing
sleep.

By Rail and Semaphore.
THE REFORMATION OF HARDEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PORT Romahapa, with its blue hills
and sleepy harbor, was the picture of
tranquillity. The hills, pyramid-shaped
and covered with bush, dipped sheer into the blue,
gleaming' waters, and great rocky faces, overhung
with flowering manuka, set the passenger on the
deck Of the incoming steamer wondering where
on earth a landing was to be made. But round a
giant ridge of sandhills the town nestled on the
tiny space of level ground, which was always
being increased by the quarrying and blasting of
freestorte for building purposes.
The Port was infested with “pugs.” It was a
dull, monotonous place, and no one lived there
who was not obliged to. It was torn with the
strife between the “Sea Lions ” and the “ Pilgrims
of Peace.” Who and what the Sea Lions were
originally has always been more or less of a
mystery, but at the time I knew them they were
an unruly push of (principally) waterside toilers.
To them the publicans kow-towed, even to the
extent of free beer, and when any of the push was
run in for riotous conduct, counsel was always
engaged and the fine promptly paid.
The Pilgrims of Peace were affluent. All the
best.people belonged to it, and all the tony young
men were members of its physical development
clnB3, which was presided over by an expensive
instructor. The society had originally been
founded by Mr. Harden, of the Goods Depart-
ment, for the specific purpose of putting the Sea
Lions in their proper place. After nearly six
months’ stubborn fighting, this object was about
attained.
The Pilgrim boom reached high-water marie
when Mr. Harden, going out fishing one night,
heard cries for help proceeding from a small house
in a disreputable lane, and, upon investigation,
discovered a stalwart coal-heaver vigorously beat-
ing his recalcitrant wife. Twenty minutes later
they took that son of toil to the hospital. It was a
chivalrous deed, and was mentioned with unctuous
approbation in two churches and three Sabbath
schools the following Sunday, and held up as a
model and a pattern for muscular Christianity to
the rising generation. Christianity was always
aggressive in that quarter of Maoriland.
As a matter of fact, Harden was a truculent
brute, a born fighter, and a bully. When there
were no Sea Lions to quarrel with, he began to
practise his “art” on anyone.who dared to differ
from him on any subject, and when he took to
fighting die other members of the staff, the Traffic
Office assumed an interest in him.
* * * *
Manager Mackenzie had been spending a week
in the country, and had returned chockful of
energy. He smiled blandly as he stood on the
hearth-rug and shook hands with Fluter,
“How have things been going—all right, I
hope ?”
“Yes, but for one diing. There has been a
most unaccountable outbreak of assault and bat-
tery all over the section. Harden, of the Goods
at Port Romahapa, is the Worst. He broke up
Grant, the sub-S.M. ; so I shifted Grant and sent
down Wilkie. He had a row with Wilkie two
hours after the latter’s arrival, and meeting him
off duty, gave him the father of a hiding. Sol
relieved Wilkie and sent down Leslie, and he’s
made a worse mess of him than he did of Wilkie.
As Harden’s uncle is ex-Premier and may be the
head of the Government again any day, I made
the brute take his annual leave, and held the case
over for your decision. ”
Manager Mackenzie swore energetically, and
then remarked that political control was the worst
of failures.
“ Were all these atrocities committed off duty?”
“All except Leslie’s—that took place in the
goods shed while they were both on duty, and
Leslie demands Harden’s dismissal in accordance
with the rule.”
“ What does the rule say?”
“ If upon inquiry it is found to be proved ”
‘ That settles it; we won’t make any inquiry.
Has Leslie anyone behind him ? ”
“No, thank heaven, he’s on his own. Seems
to be a bit of a lubber; writes poetry and can’t
telegraph worth a cent. ”
“ H’ m '■ Writes poetry, does he ! That means
hell be hobnobbing with newspaper men and
letting things out. Well, just shift him, and first
chance we get bump him out of the service alto-
gether—-you are sure he’s got no one behind him ?”
“ Quite ! I’ll make a note of it.”
Then Manager Mackenzie went off to his club
feeling that he had done a good morning’s work.
* * * *
Three weeks later Jones, commonly known as
the Labor Saver, who was relieving at far-away
Popatunoa, received a pitiful letter from Leslie
asking the loan of a few pounds. His sister, who
had been suffering from consumption of the throat
for years, was dead; his mother’s health had given
way under the shock; and to crown all, he himself
was under notice of dismissal for some telegraphic
blunder which had cost the Department a few
pounds in compensation. The “bumping out”
had eventuated. Leslie dated all his misfortunes
from the day that Harden had battered him in
Port Romahapa goods shed ; and he asked Jones
to use any influence he had on his behalf on
account of the old lady.
The outburst of language Jones perpetrated on
that occasion was a topic of admiring reminiscence
on the part of Jimmy the Rake for years after-
wards. Jones wired twice the sum requested and
sat down and indited a letter full of vengeance to
Harden at the Port. Harden was promised faith-
fully that immediately Mr. Jones was free he
would call in at the Port and “snap his cowardly
neck like a blanky carrot.”
When that letter, all wrath and sudden death,
arrived at the Port, Harden was absent on his en-
forced leave and blissfully unconscious of what
was going to happen to him when he met Jones.
Having relieved his feelings in this manner,
Jones set to work to try and save his friend’s
billet. He was on terms of social intimacy with
the Old Man, and he wrote him a brief note, in-
forming him that the trout in the Kuriwao stream
were biting beautifully. He also spread himself
on the personal beauty of the rather notorious
wife of squatter Matheson, and mentioned that if
the Old Man came up on a Saturday they could
put in the Sunday at Matheson’s.
The Old Man arrived by the Express midday
■Saturday; and Jones having left the junior in
charge, they proceeded to Squatter Matheson’s in
a neat turn-out Jones had procured for the occa-
sion. On arrival at the homestead, however, they
found that the lady was indisposed—confined to
her room, in fact —and that Matheson had gone to
Willowford to bring out a trained nurse and the
doctor. They had a look round the place, and
then, after a snack, went back to Popatunoa, and
found the usual rainstorm in full blast.
As Manager Mackenzie didn’t care about fishing
in the wet, they spent the evening in the parlor
of the hotel where Jones had his headquarters.
The Old Man talked shop persistently. Jones
discoursed fluently for two hours on how to im-
prove the station at “merely nominal cost,” and
then ordered champagne. The Old Man’s eyes
sparkled, and he rubbed his hands together glee-
fully at the word. Jones saw the opportune time
had arrived, and he told Leslie’s story in a voice
that would have done credit to an elocutionist.
•* * # •
The Old Man shook himself.
“I’m sorry I didn’t know all this before I
recommended his dismissal. However, I’ll make
another report and a certain recommendation ;
perhaps, for once, Caesar may be merciful. I
don’t know what to do with Harden. His crowd
are a nasty lot to deal with. I suppose you
couldn’t suggest any method of curing him ? ”
“Yes,” replied Jones, expectorating with great
accuracy on to the fire-dog. “ Get some hoodlum
who has no idea of fair lighting to give him a h—l
of a doing. Some rough, who’ll hit him when he’s
down, and tear his clothes and dance on him.
Why, I remember”—
“Here’s the supper—by Jove, that’s the very
idea. As soon as you’re through here I’ll send you
down to the Port,” said the Manager, interrupting
J ones before he got properly warmed and reminis-
cent.
For a moment the Labor Saver gaped at him,
and then he laughed. But Manager Mackenzie
filled his glass, and pushed the lettuce and a
breast of cold fowl towards him, without a smile
on his face.
* * * *
Harden’s holidays were running out, and Jones
had only two more days to spend at Popatunoa.
So Harden packed his gripsack and started to
drift down from the Lakes towards home and
work. He left Windy Plains by the early train
to catch the express for Romahapa, but the wind
stuck up the train, and he arrived too late for the
Express, and had to get permission to travel by
the Northern Stock special as far as Ballantyre,
The same day, Jones, having handed over Popa-
tunoa, decided to have a little excitement in the
shape of poaching trout in Aparima River. He
had boasted of his intention to have a day’s
poaching just to see what it was like, and Ranger
Burtqtl Was on the look-out. Jones went down to
Aparima by No. 1, blissfully unconscious of the fact
that Burton had driven down the night before,
When the train steamed into the station Ranger
Burton was lying in the rushes about 150 yards
above the railway bridge, waiting for Jones to
come along the track with a gaffing hook under
his arm, probably wrapped up in brown paper.
When an hour had passed without Jones putting
in an appearance, Burton grew suspicious. He
came out of his hiding place, and as he wandered
down to the hotel for a hot breakfast, he imagined
himself in J ones s place, and after long con-
sideration decided that he would have dropped
off the train two miles up, and made for the river
above Chinamen’s Bend with a view to working
up the stream.
This is exactly what Jones had done, only he
was working down the stream. Burton made a
hasty breakfast, and took horse and galloped to
Mulligan s farm, five miles above Chinaman’s
Bend, and worked down the stream, with a view
to meeting Jones as he worked up. So for half a
day he tramped down the rocky bed of the
Aparima, wondering wherever Jones could be
wasting time. Then, when the sun began to
slope downwards, it suddenly dawned on the
ranger that the poacher was working down stream
again, so as to strike the railway line. He
borrowed a horse, and galloped at full speed along
the road to where he reckoned Jones might be.
Leaving his horse with a Chinese gold-miner, he
started down the rocky bed across the great
shingle bars on foot. When he came to a face of
jutting rock, he swept the river bed with his
glass. The golden sunlight gleamed on the bright
gaffing-hook in the hand of a tall, burly man in a
knickerbocker suit, who was bending over a rocky
ledge half-a-mile away, and as Burton got the
focus he knew the figure for that of Jones. The
gaffing-hook flashed, and the tall man sprang up
with a fine four-pounder on the point. Burton
cursed with a mighty joy as he saw the trout
added to a bundle which the tall man took off a
ledge of rock close by.
Next moment Burton was fairly leaping over
the shingle. The penalty for a first offence was
£2O, and the ranger got half the fine, which Jones,
being a man of means, would surely pay rather
than go to gaol. Burton concealed himself as
much as possible as he ran. From another vant-
age point he took a second glance at his quarry
through the glass. Then he cursed in another
key. The tall man had caught up his bundle of
trout and was bounding over the rocks like a wild
goat.
Ranger Burton knew the hardest chase of his
life had commenced, for to secure a conviction he
must secure the trout and the poacher, and he
didn’t feel quite sure what he would do with
Jones when he caught him. A fairy tale about
what happened to the man who caught the tiger
occurred to him as he went stumbling over the
shingle, for further concealment was useless.
From up the reach the Chinaman, standing by his
cradle, looked on with a smile of Asiatic child-
ishness, till the two figures vanished in the haze.
The Stock Special was toiling up Banmuir
Grade, and Harden had paid his footing in liquor
to such purpose that when a second lot was
obtained at Aparima he realised he had overdone
it. The brakesman was paralytic, curled up
among a heap of dead rabbits consigned to the
freezing works at Ballantyre. Old Jack, the
driver, was very groggy, and they were progres-
sing up the grade by a series of jerks which
threatened to snap the draw-bars every moment.
Guard Merton was hilarious, and told of the
mighty drunks of the olden days. Then he
became serious.
“Look here, Mr. Harden, I want you to stand
to me—that d d brakesman’s dead to the
adjective world. I’m goin’ on to the engine. As
soon as we get over the knot of the hill old Jack’ll
whistle for brakes. Just crowd it on for all you’re
worth, and stick to it tight. I’ll come back over'
the trucks, putting down the brakes as I come,
or we’ll have a bolt down the other side, and
perhaps a smash, and I’ve got a small wife and a
large family at home. Pity we’re all drunk.”
Harden grew anxious. Merton was not fit to
go climbing over the trucks filled with stock. It
wants a cool head and a strong hand for that—and
Merton couldn’t walk straight. A cold sweat
broke out over Harden, and he was just going to
propose that they get old Jack to stop at the top,
but he heard the end door of the van banged too,
and it was too late. Merton had gone.
He walked to the recess and saw that the
ratchet of the hand-brake was working freely.
What was to be would be, he thought, and looked
out of the side window. The land was flooded in
moonlight, they were half-way up the grade, and
down below, the Aparima wound away like a
ribbon of silver.
The door at the far end of the van was suddenly
opened, and Harden’s heart gave a leap of joy.
He would be able to get Merton to stop at the
top how slowly the train was going ! Good
heavens!
It was not Merton. It was a tall, burly man in
a kmckerbocker suit, carrying about a dozen trout
on a piece of flax. His face was flushed, his
clothes were covered with mud, and the water ran
out of his clothes and squirted from his boots as he
strode down the van.
C H ;^ den bad read in the morning paper a big
headline about an escaped lunatic, but he had not
troubled about the details—and here the lunatic
He would be firm. He stepped out boldly :
Well, where are you off to, my man ?”
The man in knickerbockers wiped the water out
of his eyes and looked at him.
“Bully Harden—hoo-blanky-ray!” he whooped.
“ Yes, Mr. Harden—that’s my name.”
Well, you’re the bloke whose neck I promised
to snap like a carrot, aren’t you ? ”
"I no ’ m y fl ood man > you are mistaken ! ”
Oh, yes, you are—you’re the Pilgrim of Peace
cur who walloped little Leslie. Take that, you
damned ruffian ! ” and he bashed Harden across
the face with the bundle of trout.
It was a mighty blow, and it knocked Harden
back into the window recess. Jones rushed in to
get to close quarters, but Harden stuck out his leg
and it caught the Labor Saver under the breast-
bone add sent him to the floor.
Quick as Harden was, Jones was quicker. He
rolled over and sprang to his feet, and dealt
Harden a right-hander on the ribs as he did so.
Harden had never seen Jones to his knowledge,
while the Relieving Officer had seen him frequently
as he passed the Port in his journeys to the north.
Harden’s ignorance of the personality of his
assailant made him desperate, and had he had fire-
arms Jones’s brilliant career had ended suddenly
that night.
But he had nothing, and the muddy apparition
came at him with 12 fish and a savage resolution
that really seared Harden. He yelled loudly for
help but the rumble and rattling of the train and
voice 6 P^ antine snorts the engine drowned his
There was not much time to yell. He was
bashed and banged unmercifully. He had been
knocked down on a box of eggs which had burst
all over him. Iwice he had been sent bump on
to the drunken fireman, who cursed and rolled
over But Jones had overdone it. He had just
finished an exciting four miles cross-country con-
test with the ranger, and his strength was giving
out, and Harden was fresh. He defended himself
with skill, and Jones knew that he must knock
him out in the next few minutes. Harden soon
discovered that his weird-looking assailant was
weakening and commenced to make counter
attacks. He got to holts and tried to drag the
.Labor Saver to the door to heave him out, but
that was hopeless. The Labor Saver was like a
wild beast.
.Be gripped Harden by the collar and pounded
him savagely, and then with a mighty rip tore off
his silk vest and shirt.
Harden retaliated by tearing the front out of
Jones s raiment. Then they rolled over and over
tearing and ripping all they could. When they
staggered to their feet they were both hampered
j u j rags t^iat clun & round them. Harden
dashed the door open, and tried to heave Jones
through the aperture. Jones closed with him and
fell on him. This was about the eleventh round,
and it left them with practically nothing on but
their boots. The rules weren’t Queensberry—
rather a mixture of Chinaman and bulldog.
The drunken brakesman wakened up, and
stared in amazement at the sight. Then he shut
his eyes to see if he could still discern it. Sud-
denly, the train seemed to stop momentarily and
to make a mighty leap backwards. Jones and
Harden clung to one another in a frenzy of terror.
“ The couplings have parted ! ” “ We’ve broken
away 1 ” they shrieked, simultaneously, and each
hung on to the other to keep him from the brake,
the application of which meant instant destruc-
tion, as the van was in front and the avalanche of
trucks behind.
* * * *
When Driver Jack realised his train had broken
away, he let go both his whistles for all he was
worth, and the screeching attracted attention
where it was most Wanted. The S.M. hurried
across to the platform and saw that everything
was clear, and the surfacemen’s wives above the
bridge rushed out and took the babies from
between the rails. Then the runaway trucks
went roaring past, over the bridge and away down
into the hollow where the station was, and came
to a standstill on the level at the top of the incline
beyond.
Jack came back cautiously with his engine and
solitary truck. Merton was still thanking his
gods that Harden hadn’t put on the brake, and
the S.M. and the surfacemen jumped on the foot-
plate, and in two seconds they were in the van.
There they found one tall battered man with no-
thing On but a boot, and with twelve desperately
trodden-on trout in his hand. Mechanically Jones
had gathered up his fish like a true sportsman
when he saw death in the near distance. There
was also a tall battered man dressed in two boots
and a wild expression, but with no fish.
The latter was the reformed Harden.
He never assaulted anybody afterwards.
O. A. JEFFRLES.

Upon the Hills.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE is a nobler, purer air
Upon the hills ;
An atmosphere—a breath so rare
My being thrills
With the delights of living !
There is no rancor and no strife —
No malice here;
One borders on the better life,
Where strong wills steer
Past doubting and misgiving.
The noble gums sway down their heads—
To me they murmur gravely ;
The spiders spin their fairy threads,
And loop their grass-stalks bravely ;
And I —l think what I should think —
Of purest patriotism ;
Australia’s own warm breath I drink —
Afar from sham and schism !
The rivers wind them back and forth,
And breezes blow
Out of the balmy, tree-topped north,
And then I know
How grand a country mine is.
The essence of the Bush instils
A hope that I
May sleep for aye upon these hills
When last I die,
And have my humble finis.
Australia’s heart is beating here—
O gracious Land of Glory ;
Her mighty soul is pulsing clear
Upon this promontory.
Here at his ease a man might sleep
Within her bosom vernal—
And hear her life-blood throbbing deep !
And take his rest eternal.
O land of mine, I do aspire,
Each living day,
To catch your cadences of fire
In some swift way,
And be your chiefest singer.
You need an arch-interpreter—
Born of the soil—
To carry your sweet voice of myrrh
To those who toil,
Yet you your message linger.
There is a stirring in the heart _
Of those born of your passion ;
O that I had the minstrel art
To stir them in some fashion !
I’d waken all the dormant love
Of country hidden in them ;
Gum-boughs that sing and sway above—
Give me the power to win them !
Upon the hills I sing a song
That some may hear
In some far city’s distant throng
Or other where,
And set their true hearts beating
Eor her, our Mother of the Bush,
Serene and grand—
The goddess of the great hills’ hush
Our own dear land,
Who sends her children greeting !
Upon the hills I sing a song—
Straight from the heart it gushes,
Like some vast river swift and strong
From its deep source it rushes.
I sing the song of Liberty
The song Australia tells me
To send from these far hills to ye—
As loyalty impels nie !
vio.
GRANT HERVEY.
The Coward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AS Evans unfastened the gate that even-
ing on his return from the colliery, his
mood was very bitter. He was a small,
timid man, and keenly aware of bis constitutional
failing. And now, in spite of his caution, what
he had dreaded for so long was, perforce, thrust
upon him. And the very worst man in the
colliery, big Charley Ryan-ah, well he knew
what would be the result of the fight to-morrow
He knocked viciously at the door, and Mrs.
Evans, divining in one quick glance Ins ill-humor,
let him in silently. Contrary to custom, he did
not enjoy his tea; the thought of the coming
ordeal had taken full possession of his weak, little
mind. And when his wife left him later in the
evening to visit her people, he sat down to nurse
his terror. Which, of course, made it worse. His
memory would not be stilled on the subject of the
Ryan-Briggs fight, six weeks ago Gruesome
details obtruded themselves, principally of Briggs
bleeding face and broken nose. And the funny
thing to Evans’ timorous spirit was that Briggs,
after his defeat, could laugh and shake hands
with the man who had disfigured him. Big
brutes like this were evidently insensible to pain.
But he was not built that way, and he wondered
shrinkingly how it would feel. Thirteen stone of
baresark Irishman scientifically concentrated into
one darting fist. Plainly a thing to be feared.
He knew himself for a coward, and the knowledge
made him bitter against the normal man, who lias
naught but contempt for this failing. He might
sham sick to-morrow—but it would only put on
the evil day. And his thoughts turned to Death,
as to a protecting friend. How he wished he
could go to sleep and never wake. The ticking of
the clock irritated him with its ceaseless suggestion
of the inevitable to-morrow. Tick—tick—tick-
damn the thing ! He rose, and m his fit of nervous
temper drove his fist through the clock face, and
swept it, a whirring protest of released machinery,
to the floor. The gfass cut his hand, but he was
past fear now ; at another time the sight of his
own blood would have made him sick. And
suddenly his eye rested upon a . means of escape.
It lay on the mantel loaded in six chambers,. He
nicked it up, and peered, fascinated, into the
cylinders, whence tie ugly little bullets peeped
hie adders One pull, and Ryan’s brutal strength
cS> totrouble him, And there is little
doubt, but that this fantastic coward would have
fiddled about with the venomous little weapon,
until induced by sheer terror, he had shot him-
self, but for the saving interruption of his wifes
return. He had barely time to replace the pistol
on the shelf when she entered, She saw the
broken clock, and her eyes questioned him. I
wTs looking for-matches,” he said foolishly. Her
subsea uent language restored his sanity. The
wUd romance of suicide faded under her wither-
ing commonplaces about a clock worth at most
ssf6d But Re shivered as he realized how near
lt Ncx tnior nin g he went forth feeling like a con-
demned^Zn wL sees the grey dawn of his last
day on earth. And behold, upon the Stockton
An^Ryan^catclbng^^h^oGthe Sinking Evans,'
malm loike you. ’Twas this way—the bhoysi laid
foive t’wan ye’d not turrn up to-day, ye d be so
sheared. An’ ye’ve won me two-poun -ten be yer
pluck. B’ gob, Oi’ll shtand yez as much dhnnk
as ye can howld.” , _ ,
The revulsion of feeling dazed Evans tor a
moment. And then rage, the rage of the sheep,
seized him. The unspeakable agony of the pre-
vious night, the numb despair of the morning,
could never be forgotten. And this grinning fool
had made two pounds ten out of it. He hit out
as he never dreamed he could hit. Ryan did not
expect it, and took it on the angle of the jaw. He
dropped, and Evans, hardly realising the knock-
out, stood over him with spasmodically-clenched
fists, murder glaring from his eye. At length the
Irishman showed signs of life. He looked owlistily
at the fists of his conqueror and grinned weakly.
“ F’r a little wan,” he declared “ yer a wonder.
No ! Oi’ll not kill yez—’twas me own fault, an Oi
bear no malice. Let me up an’ shake hands. Yer
the furrust mahn that ever knocked out Charles
Ryan. An’ Oi think ye’ll be the last.” And so
the episode closed in the bar of a small pub. at
Stockton, whence, a little elevated by beer and
pride, Evans departed arm-in-arm with Ryan a
quarter of an hour later for his day’s work in the
darkness of the pit. . .
“ I fought big Ryan to-day,” said Evans to his
wife that evening—“knocked him clean out.”
“Serve the big brute right,” replied Mrs.
Evans indignantly, “to fight a man o’ your size.
It only shows what pluck will do.”
Evan hesitated a while. “Ryan’s not a bad
sort,” he at length admitted magnanimously;
“he bears no malice whatever.”
HOWARD ASHTSON.
In a Restaurant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was five minutes past one and they
were in the very thick of the dinner rush.
Everywhere there was the hum of talk,
scraping of chairs, and rattle of knives and plates.
The proprietor’s wife—a big, red-haired, angry-
looking woman—stood in the pay-box next the
kitchen partition, taking the cash and overlooking
the work of the waitresses. Every now and then
she would slide open a window in the partition
and screech through an urgent order, or shriek
wildly about somebody’s steak or tripe.
As I waited, an anxious-looking girl fluttered
from the far corner of the room to the pay-desk
and muttered something in the red-haired lady’s
ear. The red-haired one whirled round to the
open window and bowed her head.
“Roast beef!” she screamed. “Roast beef,
you wretch —and be quick about it ! ’
Then she turned back to the desk, took two
shillings, filed the checks, and felt her back hair.
There was a sudden sound of thumping.
“ What is it ? ” she snapped, wheeling round to
the window. .
I half rose, and caught sight of a curious white
face, with black whiskers growing on it, talking
rapidly back from inside the window.
“Oh, shut up!” , . , ,
“Shut up, yourself! shouted the face, and
vanished. . , , ,
That was all I heard ; the noise had drowned
Somebody touched me on the shoulder. I turned
and found the next diner grinning at me.
“’Er ’usband,” he whispered. “ They re avin
a row. Been at it f’r the last ’arf-hour.”
I thanked him and looked round for a glass.
“ Tripe an’ onions,” wailed a girl.
The red-haired one turned to the window.
“ Tripe,” she shouted, “ tripe an’ onions.”
There was a faint cry within, as if someone had
been stabbed.
“ Tripe you fool, an’ onions 1
“ Steak two! ” howled the lady.
“ What ?” from the kitchen.
“Steak, you ass !”
In a moment the face with its whiskers was back
at the window and talking—talking its hardest,
to judge from the way its jaws moved.
“Well, shut up, then, an’ send it out!’
“ Don’t you dare to speak ! ”
“Roast beef one,” chanted a shrill voice.
“ Plum pudding two; custard two ! ”
“Go t’ blazes !” shouted the face, and disap-
peared again.
“Come here!” screamed his wife after him.
“Do you hear, you wretch, come.” passing
waitress yelped something ,at her.) “Oyster
soup two —an’ get it at once! ”
In response there was an angry yell trom
within, then shouts and sounds of scuffling.
The red-haired one stopped dead in the act of
taking a shilling, and breathed hard through her
nose. Waitresses stood motionless in the pass-
ages, and the diners, cowed into silence by the
shadow of impending tragedy, gazed at each other
and waited. The place that, a moment before,
had resembled a hungry zoo, was, in a flash, as
silent as an egg. ,
Then there was a sudden gasp of rage, and,
with a swish of silk, the proprietor’s wife swept
from the pay-box and, sobbing hysterically,
vanished round the corner.
Eour seconds of horrible silence, then the mas-
sacre began. . ,
Two screams and a howl —frenzied tramping ot
feet—more screams —thuds —slow, heavy thuds
crashes —more thuds and screams.
At the sound, the petrified waitresses came to
life again, and, dropping dishes, plates and knives,
answered with screams and cries for help, btrong,
brave men shouted and stood on chairs to get a
better view. . .
Suddenly from behind the partition an old man
staggered, and fell in the midst of the seething
mob. There was gravy on his grey hair, and
lumps of fat were clinging to his coat. _
He was dragged to his feet and questioned. _
“ She’s hitting him,” he panted ; “ she’s hitting
him with the corned beef ! ”
Instantly there was a chorus of screams that al-
most drowned the uproar within, and a kindly
waitress, crying miserably, picked the scraps of
fat off the old man and dropped them into my
glass.
For about another minute the racket proceeded.
Then something came flying through the air and
landed with a thump near me. Immediately the
ghastly thudding and howling in the kitchen
stopped, and only a subdued sound of sobbing
broke the silence that had descended like a bene-
diction upon the scene.
The old kitchenman, with a glad cry, caught up
the heavy object that had fallen near me—it was
the coined beef—and hurried behind the partition.
Out came the vengeful lady of the red hair,
hysterical and spattered with gravy, but still alive
and ready for duty. .
“ That brute !” she sobbed, “ he tried to strike
me —actually tried to strike me!” Without ex-
plaining whether he had succeeded or had perished
in the attempt, she struck her bell, and in tones of
deep emotion ordered the resumption of business.
Round the corner appeared the head and
shoulders of the old kitchenman. “ Corned beef s
orwvgain!”heyelled.

Mrs. Mabel Boulton, of Turbot
